CONTENTS

=== PAGE 8 ===
We Call It Home
Until it’s been categorized by the Unity 
Bureaucracy, the correct designation is X-835 
Prime but, since we’re here and we have to  
call it something, we’re calling it ‘Home’.  
It’s aspirational, at least.
It doesn’t always feel like home. That wildlife 
is aggressive – large, dangerous animals 
like you’ve never seen. You remember those 
instructional videos when you join the colonist 
programs? Yeah, nothing like that. There’s 
razor-fanged rats the size of dogs that teleport 
up on you and there’s lizards – or something 
like lizards, but too spindly – that make you 
nervous, but only when they see you and freeze. 
The scientists reckon they’re psionic, but not 
intelligent, but you could have fooled me. At 
least they keep our security troopers busy (or 
just trigger-happy?) It keeps them getting into 
other trouble of one kind or another.
The landscape is quite something. There’s a 
mountain range out west that we’re going 
to scout soon – scans are showing exotic 
minerals. It’s oddly symmetrical, though, and 
late at night the sun plays on something in the 
atmosphere and the cliffsides radiate purple 
light. You could call it romantic, but it does 
make people look a little strange. And those 
lizards? Near-invisible in it.
Test Media Report
UnityComms Archive 37-D-Kensington
“As far as you can see, beautiful vistas which 
these hard-working colonists will be turning 
into their very own corner of our incredible 
Unity family! Imagine the fruits of your hard 
work, as you build your new home in one of 
the worlds of the Promise! These worlds are not 
just new colonies but, thanks to the abundance 
of ruins and ancient sites, provide countless 
opportunities for funding from Unity Research 
grants. This isn’t merely build-to-survive, you’ll 
be able to fund your colony from R&D and, 
thanks to the Unity Guarantee, your own share 
in any found technologies! Your children 
will grow up knowing their world will deliver 
countless benefits for the Unity family.”
Introduction

=== PAGE 9 ===
7
Sorry about the background noise. Supply day. 
That’s a Unity Auto Shuttles coming down. As 
you know, the system mostly runs on autopilot: 
We just load anything we want to meet our 
quotas and every few weeks another one arrives 
with supplies. We’re not quite self-sufficient yet, 
though Admin says it’s only a matter of months. 
Soil here’s the most fertile I’ve ever seen, even 
in places you wouldn’t believe. The ground is 
covered in a thin layer that looks like sand, but it 
contains a ton of nutrients for plant growth. We 
are getting growth rates of about 115% of Unity-
Standard rates and that’s on an average day.
What’s strange is the stuff seems to replenish 
itself. We don’t know how. The top layer seems 
to regenerate after a few days even if you 
shovel it away. You can build on it, but we 
max the filters keeping the dust out. It’s not so 
dangerous, but unpleasant to breathe in, so 
enviro-suits or filter mask. You get used to that, 
but the combination of the suit and that purple 
light throws your aim off. Hopefully nobody’s 
relying on my pistol skills.
I hope you see this place someday. The 
assignment procedure’s tough but get your 
grades and maybe you can be a scout too. If you 
finish in the top 5%, you get to pick your first 
deployment. 
Salut to the folks. I hope they’re finally proud 
of me. I named a rock after the old farm on 
Rupprecht III. 
Introduction

=== PAGE 10 ===
An Introduction 
for NEW SETTLERS
Welcome to the Unity Colonization Program!
Congratulations on passing your Unity Ops 
FDC2 course – you are now promoted to 
Colony Administrator. For your first assignment 
we have a priority colony expedition heading 
to one of the worlds in the Kensington Reach. 
Please see the mission dossier below for 
full details, and make it quick – the ship is 
leaving in 3 hours!
In Planetfall, you lead a crew tasked with 
helping a colony grow and survive on a 
dangerous and exciting new world. This is a 
prime colonization target, not just because 
of the rich resources, but because of the 
wreckage and ruins littered across them.  
There are unknown dangers, ancient 
mysteries, dangerous new lifeforms, but huge 
opportunities to discover the secrets of your 
world and forge a future for your settlers!
You will need to be creative, decisive, and 
occasionally lucky, but the journey ahead of you 
will be filled with amazing moments!
Adventure Wargaming
Planetfall is part of a new breed of miniatures 
games we call Adventure Wargames, 
intended to be played solo, co-operatively, 
or with a Gamemaster. These are campaign 
games that create exciting stories as you play 
with randomly generated content to make 
each play session unpredictable. As your game 
develops in a procedural way, the story evolves 
with the results. 
The rules are written to be played “out of the 
box”, meaning this book tells you everything 
you need to know to set up and play the game.
You may find that the combat rules are a little 
simpler than in many other science fiction 
miniatures games. This is intentional as the 
game is typically played by a single player who 
must manage every aspect of the game.
If you want to play cooperatively, simply split 
the characters between you for each mission. 
You may also decide to split the campaign 
responsibilities so, for example, one player 
manages construction of the colony while the 
other handles research. If you prefer playing 
with a Game Master, this is discussed in the 
Appendices (see page 185).
New around Here?
As many players will be coming here from 
other Five Parsecs from Home games, this brief 
introduction leads quickly into creating your 
colony crew (page 14) and combat rules 
(page 28). However, if this is your first time 
playing a Five Parsecs game, we’ve included 
plenty of help in the appendices.
An overview of the Unified Space setting can be 
found on page 180.
If you need to know more about how Adventure 
Wargames differ from role-playing games or 
wargames, see page 176.
If you are completely new to tabletop gaming, 
and need to know more about dice and 
miniatures, go to page 174 of the Appendices 
before diving in with the main rules. (Even if you 
are used to the tabletop gaming conventions, 
but not 5X gaming, it’s worth checking that same 
appendix for how we randomize directions, 
calculate measurements and miniature 
movement, and how to establish Line of Sight 
and Cover in this specific game.)
Introduction
8

=== PAGE 11 ===
Unified Space, Unified Gaming
Planetfall builds on the Five Parsecs from 
Home series of games, including Five Parsecs 
from Home: Bug Hunt and Five Parsecs from 
Home: Tactics. While there are small changes to 
reflect particular environments and characters, 
the core game rules are all compatible, and 
once you know how to play one game, you can 
easily pick up another in the series. 
The games are all set within a setting known 
as Unified Space, described in each rulebook 
(see page 180). You are welcome to modify 
the setting to your own needs or to substitute a 
game world of your own. 
You can move characters between Five Parsecs 
games, allowing you to build the ultimate 
space adventure. You may carry over a favorite 
character from Five Parsecs from Home into 
Planetfall, only to decide that your story here 
should end with a huge war transitioning into 
a Five Parsecs from Home: Tactics campaign, 
and so on. As you play multiple games, you will 
have a vibrant, living universe where connected 
events can influence each other.  
Feedback
If you want to contact the author with 
questions, suggestions, or ideas, you are 
welcome to do so at nordicweaselgames@
icloud.com 
You can keep up with thoughts, musings, 
and game ideas at https://nordicweasel.
posthaven.com/ 
You can check out other exciting Modiphius 
games online at www.modiphius.net.

Introduction

=== PAGE 12 ===
Gameplay Overview
The Campaign Turn
Your Planetfall campaign will stretch over 
multiple campaign turns. 
Each turn, you will generate random 
events and make decisions, then resolve 
the consequences of those decisions and 
events, such as your characters progressing in 
experience and gaining new equipment (or 
getting injured and killed, if you are less lucky).
Each campaign turn features a tactical game, a 
tabletop battle played using miniatures, where 
your colonists explore the world around them 
and interact with dangerous lifeforms!
The campaign has a grand goal at the end of 
it: As you build your colony and explore, you 
will develop Milestones, representing major 
achievements – scientific, social, and cultural – 
in the progress of your colony. 
When sufficient Milestones have been reached, 
you will progress to the end game, where you 
will have important decisions to make about 
the future of the colony and perhaps even life 
on this world!
The Tactical Game
Your crew will regularly face dangerous 
enemies that threaten the safety and future 
of the colony. This tactical game is where 
your characters take to the battlefield, face 
down the enemy, and hopefully prevail while 
trying to carry out their mission goals. This 
plays similar to other miniatures games, with 
measurements, weapon 
ranges, and rolling to hit.
As these battles are 
both the centerpiece of 
campaign turns and the 
wargame action most 
players are familiar with, 
this rulebook covers those 
rules first.
To work through character creation for your 
colony crew, turn to page 14.
 
◆For the Combat Rules, turn to page 28.
 
◆For your newly arrived colonists’ first 
missions, play out the Planetfall section, 
page 44.
 
◆To generate new scenarios for your colonists, 
you’ll need the Missions section, page 110.
 
◆For all missions, set up your game table first 
using the Battlefield Setup, page 138.
The Enemies
The new world you have landed on is full of 
hostile lifeforms! As the campaign progresses, 
you will face off against multiple foes. You 
will discover these as you play the game, with 
each being generated randomly the first time 
you encounter them. As the game progresses, 
you will build a unique encounter list which 
will further change as the lifeforms evolve to 
fight you.
Later in the campaign you will also have to fight 
intelligent opposition. These can range from 
pirates to colony separatists to alien invaders. 
When they arrive on the world, you will engage 
in combat against these Tactical Enemies.
Finally, there are the mysterious threats like the 
alien Slyn that assail you at every step and the 
mechanical Sleepers that guard the 
alien ruins of the world. 
Each type of enemy has 
its own strengths and 
weaknesses and operates 
in a unique fashion, 
requiring you to 
adapt in order to win 
battles against them. 
You can read more 
about generating the 
enemies on page 146. 
Introduction
10

=== PAGE 13 ===
The Campaign Game
The tactical game is only half the story, 
however. The results of tactical missions feed 
into the campaign game, just as the results of 
the campaign game determine what forces 
and equipment you can bring on a mission. 
Together, the tactical and campaign games 
create the full experience, as events in one 
inform your pursuits in the other.
For example, some tactical missions may ask 
you to resolve a problem in the main campaign 
game, while at other times you will be pursuing 
specific goals in the campaign to bolster your 
troops in the field. 
 
◆For rules on beginning your main campaign, 
the campaign turn sequence, and how to use 
and expand the armory before each mission, 
see the Campaign section, page 48.
 
◆As your colony reaches Milestones, copes 
with Calamities and progresses towards 
the settlement’s End Game, you’ll need the 
Campaign Development section, page 155.
The Colony
The core of Planetfall is your colony. As you play, 
you will construct new facilities, expanding 
your capabilities and improving your efficiency. 
Without a well-developed colony, your 
expeditions in the field will struggle. 
The colony is also where your 
characters return between missions. Beyond 
your crew, it is home to a wide range of 
support personnel not otherwise tracked in 
the game, such as researchers, mechanics, 
armorers, and all the civilian personnel 
required to make a colony function. It’s still 
your job to help them thrive, however!
The colony might be safe for your characters, 
but it is not safe from threat: Attacks and 
disasters can inflict damage, jeopardizing the 
integrity of the colony. To learn how to build 
your colony and conduct research towards 
bigger and better resources, visit the Colonies 
section, page 81.
Research
The final component of success is Research.  
As you learn more about the world you are now 
living in, you will develop new facilities for your 
colony, new weapons to carry into the field, and 
even transcend the limitations of the Human 
frame itself. 
Research is a vital part of being successful on 
the new world and great care must be taken in 
staying up to date on new developments. You 
can read more about Research on page 91.
Getting Started
Ready to begin? If so, generate your first 
Colony Crew (page 14), and once you have 
familiarized yourself with the Combat Rules, 
head for the Initial Mission (page 44).
Game Markers
The missions make frequent use of game 
markers. You can use your own markers or 
download a free PDF sheet including the 
following from www.fiveparsecs.com:

=== PAGE 14 ===
The Find
“Are you reading this?”
“Affirm. Signal is clear...”. Breaking form, 
Operator added, “...though we have no idea 
what it is we’re seeing.”
A step closer, the scout panned the helm-cam 
again. 
“I don’t know either. It looks like some sort of 
ancient construction. Ship interior, maybe?”
The rangefinder beeped, the read-out indicating 
that, whatever it was, it was at least a mile long. 
The scout knelt and tapped his wrist-console, 
prompting the suit AI to begin processing. 
The comms crackled again.
“Point your camera at the ground. Science team 
wants to see if it’s a crash.” 
The scout held his hand in front of the camera 
and gave a thumbs up, then secured himself to 
a nearby rock formation. Giving the rope a solid 
tug, he slowly leaned forward over the cliffside 
to get the optimal view. 350 meters below, the 
ground was covered in the same violet grass-
like growth they’d seen near the landing site. 
“I don’t think it’s recent...” – he let the AI handle 
the camera – “...there’s things growing up the 
sides. It must have been here quite some time.”
Silence. The base team were conferring. 
Eventually, Operator came back, “Science says 
negative to crash site, they think it’s a building.”
“Yeah, could be,” the scout mused. Data 
summaries scrolling by on his helmet 
display. “AI says it may be built into the rock 
surface itself”.
Operators voice crackled once more. “They’re 
also saying origin unknown. Command is 
classifying this location as T-7. You know the drill 
from here on out.”
He grimaced while the camera finished up its 
work. It was only then that he saw it: A strange 
glow, faint at first, emanating from a structure 
below his position. For a moment, his mind 
remained locked on the procedural implications 
– investigation, description, categorization – 
before, finally, he acknowledged a growing 
disquiet, not just there within him, but 
emanating like some ancient susurrus of 
presence from the site itself.
5th Survey Group. Notation to K256 Files.
Researcher E. Davison, Senior Analyst. 
“Who lived here and what became of them 
is unknown and while galactic remnants are 
common across the galaxy, the nature and 
type of construction in the Reach appears 
only here. Many of the structures appear to 
be dug deep into the world itself or perhaps 
raised from it. While the decay of time has 
weathered away most of what must have 
been major facilities, these worlds surely 
featured prominently in some grand scheme 
of colonization or habitation. 
What is stranger is that many of the remains 
do not appear to serve obvious purposes. 
Initial surveys showed machinery may have 
been removed, though our suspicion is that 
systems are built into the structures themselves. 
More research on site will be required. 
While the remains on the surface are 
mainly of interest to archeologists and 
xenoanthropologists, there are many sites that 
contain interesting remnants and artifacts. What 
is more worrying is that these hidden sites 
are also defended by automated sentinels left 
behind to prevent those artifacts from falling 
into the wrong hands or perhaps any hands 
at all. An enhanced Unity security presence is 
recommended for all colonial expeditions.”

=== PAGE 16 ===
The Character Roster
Welcome to your new home, Colony 
Administrator! Let’s find out who came along 
with you to build this new project. In this 
section of the book, you will create the roster of 
characters you’ll use to play the game. 
Your colony crew has an initial roster of eight 
characters, expandable to ten over the course 
of a campaign (the maximum number of 
characters available to you). When you take on 
missions in the tactical game, you will usually 
use a subset of this roster. Note that due to 
injuries and deaths, you may well go into 
missions short-handed! 
The roster does not represent every character 
of that type. Your colony is assumed to 
have several security personnel, colonists, 
scientists, engineers, and others, in addition 
to those on your roster. Instead, your roster 
represents the most important slice of 
your colony. Think of them as the focal 
characters in a television show. You might 
see any number of other individuals in the 
background during each episode, but the 
story is ultimately about a small handful of 
recognizable people. 
Classes
There are three classes available to you: 
The scout, the scientist, and the trooper. 
 
◆Scientists are the brains of the operation, 
helping evaluate the strange and the alien, 
as well as improving the coordination of 
your team in the field.
 
◆Troopers bring the brawn, being trained 
in heavy weapons and armor. They are 
professional soldiers with tactical flexibility.
 
◆Scouts help with the exploration of the 
world and have superior battlefield mobility.
Your roster may contain any combination of 
these three classes, but you must include at 
least one of each. If you are uncertain what’s 
best for your initial roster, we recommend  
2 scouts, 2 scientists, and 4 troopers. 
Note that if you are importing a character 
from another game, they count against your 
roster limit. For example, if you were to bring 
two characters from a Five Parsecs from Home 
campaign, you would have six remaining 
character slots. 
Character-driven Stories
Several events will refer to characters on your 
roster. This always refers to the characters that 
can gain experience and progress. Incidental 
civilians that are along for a mission – bots and 
the military grunts that make up your fireteams 
– are NOT part of your character roster and can 
never be selected as the target of an event that 
affects characters on your roster.
Your Colony 
Crew
Your Colony Crew
14

=== PAGE 17 ===
Scientists
This class encompasses researchers, biologists, 
technicians, and more. While each has a 
specialty they were trained in, they carry AI-
assisted modules, giving them a wide range 
of addition AI expertise and instantly available 
situational analytics.
Scientists can only use Civilian and Scientist 
weapons. 
 
◆Scientific Mind: When making a Savvy test, 
scientists may roll the die twice and pick the 
best score.
 
◆Problem Solving: When making a Reaction 
roll, you may roll one additional die for 
each scientist on the table. After assigning 
initiative dice to your characters, any excess 
dice are dropped. 
Troopers
The brawn of the mission, troopers are veteran 
soldiers capable of dealing with an array of 
battlefield challenges. They not only benefit 
from enhanced combat abilities, but are capable 
of using a wide range of military weaponry.
Troopers can use Civilian and Trooper weapons 
and equipment. 
 
◆Trooper Armor: All troopers wear protective 
armor plating, giving them a 5+ Armor 
Saving Throw.
 
◆Intense Combat Training: A trooper 
activated in the Quick Action Phase (see 
page 28) may move and attack as normal 
or may choose to take two actions (such as 
firing twice). If they do, one action happens 
in the Quick Action Phase and the second in 
the Slow Action Phase. 
15
Your Colony Crew

=== PAGE 18 ===
Scouts
This class represents the specially trained scouts 
that every colonial expedition employs. Scouts 
can move quickly through a variety of terrain 
and excel in flexibility on and off the battlefield.
Scouts can use Civilian and Scout weapons and 
equipment. 
 
◆Flexible Combat Training: Unlike other 
characters, when a scout activates, they  
may choose to take their action before or 
after moving.
 
◆Jump Jets: Scouts wear jump modules, 
allowing short, powered jumps. When 
moving, a scout can choose to jump instead 
of walking. The distance traversed is equal to 
the normal movement speed.
The jump must be in a straight line but 
can clear obstacles up to double human 
height and can be used to jump on and off 
things. Scouts can descend from any height 
without taking damage, but a drop beyond 
their movement speed uses up the entire 
activation. 
Grunts
You also start with 12 common soldiers known 
as grunts. Should this number drop below 
12, more can be trained, though they are not 
an infinite resource. These common soldiers 
do not need to be listed separately on your 
roster, merely tracked on your Colony Tracking 
Sheet, though you may like to name them in 
the 'Notes’ box as you play the campaign – it is 
more interesting to know that Private Estrada is 
the one that took on an alien beast alone.  
You will be accompanied into combat missions 
by fireteams of such soldiers. These are not 
as experienced or battle-hardened as your 
troopers, but they are proficient and capable 
military personnel.
Unlike your main characters, grunts do not gain 
experience or progress. Grunts always have the 
same profile and are not tracked individually. 
In the campaign game, they use a simplified 
casualty table to determine their fates. Grunts 
use their own weaponry (usable only by them) 
and cannot be outfitted with other weapons.
Each mission type will state if it allows the use 
of grunts and, if so, how many you can bring.
Your Colony Crew
16

=== PAGE 19 ===
Civvies
Civvies do not appear on your roster but may 
show up during missions. If you are short on 
people, you may also opt to bring along civvies 
to fill any missing positions. 
Civvies have the basic profile on page 18 and 
have no special abilities. They can only use 
Civilian weapons.
They do not progress in campaign experience, 
and the same civvy will not typically participate 
in multiple missions (though for narrative 
purposes you might decide that, for example, 
the newly recruited trooper was actually a 
civvy from a prior mission who has now 
undergone basic training).
The Colony Bot
Bots are cybernetic assistants commonly 
assigned to dangerous missions. They can 
stand in for a wide range of crew roles in the 
field and are easily repaired if damaged during 
a mission. You have a single bot available. It 
can be named (and Unity soldiers often do give 
their bot compatriots a name) or you can give 
it a designation of letters and numbers to suit 
their artificial nature. 
The Incremental Productions Synth-Person 
(nicknamed Eypees by Unity troopers, and 
more commonly colony bots) were developed 
to be a multi-purpose bot suitable for 
colonization and exploration efforts. 
As research shows colonists tend to prefer 
the bots being individually distinguishable, a 
number of personality modules are available. 
The basic chassis is humanoid in form. 
You begin the campaign with the Eypee colony 
bot. You may opt to bring the bot along with 
you on a mission in place of another character, 
but the bot cannot gain experience or receive 
any upgrades. This makes it valuable early in a 
campaign, but it will eventually be outclassed 
by your other characters.
In combat, the bot can use Civilian weapons. 
Bots receive the following abilities:
 
◆Made of Metal: Bots receive a 6+ Armor 
Saving Throw when hit by attacks.
 
◆Mindless and Strong: When playing 
special scenarios, bots are never affected 
by psionic influences that are not strictly 
physical in nature (so a bot cannot be 
mind-controlled but could be knocked over 
by a blow of psionic force). They are never 
penalized or slowed down for carrying or 
hauling heavy items. 
 
◆Replaceable: Bots do not roll for post-
battle injuries. If a bot becomes a casualty 
during the game, it is broken. Your 
facilities can repair or replace one bot every 
campaign turn.
Bot Models
Players of Five Parsecs from Home will note that 
bots have both a different starting profile and 
different ways in which they are handled during 
a Planetfall campaign. This reflects the types of 
bots assigned to exploration and colonization 
fleets, which are optimized for durability rather 
than combat potential.
17
Your Colony Crew

=== PAGE 20 ===
Initial Character Profiles
Each character begins with a starting profile 
as shown in the table below. Copy this data to 
the roster on your Colony Tracking Sheet 
(see page 190).
Choose eight profiles from among scientists, 
scouts, and troopers. You will also have 
12 grunts and one bot. When choosing your 
scientists, scouts, and troopers, you can choose 
Human sub-species and other non-Human 
profiles – see page 19. 
Ability Scores
Each character has a profile with 
ability scores that provide their basic 
combat characteristics. Players of 
other Five Parsecs games will 
recognize that the abilities are 
directly compatible.
Reactions
A measure of initiative, observation, and 
alertness. A character with high Reactions has 
more flexibility in the combat round sequence. 
Reactions range from 1 to 5, with a typical 
civilian having 1.
Speed
The ability of the character to cover ground in 
a tactical situation. This is always listed as a 
number of tabletop inches. A typical civilian 
has a Speed of 4”. 
Combat Skill
A measure of weapons training, 
fighting ability, and killer instinct. 
This is listed as a bonus to  
combat dice rolls. A typical 
civilian has +0.
Toughness
How rugged and tough the 
character is and how well they 
can keep fighting despite 
minor injuries. This ranges 
from 3 to 6, with a 
typical civilian 
 having 3.
Savvy
Wits, logical reasoning, and some 
technical aptitude. This is used for 
problem solving and to get out of 
certain sticky situations. It is listed 
as a bonus to a dice roll with a typical 
person having a +0.

Imported Characters and Difficulty
A character brought over from another Five 
Parsecs from Home campaign arrives with 
considerable advantages in terms of XP 
gained and items acquired. This will make 
the initial game a little easier. They bring 
the abilities they had in that campaign 
(as far as those abilities apply to the 
Planetfall rules). Crew are assumed to be 
advisors, mercenaries, or Unity-affiliated 
agents with particular talents that are 
going to be beneficial to the mission.
See “Imported Characters” (page 26) 
for more info. 
Your Colony Crew
18

=== PAGE 21 ===
Human Sub-Species
There are 57 registered sub-species of 
Humanity according to Unity Survey data. Of 
these, three are considered of particular value 
to colonists: Feral, Hulkers, and Stalkers. You 
may opt to include a maximum of one Feral 
plus one sub-species of choice (Hulker, Stalker, 
or a second Feral).
Select any character on your roster and add the 
following modifications and abilities.
Feral
Genetic uplifts based on various Earth 
carnivores, Feral most commonly resemble 
humanoids with fur and wolf-like facial features 
and tails. While originally developed for military 
purposes, they can be found throughout 
Human space. 
Feral have no adjustments to their profile, but 
receive the following two characteristics:
 
◆Keen Senses: All visibility limitations and 
detection ranges are increased by +1” 
for a Feral. For example, if something was 
normally spotted at 6” range, a Feral would 
spot it at 7” range instead.
 
◆Impetuous: When making a Reaction Roll, 
if you roll a single 1, it must be assigned 
to a Feral character, if possible. If you roll 
multiple 1s, you are not required to do so.
Hulker
Gene-modded to have massive strength, the 
Hulker is an intimidating individual, towering 
over the average Human.
Hulkers begin with Toughness 5.
They receive the following characteristics:
 
◆Poor Aim: Hulkers have poor eyesight. They 
always count as having Combat Skill +0 
when shooting and can receive no weapon 
hit bonuses.
 
◆Great Strength: Hulkers do not suffer a 
penalty when using heavy weapons. In 
brawling combat, they can strike using their 
fists (Melee weapon, +1 Damage).
19
Your Colony Crew

=== PAGE 22 ===
Stalker
A rare gene-mod, the Stalker physically 
resembles an ordinary Human except for the 
blue coloration of their skin.
Stalkers have no adjustments to their profile, 
but receive the following characteristic:
 
◆Shift!: Instead of moving normally, the 
Stalker may conduct a short-range teleport. 
Roll 1D6 and position the figure anywhere 
within this distance in inches. This is not 
regular movement, and the figure can shift 
through terrain and other figures. They may 
still take a normal action. 
Soulless emissary
Some colonies have created highly unusual 
partnerships with Soulless who, for 
diplomatic reasons, decided to support the 
colony as robotic support and or security. It is 
not uncommon for Souless representatives 
to travel with starship crews, so this does 
represent an intriguing dilemma for a 
colony. Do they refuse the help and possibly 
create tension with a nearby Soulless 
expedition, or accept the help and risk the 
Soulless having access to the colonies' data 
and risk a security breach?
You may include a single Soulless emissary, 
taking up a roster slot.
 
◆Soulless can only occupy the Trooper 
class due to security restrictions. 
 
◆The Soulless character receives a 6+ 
Saving Throw due to their metal 
construction and begins the campaign 
with Toughness 4 and a +1 Savvy score.
 
◆Soulless cannot receive any 
Augmentations. 
 
◆Due to the need for repairs, they use the 
normal injury table after missions. They 
cannot benefit from any accelerated 
healing and recovery.
Alien Characters
If you own the Five Parsecs from Home core 
rules or Compendium, you may also select alien 
characters from those books. These are assumed 
to be specially assigned to the mission and have 
been granted Unity citizenship. 
Your Colony Crew
20

=== PAGE 23 ===
Character Backgrounds
Your roster of eight initial characters includes 
four experienced characters who have had 
longer careers behind them and bring a wider 
range of talents into the colonization project. 
If you have now chosen your eight roster 
characters, select which characters to define as 
experienced.
For each experienced character, roll on each of 
the three tables below – Motivations, Prior 
Experience, and Notable Event – and track any 
modifications listed. 
The four inexperienced characters are 
straight out of their professional training 
programs. They will roll on the Motivation 
table only. 
Motivations
What compels someone to leave everything 
they know behind and set out on a dangerous 
and uncertain path among the stars? The 
motivations of a character will become 
important in the campaign endgame. 
Both experienced and inexperienced characters 
roll on this table.
Prior Experience
Prior experience details what earlier careers 
or life paths the character may have followed. 
Experienced characters roll on this table. If 
you want a more detailed story, roll twice, 
treating the first roll as history just after 
adulthood and the second roll as history just 
prior to being recruited. Claim the bonus for 
the second roll only. 
Note that prior experience does not have to 
line up with the class chosen (though you are 
welcome to do so). The colonization programs 
screen for mindset as much as for skills, and 
someone with an army career may well have 
the outlook that the scientific teams need. After 
all, practical experience can be accelerated 
through Hyper-Learning Implants. The long 
and the short of it is that you are free to make 
interesting character combinations if you like. 
Inexperienced characters do not receive any 
bonuses from this table. By default, they do not 
roll on it but, if you prefer to add a bit of flavor, 
you can do so. They are assumed to have only 
been in that career or path for a brief period 
before being recruited.

Equipment
At the outset, the colony operates a central 
equipment store rather than characters owning 
individual items. Characters are assigned 
standard equipment when a mission begins, 
with options according to class-based training. 
When creating your roster, leave the character 
items blank, and fill out this section once your 
characters receive the mission briefing (during 
the ‘Lock and Load’ step of the campaign turn,

=== PAGE 26 ===
Experience
As you undertake missions and train, your 
characters will build up their Experience 
Points (XP) which are tracked individually for 
each character.
The XP you earn can be cashed in for upgrades 
that will improve character performance. 
Progressing an already talented character can 
really improve your chances, but players should 
take care not to over-specialize: The unexpected 
death of an elite character can leave a serious 
hole in your line up if your replacements are all 
green recruits straight out of the Academy. You 
may find that it advantageous to bring along 
a rookie or two on a mission, so they can get 
some experience under their belts. 
Character Loyalty
Characters can exhibit three stages of loyalty to 
the administration:
 
◆Loyal: They believe strongly in the mission 
and are unlikely to be a security risk.
 
◆Committed: This is the default state and 
represents the character just trying to do 
their job.
 
◆Disloyal: They believe that the 
administration is leading them down a 
poor path or has begun associating with 
dissident groups.
Your rolls on the character creation tables may 
result in some characters being Loyal. Imported 
characters also begin the campaign Loyal. 
Everyone else should be marked as Committed.
Note: Grunts do not have Loyalty ratings and 
are never affected by events or factors that 
modify Loyalty.
Voice Note 2144.Alpha. 
Scout Ship Taurine, 5th Survey Group.
“As you can see, the landscape is dotted 
with strange ruins and collapsed structures, 
potentially dating back thousands of years. Some 
are completely overgrown, while others jut into 
the sky. Their construction varies, some jet-black 
stone, others showing clear signs of extensive 
circuitry and power conduits. Deep penetration 
scans are inconclusive about what lurks within 
these ruins. Based on previous reports, we’ve 
been careful not to disturb any sentinels. And 
this is just one valley, repeated across the world. 
Whatever was here before was on a huge scale.”

The Administrator
The Administrator represents you in the 
campaign. When decisions are being made 
about the colony, they come from the 
Administrator and reflect your priorities and 
decisions in the campaign. Unlike the other 
characters, there are no stats or miniatures. 
The Administrator does not take the field in 
missions and cannot engage in combat.
Many players will want to paint a miniature to 
represent their Administrator. You can leave 
it sitting at the side of the table for good luck 
when playing a tabletop battle, or leave a dice 
next to it to show the campaign turn. 
You should decide the physical presence of 
your Administrator for roleplay purposes. 
Typically, they will be on the colony to direct 
things in person, but they could also be located 
in an orbiting station.
To determine the Administrator past history for 
flavor purposes, roll or pick on the table below.
You may choose to take this career 
into account when making 
decisions. For example, a prior 
military Administrator may prefer 
a strong military presence. 
However, you are not 
required to do so and 
can make your own 
decisions at any time.

Imported Characters
Treat any character brought in from another 
Five Parsecs game as Crew: Specialists with 
unique skills now attached to the mission.
Five Parsecs from Home characters
 
◆All ability scores remain the same. 
 
◆They will retain any combat abilities they 
had in that game.
 
◆Characters with Luck points receive 1 Kill 
Point per Luck point they would have had. 
Kill Points are explained in the combat 
rules (see page 39). Optionally, you may 
prefer using the Luck system from the 
original rules.
Five Parsecs Bug Hunt characters
 
◆All ability scores remain the same. Tech is 
converted to Savvy (or vice versa).
Five Parsecs Tactics characters
 
◆Ability scores except Kill Points and Training 
remain the same.
 
◆Reduce Kill Points by 1 to match how they 
are calculated in Planetfall. 
 
◆Training is not used in Planetfall.  
 
◆Veteran Skills are retained when applicable 
to Planetfall rules set.
Class Training
Some promising individuals will be given 
expanded training when attached to a mission. 
If you are importing characters, you may train 
up to three characters as follows:
 
◆A single character may be given the  
trooper class.
 
◆A single character may be given the  
scientist class.
 
◆A single character may be given the  
scout class.
To qualify, the character must pass the Unity 
Field Aptitude Test. If the character has a 
background or history that fits in the related 
class (military for troopers, academic for 
scientists, exploration and wilderness for 
scouts), they automatically qualify due to prior 
experience.
 
◆Trooper Backgrounds: Military Brat,  
War-Torn Hell Hole
 
◆Trooper Classes: Soldier, Mercenary, 
Enforcer
 
◆Scientist Backgrounds: Tech-guild, 
Research outpost
 
◆Scientist Classes: Scientist, Technician, 
Hacker
Your Colony Crew
26

=== PAGE 29 ===
 
◆Scout Backgrounds: Long Term Space 
Mission, Drifter
 
◆Scout Classes: Explorer, Scavenger, 
Negotiator
You may also attempt to pass the test manually. 
Each character can only test once. The test is 
conducted by a series of examiners and AI 
modules which will assess the qualities of a 
given candidate with their psych profile and 
current operational needs. Roll 1d6:
D6
RESULT
1-2
The character fails the aptitude test and 
is not approved for class training.
3
The character is approved for a random 
class. Roll 1D6 again: 1-2 is trooper,  
3-4 is scientist, 5-6 is scout.
4-6
The character is approved for a class 
of your choice.
Progression for Imported Characters
Players of other Five Parsecs from Home 
rule sets should note that progression works 
differently in Planetfall. You are encouraged to 
use the new progression system while in the 
Planetfall campaign. 
Characters are always brought in with the 
profiles they had in their parent system. Any 
unused XP is kept but other advancement 
values, such as Bug Hunt’s Reputation, have no 
value in Planetfall. The same applies in reverse 
when moving to another campaign. 
Intangibles such as Patrons and Rivals do not 
matter in Planetfall.
Multiple Campaigns at Once
A character can only be in one place at once. 
If a character moves to Planetfall, they can no 
longer adventure with their old crew or squad, 
for example.
At the end of a campaign (or if a character 
leaves a campaign for any reason) characters 
can transfer campaigns. For example, 
any character that leaves the Planetfall 
campaign could be imported into another 
Five Parsecs game. 
You may retire characters from Planetfall if 
you want. For example, you might decide that 
some of your characters want to strike out on 
their own.
Imported Equipment
Tactics and Bug Hunt characters do not normally 
carry their equipment with them (it is, after all, 
military property).
Five Parsecs from Home characters may carry 
over any personal equipment, provided it 
has a rules function within the game. For 
example, as Planetfall does not have a Seize 
the Initiative roll, items that affect this will not 
have any effect. 
What constitutes a character’s personal 
equipment is left to the player to decide. The 
intention is not to let one character make off 
with everything the crew owned. 
Note that some weapons have different 
profiles in Planetfall. Characters may either 
retain the original version or acquire the 
Planetfall counterpart.
Characters transferring out of Planetfall may 
keep their weaponry. They can only bring 
reasonably personal equipment – you cannot 
load up with 20 pistols and give them to 
your new Five Parsecs from Home crew. 
Planetfall-specific weapons, including ones 
gained from research, cannot be replaced in 
another campaign.
Credits have no value in Planetfall, and no 
Credits can be earned during the campaign for 
use elsewhere.

Your Colony Crew

=== PAGE 30 ===
Introduction to Combat
This chapter provides all the rules and 
information needed to conduct the tactical 
miniatures game using a tabletop battlefield.
The Campaign rules (page 48) cover how to 
determine the enemies you face and what 
troops you will have available. 
Veteran Players
Veterans of other Five Parsecs game titles 
should read the combat rules carefully. Some 
rules have been tailored to the characters and 
combat environments of Planetfall.  
The Battle Round Sequence
The game is played in a series of battle rounds 
(or simply ‘rounds’), during which every figure 
on the table has a chance to act. Each round is 
subdivided into phases, during each of which 
certain activities take place. 
Carry out each phase in order, completing all 
activities before moving on to the next one.
1.	 Start Phase
2.	 Reaction Roll
3.	 Quick Actions Phase
4.	 Enemy Actions Phase
5.	 Slow Actions Phase
6.	 End Phase
Start Phase
Any activities described as occurring at the 
start of a round take place in this phase. If 
multiple Start Phase events are taking place 
simultaneously, resolve them in any order 
you like.
Reaction Roll
The player rolls and assigns Initiative dice. 
Quick Actions Phase
Any player-controlled character assigned an 
Initiative die equal to or below their Reaction 
score acts in this phase. The player can choose 
the order in which these characters act.
Enemy Actions Phase
All hostile forces act in this phase, including any 
presented by Contact markers. 
Slow Actions Phase
Any player-controlled character assigned an 
Initiative die higher than their Reaction score 
acts in this phase. In the event a character lacks 
an Initiative die for any reason, they also act in 
this phase. The player can choose the order in 
which these characters act.
End Phase
In this phase, you check to see if the battle is 
at an end, whether due to no player-controlled 
forces remaining on the table or because the 
Combat 
rules
Combat Rules
28

=== PAGE 31 ===
scenario rules dictate that it is over.  If not, any 
activities described as happening at the end of 
a round take place now. If multiple End Phase 
events are taking place simultaneously, resolve 
them in any order you like.
The Reaction Roll
The Reaction Roll determines the phase in 
which player-controlled characters will act. First, 
roll the same number of D6 as you have player 
characters on the battlefield. 
Assign the D6 results to the characters of  
your choice. 
Any character assigned a die equal to or below 
their Reaction score will act in the Quick 
Action Phase.
All other characters act in the Slow Action 
Phase.
Note that all rolled dice must be assigned to a 
character and each character can receive only 
one die. Some factors can result in the player 
rolling more dice than they have characters. 
After rolling, assign dice to your characters and 
then discard any excess dice. 
An easy way to avoid losing track is leaving the 
die next to each character until they have acted. 
Turn the Quick Actions to show a 1 and the Slow 
Actions to show a 6, then remove the die once 
that character acts. 
Note that the enemy does NOT roll for 
Reactions. All opposing forces act in the Enemy 
Action Phase.
Quick Thinking
Each scientist on your team allows you to roll 
one additional die for during the Reaction Roll. 
After assigning one die to each character, any 
excess dice are discarded. Scientists do not allow 
you to take more actions, they just increase your 
chances of having characters move before the 
enemy does. If scientists become casualties, the 
extra dice are lost at the start of the next battle 
round. No change happens mid-round.
29
Combat Rules

=== PAGE 32 ===
Fireteams and Initiative
Grunts are organized into fireteams, typically 
four to a team, and formed at the point of 
deployment. You cannot form or dissolve a 
fireteam during a battle, and it cannot include 
any other character type. 
Fireteams are treated as a single character 
for initiative and activation purposes: They 
contribute a single die to the pool of initiative 
dice and are assigned a single die. Each 
member of the fireteam will usually activate 
in the same phase depending on the die they 
were collectively assigned. 
Fireteams benefit from additional coordination 
when they are in formation: 
If all fireteam members left on the table are 
within 2” of each other (forming a chain, blob, 
or other formation) they count as having a 
Reaction score one point higher (i.e. 3 instead 
of a grunt’s usual 2).
Every fireteam member must be within 
formation distance during the Reaction Roll 
to get this bonus, and there must be at least 
two grunts left. (A single surviving grunt is 
not a formation!)
Player Character Actions
Within the Quick and Slow Action Phases, 
the player can choose the order in which each 
character will act. Each character carries out 
their intended actions one at a time. You do 
not have to declare any actions in advance: For 
example, if one character is going to shoot, you 
can see what the results of that are before you 
pick the next character to act. 
Once a character has been activated, they 
cannot be activated again this round and 
you cannot take part of an activation, act with 
a different character, and then go back to a 
prior character.
The activations work differently depending on 
the type of character being activated:
Player Character Activations
 
◆Scientist, Bot, Civvy, Crew, Grunts: 
The character can perform one Move and 
then perform a single action. 
Note that grunts in fireteams are not 
required to all take the same actions. 
 
◆Scout: The character can perform one 
Move and one action. Scouts may choose 
to move before or after taking their action, 
but the character must complete all of their 
movement at one time.
 
◆Trooper: Troopers can perform one Move 
and then perform a single action. A trooper 
assigned to the Quick Action Phase may 
instead choose to forego moving and take 
one action in each of the Quick and Slow 
Action Phases (typically allowing them to 
attack twice). 
Player Character-controlled Activations
In special scenarios you might have non-player 
characters temporarily fighting alongside the 
player characters. In this case, they are treated 
as part of the player’s forces and have Reaction 
dice rolled normally. A character without a 
Reaction score does not receive a rolled result, 
automatically going in the Slow Actions Phase.
Delaying
The player may opt to roll fewer dice than they 
are allowed. Any character not assigned a die will 
act in the Slow Action Phase.
Note that a character assigned a die must act in 
the phase indicated by the die. 
Combat Rules
30

=== PAGE 33 ===
The Actions
The following actions are generally available 
to player characters:
ACTION
DESCRIPTION
Carry Out Task
Perform a task required 
in a scenario.
Rush
Move an additional 2". 
Shoot
Fire one weapon you 
are carrying.
Aid
Aids an ally within 3”. 
Place an Aid marker on the ally 
if they do not already have one 
OR remove a Stun marker 
from the ally.
Use Equipment
Use a carried item which 
requires an action.
Enemy Actions
In the Enemy Actions Phase, the enemy 
activates all specialists first, then activates all 
remaining figures. In both cases, activate the 
enemies closest to the player’s battlefield edge 
first, then progress backwards across the table.
Note that only Tactical Enemies have specialists.
Enemies only use the Shoot option in normal 
play. In special scenarios or when playing with 
a Game Master, the enemy can perform actions 
suitable to the scenario.  
 
◆Enemy with ranged weapons move as 
discussed in the movement rules below. If 
they have Line of Sight to an opponent, they 
will then take an action to fire at the target.
 
◆Enemy without ranged weapons or 
who are out of range will simply move, 
(potentially entering Brawling combat –  
see page 39).
Hesitation
Lifeforms and Tactical Enemies that activate 
without an opponent visible to them must 
roll 1D6. On a 1, they hesitate and do not 
act this Phase.  The Slyn and Sleepers are not 
affected by this.
End Phase
First, check if any scenario conditions have 
been met that would end the battle. A scenario 
always ends if, during the End Phase, there 
are no player characters on the table. Note 
that an absence of enemy figures does NOT 
automatically end the scenario if the scenario 
rules allow for more enemy figures to arrive.
If you are fighting the Slyn, the battle ends if, at 
the end of the round, only a single pair remains 
on the table.
During this phase, check for anything that says 
it should happen at the end of the round.
If you are fighting Lifeforms or Tactical Enemies 
and inflicted casualties this round, Panic checks 
are made now (see page 40). Note that some 
enemies are Fearless, such as the Sleeper robot 
species (see page 154).
Slyn do not take Panic checks, but if only a 
single pair remains on the table at this stage, 
the battle ends immediately as they teleport 
out (see page 152). 
CARRYING OUT Tasks
Scenario objectives and conditions often 
require a Task roll. Characters must be within 
1” of the terrain feature, objective, or similar 
feature to perform a Task.
To perform the Task, roll 1D6 and add the 
ability score mentioned in the scenario, 
typically Savvy. A roll equal to or above the 
target number indicated in the scenario is 
a success. 
Unless otherwise stated, a particular Task can 
only be attempted once per battle round.

Combat Rules

=== PAGE 34 ===
AI ENEMY VariationS 
(optional rule)
This rule is optional as it adds an additional 
die roll to the game sequence. It is included 
for players who prefer a more unpredictable 
battle where the enemy will not always act 
as expected. You can use this rule in some 
battles and not in others as you see fit. It is not 
required that you use it in every encounter.
AI Enemy Variations apply only to Lifeforms and 
Tactical Enemies. It does not apply to the Slyn or 
Sleeper enemy types. 
To use AI Enemy Variations, roll 1D6 at the start 
of the Enemy Actions Phase and consult the 
table below. A ploy means one of the enemies 
in the encounter is up to something, but an AI 
action means one of the enemies gets an idea 
into their heads. 
D6
AI VARIATION
1
Roll on the Ploy table
2-4
Nothing happens
5-6
Roll on the AI Action table 
As the Ploy and AI Action tables also use a D6, 
you can speed up the process by rolling two 
differently colored dice at the same time, with 
one color being the AI Variation table and 
the other color die only being read if you are 
directed to one of the other tables. If this seems 
too confusing, just roll one at a time. 
There are separate tables for Tactical Enemies 
and Lifeforms. Ensure you use the correct one.
The ploy or action affects a single enemy figure. 
Randomly select which target figure from those 
present on the table. 
Tactical Enemies Ploys table
D6
PLOY
DESCRIPTION
1
Redeploy
The target is moved to the nearest terrain feature and placed on the highest point. 
2
Take charge
The next Panic die rolled by the enemy side is ignored. 
3
Recovery
The target removes all Stun and Sprawling markers. It then activates normally. 
Note that the target for this ploy must be an enemy with Stun or Sprawling 
markers. If nobody eligible is on the table, there is no effect.
4-5
Reinforcements
An additional enemy with basic weapons arrives at the center of the enemy 
battlefield edge. The arriving figure does not activate this phase.
6
Concentrated 
attack
Affects a player character. Any enemy that can see this character will fire on them 
this phase and receive a +1 hit bonus.
The Enemy are not Idiots
In a game like this, you need to inject a plethora 
of moment-by-moment decisions into moving 
and positioning enemy forces. If you are 
uncertain what to do, review the AI guidelines 
and if you cannot find a satisfactory answer, 
randomly pick from the available options. 
In most cases, defaulting to “the enemy would 
pick the most logical approach”, will do just fine.
When in doubt, remember that the enemy is 
intended to represent people (or creatures or 
robots) who are trying to win a fight. An action 
that is sub-optimal is fine: A Human player may 
not want a single combatant to engage several 
opponents, but the AI is happy to do so because 
it keeps the game moving along. However, 
the AI should not act in ways that are evidently 
suicidal or engineered to give you a cheap win.

D6
ACTION
DESCRIPTION
1
Hold position
The target will remain in place and defend their current position for the rest of the 
battle (unless selected as a target for another AI action). They will move to cover 
within one move, if currently in the open and cover is available.
2
Hesitate
The target does not act this phase unless they have opponents within 6”, 
in which case they remain in place and fire.
3-4

The target does not move this phase but may fire normally.

The target will move towards the nearest terrain feature that is closer to the players 
battlefield edge, rushing if needed. 
6

The target will move towards the nearest ally and try to get within 2”. 
If already within 2” of an ally, they will stick with them this activation.
Lifeform Ploys table
D6
PLOY
DESCRIPTION
1
Call for more
The target remains in place and takes no actions this phase. 
When they activate next, place an additional enemy within 1” of their position. 
This is interrupted if they are knocked Sprawling or killed before then.
2
Hide
Remove the target from the table and place a Contact in the nearest terrain feature.
3
Frenzy
The target removes all Stun and Sprawling markers. It will move at full speed 
towards the nearest opponent and may engage in brawling combat.
4
Lurk
The target does not activate this phase. Until it has activated again,  
a -1 hit penalty applies to shots against it. When it activates again, roll for a 
lifeform ploy (see page 22) and apply the result to the lurking figure (which can 
result in two ploys happening in the same phase, but not to the same figure). 
If the lurker is knocked Sprawling or killed, the ploy does not occur.
5-6
Flanking 
attack
An additional Contact is placed at the center of a random battlefield edge. 
The new arrival does not activate this phase.
Lifeform AI Actions table
D6
ACTION
DESCRIPTION
1
Focus
The target randomly picks a visible opponent (closest enemy if none are visible). 
It will continue moving towards and trying to kill that target for the rest of the 
battle. This is interrupted if the same figure is the target of another AI action.
2
Hesitate
The target takes no actions this round but recovers normally if 
Stunned or Sprawling.
3-4
Move to cover
This activation, the target will move to the closest terrain feature that provides 
cover. If already in cover, it must remain there this activation.
5-6
Move to flank
This activation, the target moves towards the nearest neutral battlefield edge, 
trying to remain out of sight or in cover.

Combat Rules

=== PAGE 36 ===
Movement
When a character moves, they may move up to 
their Speed score in tabletop inches. Figures 
can turn as often as they like. 
Characters cannot move through each other 
under any circumstances. 
Obstacles can be crossed by treating the vertical 
height as additional movement both up and 
down. For example, a 2” tall wall costs 4” to 
climb up and back down to the other side. If a 
wall or obstacle is wide enough for a figure to 
stand on, a figure can end the climb on top of it. 
The width of the obstacle must then be added to 
the distance moved when crossing.
The same system is used for climbing terrain 
features provided they are obviously climbable. 
Ground that is challenging to move through 
(e.g. mud, sand, or jungle) is designated 
Difficult ground: Every full 2” moved in such 
terrain consumes 1” additional movement 
allowance. Characters with flying movement 
are not subject to this penalty. 
Some terrain features cannot be entered. 
These are called Block features and include 
large boulders and similar. They can 
sometimes be climbed but typically act as 
impediments to sight and movement that 
must be moved around. 
Fireteams are not required to remain in 
formation as they move, though if they move to 
break formation, they will not receive the bonus 
in the next battle round. For example, you may 
need one grunt to go check out something or 
to spread out to engage the enemy effectively.
Brawling Combat
A moving character that contacts an opponent 
will enter brawling combat (see page 39): This 
must be resolved immediately. Brawling ends 
the activation of the character. However, being 
attacked in brawling combat does not prevent a 
character activating later in the round.
Enemy Movement
Lifeforms without ranged attacks will move 
towards the closest visible opponent to try to 
engage in brawling combat. If no opponents 
are visible to an enemy, randomly pick from the 
three closest opponents. The Lifeform will move 
towards the selected opponent.
Lifeforms with ranged attacks will only move if 
they are currently unable to attack. If so, they 
will move the shortest distance that will enable 
them to make an attack. Intelligent enemies 
will try to move towards the nearest opponents.
Once within 12” they will halt to fire unless 
their modifier (Combat Skill+Weapon Bonus) 
exceeds that of the nearest opponent, in which 
case they will continue to move to get close 
enough to engage in brawling combat. 
Intelligent enemies always try to move through 
cover if possible but will always prioritize being 
able to attack over their own protection.
Tactical enemy specialists will remain in place 
and fire if they can see any opponents in range. 
If no targets are visible, they will move towards 
the closest location that lets them establish a 
line of fire against a target.
The enemy does not take Rush moves, always 
moving at the standard pace in their profiles. 
The enemy typically ignores Objective markers 
unless the details for a particular mission type 
dictate otherwise. 
Jumping Down and Falling
A character can intentionally jump down. A 
jump over 1” high ends the activation. Scouts 
can jump down from any distance with no 
damage and can continue their activation if 
they jump down less than their Speed in inches.
A character falling down 3” or more takes a 
Damage +0 Hit and is knocked Sprawling. 
Combat Rules
34

=== PAGE 37 ===
Questionable Shots – 
the Weasel Rule
Sometimes it can be hard to determine if a 
figure is visible or if it is protected by cover.
Rather than spending a lot of time agonizing 
about this, apply this rule:
If you are uncertain, assume a figure can be 
seen but is in cover.
This means a figure is out of sight when it is 
clearly not visible at all and is in the open when 
it is clearly not behind any terrain feature. Keep 
this in mind and you can adjudicate the majority 
of questionable cases easily.
Shooting
When taking a Shoot action, the character 
may perform a ranged attack. Characters with 
multiple weapons may fire only one of them 
but you can pick which weapon each time the 
character activates. 
Fireteam members are not all required to fire, 
nor are they required to pick the same targets. 
Player characters may shoot at any enemy 
visible to them and within range (see below). 
However, enemies will always target the visible 
opponent they have the highest chance of 
hitting. If multiple targets are equally easy to 
hit, they target the closest one.
Range and Shots
Each weapon has a Range, measured in 
tabletop inches. Targets can only be fired at if 
they are within this range. Note that weapon 
ranges are compact, representing hip shots in a 
hasty skirmish environment, not the theoretical 
range of a futuristic weapon on a firing range or 
in the hands of a sharpshooter. 
You can check distances at any time. There is no 
need to try to guess if you are within range. 
Each weapon also has a Shot rating (often 
stated as ‘Shots’), which determines how many 
attacks are rolled, with each shot representing a 
volley of bullets or a short burst of fire.
If a weapon has multiple shots, resolve each 
one at a time. If you kill a target before you 
have run out of shots, you may select a new 
target within 3” of the prior target. 
Visibility, Terrain, and Cover
It is substantially more difficult to hit a target 
using cover than one nearby and in the open. 
In some cases, positioning a figure behind or 
within terrain prevents anyone targeting a 
shot at all. There are two considerations related 
relating to whether you can target someone: 
Cover and Line of Sight.
Line of Sight
To determine if two figures can shoot at each 
other at all, determine whether the shooter has 
Line of Sight (LoS). Imagine a straight line 
drawn between the figures: If it reaches the 
target – any part of the target – they are in sight. 
If a terrain feature or another figure is in the 
way, there is no Line of Sight.
In some situations, two figures may have Line 
of Sight of each other but one enjoys Cover the 
other does not (see below).
Line of Sight Example:
Example: In the diagram above, (A) has 
Line of Sight to (B1) and (B2), but (B2) is 
partially hidden behind a high wall and 
benefits from Cover. There is no LoS to (B3).
A
B1B1
B2B2
✘
B3B3
35
Combat Rules

=== PAGE 38 ===
Cover
A character is in Cover if there is terrain 
between them and the firer which would 
obscure their position.
Characters within forest, bush, and similar 
vegetation (all Area features) can only see 
outside and can only be seen from the outside 
if they are at the edge of the terrain feature 
(and then only to the side on which they are 
placed). Characters within the same vegetation 
feature can see each other if they are within 
3” of each other. Treat all characters within 
vegetation as having Cover. 
A Linear obstacle such as a wall does not count 
if the shooter is within 1” of it, as the shooter 
can fire over it with ease. (Assume walls are low 
enough to fire over unless suggested otherwise 
by a scenario or specific terrain piece.)
High ground provides an advantage when 
firing. A target on lower ground only receives 
the benefits of Cover if they are within 1” of 
the far side of a terrain feature between them 
and the shooter. For example, a shooter on a 
hill could shoot at targets on the other side of a 
wall without counting them as being in Cover, 
unless they are within 1” of the wall. 
Cover Example 1:
Figure (B) is at the edge of a forested Area 
feature while figure (A) is in the open.  
Figure (B) receives Cover. Figure (A) does  
not receive Cover when fired upon by (B).
 
Cover Example 2:
Figure (C) is within 1" of a waist-high wall. 
Figure (D) is 10" away from the wall. 
When (D) fires on (C), (C) will receive Cover 
as it crosses the obstacle. When (C) fires 
on (D), (D) does not have Cover since the 
shooter is within 1” of the linear obstacle.
No Cover
Has Cover

B
Within 1" = 
receives Cover

D
Combat Rules
