=== PAGE 39 ===
Scatter Terrain
Individual terrain pieces that are roughly 
the size of one figure, such as single trees, 
boulders, strange crystals, and so on, are 
designated Scatter terrain. 
Scatter terrain does not provide regular cover. 
Instead, if LoS crosses one or more pieces of 
Scatter terrain, a die roll of exactly the score 
needed to hit the target indicates that the 
Scatter terrain has taken the hit: Move it from 
play; the shot has no other effect.
If Scatter terrain is within the affected area 
of an Area or Stream weapon, roll 1D6 
per terrain piece with a 6 indicating it was 
destroyed. This will absorb the effect of the 
attack if a figure is on the other side. (Apply 
common sense here.)
Rolling to Hit
For each shot on the weapon profile, roll 1D6 
and add the Combat Skill of the shooter, along 
with any modifiers that might apply. If the roll 
is equal to or above the target number in the 
table below, you have scored a hit; otherwise, 
the shot is a miss. Misses have no effect.
Please note that it is possible for a shot to have 
no chance of missing (or no chance of hitting)  
if the modifiers get sufficiently large. 
Target is within 6” and in the open	
3+
Target is in the open	
5+
Target has the benefit of Cover	
6+
RESOLVING A HIT
Armor
Some characters and creatures have armor 
either due to natural protection such as scales 
or due to wearing body armor. This gives them 
additional protection from weapon strikes and 
animal claws. 
Characters with armor receive a Saving Throw 
listed as a dice target number such as 5+ or 
6+. Each time that character is hit for any 
reason, roll 1D6:
 
◆A roll equal to or above the Saving Throw 
number negates the hit: The character does 
not have to roll against their Toughness 
value and is not Stunned.
 
◆A roll below the Saving Throw has no effect: 
Either the attack broke through the armor or 
struck an unprotected area.
Some attacks may state that they ignore Saving 
Throws outright. 
In the event a character has access to more 
than one Saving Throw, do not roll separately: 
Instead, they receive a single combined Saving 
Throw that is one step better than the single 
lowest Saving Throw. For example, a character 
with a 5+ save and two 6+ saves would receive 
a single combined 4+ Saving Throw.
Trooper Armor and Bots
All characters of the trooper class wear armor, 
giving them a 5+ Saving Throw.
As bots are made of metal, they receive a  
6+ Saving Throw.
Other character types including scouts, 
scientists, civvies, and grunts do not receive a 
Saving Throw unless a rule or item grants one. 
Rolling for Damage
For each shot that hits, roll 1D6 for damage, 
adding the weapon’s Damage rating to  
the roll.
 
◆If the roll is above the Toughness of the 
target, they become a Casualty.
 
◆If the roll is equal to the Toughness of the 
target, they are knocked Sprawling.
37
Combat Rules

=== PAGE 40 ===
 
◆If the roll is below the Toughness of the 
target, they are Stunned.
 
◆If a character takes multiple hits from a 
single attack, roll damage for each:
If the target has Kill Points (see below), they 
lose 1 KP for each roll above Toughness.
Otherwise, apply the single highest result. 
Casualties are removed from play. They are 
either badly wounded, knocked unconscious, 
or dead, and will play no further part in this 
engagement.
Stunned
If a figure is Stunned, place a marker next 
to them. A character can receive a total of 3 
Stun markers at a time. Ignore any additional 
markers. When the character activates next, 
they can either move OR take an action/attack 
but cannot do both. (5PFH players should 
note that characters are not knocked out upon 
receiving a third Stun marker.)
Events in the game such as being struck by 
attacks will result in a character being Stunned. 
A Stunned character is at a penalty if engaged 
in brawling combat. 
When the character activates, they may Move 
OR perform a single action, but not both. 
Stunned Troopers cannot use their special 
ability to perform actions in both phases.
Enemies may Move OR attack while Stunned.
After a figure completes its activation, a single 
Stun marker is removed from the figure. 
The Aid action can be used to allow an ally 
within 3” to remove a Stun marker.
Stunned enemies will move to cover or out of 
sight if they have no ranged attack or are not 
in range, otherwise they will always choose to 
remain in place and fire on you. 
Sprawling
Sprawling figures can be marked with a 
suitable counter or laid down on the table. 
If a character has been knocked Sprawling, 
they can perform no actions of any kind next 
activation except to regain their footing and 
bearings when they activate. After their next 
activation, they are recovered.
If a character moves into base contact and 
spends their action helping them up, the 
Sprawling character can take an action upon 
their own activation (but cannot Move).
A Sprawling character can be moved through 
(stepped over) by allies but not enemies. 
You cannot end a move overlapping the 
Sprawling character.
While Sprawling, a character foregoes any 
Saving Throw they would receive and their 
Toughness is considered 1 point lower.
Kill Points (KP)
Many creatures are too powerful to be brought 
down by a single burst of fire. Likewise, the 
most experienced and battle-hardened heroes 
often have a knack for surviving unlikely 
events. We represent this using Kill Points 
(KP). If a creature has KP listed in its profile, 
each damage roll that exceeds Toughness 
will remove 1 KP. Once all KP are gone, any 
additional hit that exceeds Toughness will 
result in a Casualty just as it does normally.
Note: Hits that remove KP do not cause a Stun. 
We recommend tracking Kill Points with tokens 
or small dice placed with the appropriate 
number showing next to the figure. Some 
players prefer tracking the current KP while 
others prefer to track damage sustained. 
Five Parsecs: Tactics Note
The Tactics rulebook assigns all figures a KP 
value. When converting creatures from Tactics to 
Planetfall, reduce KP by 1. Increase by 1 when 
converting the other way.
If converting Planetfall creatures to Five Parsecs 
from Home, use the KP rules as described here.
Combat Rules
38

=== PAGE 41 ===
Brawling
When a moving character makes contact with 
an opponent, a Brawl immediately occurs 
between them. This uses up the moving 
character’s action. You CANNOT enter Brawling 
combat unless you have an action available.
Each character rolls 1D6 and adds their 
Combat Skill. 
 
◆All Lifeforms and any character armed with 
a Melee weapon may add a +2 weapon 
modifier. 
 
◆Characters without a Melee weapon, 
but armed with a Pistol may add a +1 
weapon modifier.
Compare the scores: The character with the 
highest modified score wins and scores a 
single hit on their opponent. On a draw, both 
characters take a hit. This can result in one or 
both characters becoming casualties. 
 
◆If the die roll of either character is a  
natural 6, they have succeeded in a feint 
and inflict a bonus hit on their opponent. 
 
◆If the die roll of either character is a  
natural 1, they have fumbled, and their 
opponent inflicts a bonus hit on them.
Bonus hits are in addition to any outcomes of 
the brawl itself. A character may end up being 
hit multiple times because of this rule.
If a character is armed with a Pistol or Melee 
weapon and claimed the brawling die roll 
bonus for that weapon, they will inflict any hits 
with the Damage value of the selected weapon. 
A character lacking any suitable weapon 
strikes at +0 Damage (using the stock of their 
weapon, a pocketknife, or a big rock).
See page 37 for how to resolve hits. 
Melee Weapons
Melee weapons are not part of the normal 
colonist equipment, though you may obtain 
them during the campaign. Imported 
characters may also carry some.
Knock Back
Any character taking a hit in brawling combat 
but surviving is knocked back 1” for each 
hit taken. This knock back is always directly 
away from their opponent and can result in 
characters falling off things. 
A character that is knocked back more than 1”  
is also Knocked Sprawling (see page 38).
Exploitation Move
A character that eliminates their opponent in 
brawling combat may move 2” in any direction. 
Humanoid characters cannot enter into a new 
brawl using this move, but Lifeforms can and 
will fight immediately if they do (though see 
the Multiple opponents rule below).
Bad Position
If a character is Stunned when they become 
engaged in a brawl, remove all Stun markers. 
The opponent receives a +1 bonus for each 
marker that was removed. 
A Sprawling character counts as having a 
Combat Skill of +0 and cannot claim any 
weapon bonuses on the roll. In effect, they 
only ever roll 1D6+0 when attempting to win. 
If they survive, they immediately regain their 
footing in addition to inflicting hits normally. 
Multiple Opponents
In some cases, a character may end up fighting 
multiple battles in the same phase. Each 
subsequent opponent gets a cumulative 
+1 Combat Skill bonus to their roll.
This bonus resets when the specific phase ends. 
For example, a Lifeform fighting one of your 
characters in the Enemy Actions Phase and then 
fighting again in the Slow Actions Phase does 
not receive the bonus. 
39
Combat Rules

=== PAGE 42 ===
Panic
The player’s exploration teams are exceptional 
individuals, not subject to morale tests during 
battle. They will fight or retreat as the player 
wishes. Preserving your forces to fight another 
day is a vital to successful campaign. 
Player characters may abandon a battle by 
moving into base contact with any battlefield 
edge. The character is removed from the battle 
and cannot return for the duration of it. 
Tactical enemies are subject to Panic tests 
when they take casualties. The sight of their 
comrades pierced by lasers or riddled with 
bullets tends to dampen their bloodlust, at 
least temporarily.
Unless the enemy is indicated as being 
Fearless, each time an enemy is removed 
as a casualty, make a D6 roll immediately 
afterwards. If the roll falls within the Panic 
range of the unit (usually 1, 1-2, or 1-3), one 
enemy figure within 8” of the casualty is 
removed from play as they flee the battlefield. 
If there are multiple eligible figures, remove 
the one furthest away from the player’s 
battlefield edge. 
The Slyn are not subject to Panic tests, using 
their own system to determine when they 
abandon a battle. This is explained in the 
encounter rules for the Slyn (see page 152). 
Lifeforms and the robotic Sleepers do not take 
Panic tests of any kind. 
Aid
Characters taking the Aid action may grant an 
Aid marker to a comrade. These can be used in 
three ways:
 
◆If a character is attacked, they may apply a -1 
penalty to a single enemy hit roll.
 
◆During their own activation, the character 
may add +1 to a single hit roll, brawling roll, 
or Savvy roll.
 
◆If struck by a hazard, trap or environmental 
concern, the character may add +1 to a 
single Savvy test or Saving Throw.
The Aid must be used before the roll is made 
and the marker only grants a +1 to a single 
roll. It is removed after use, even if the +1 did 
not help.
Sec-Ops Lifeform Audit
Kensington Reach – File 098 [Closed]
“Lifeforms in the Kensington Reach exhibit many 
strange features not found on typical colony-
approved worlds. Many are highly peculiar and 
relentlessly aggressive. We forecast that, over 
time, many could be domesticated or relocated 
to enable the colony to progress safely but, in the 
months after planetfall, wilderness expeditions 
will be fraught with hazards. The new Type 4 labs 
should allow on-site weapons R&D to help the 
colony adjust their weapons to cope with the 
local lifeform strengths and weaknesses. 
Many lifeforms surveyed show clear signs of 
genetic engineering. They may have been 
produced specifically to be aggressive or 
to enhance their abilities, though all have 
progressed to a stage where they function in a 
stable population.
Of particular concern, the presence of Humans 
appears to both enrage and entice many of the 
more dangerous species. An expedition must 
move quickly before a large group of  
creatures converges on their position, and 
colonies must improve defenses as a priority. 
While none of the lifeforms seen may be fully 
sapient, many exhibit high intelligence, often to 
the level of mammals found elsewhere. This type 
of enhanced lifeform must have been seeded for 
some purpose. Colony science teams should be 
directed to include this in their analysis.”
Combat Rules
40

=== PAGE 43 ===
Contacts
During battle you will frequently be uncertain 
of the number and type of enemy forces. 
Your characters are equipped with scanning 
devices capable of measuring sub-psionic 
molecule distortion (far more advanced than 
the primitive “motion trackers” used in the 
22nd century), allowing them to detect likely 
lifeforms in the vicinity. These can accurately 
identify the presence of the Slyn and other 
sentient creatures, but the wildlife of this world 
tends to make accurate scanning difficult.
These suspected lifeforms are represented by 
Contact markers.
Contact Activations
If any Contacts are present, they move at the 
end of the Enemy Phase after all revealed 
enemies have acted. Contacts are activated 
in order, starting from the one closest to the 
player’s battlefield edge and working your 
way backwards. 
Contact Movement
When a Contact activates, roll two differently 
colored D6 (we suggest using red and white). 
The first die (red) is the Aggression score. The 
second die (white) is the Random score. Both 
scores are used only for this activation and are 
not tracked.
 
◆If the Aggression score is highest, the 
Contact will move towards the nearest 
member of your squad. It moves a number 
of inches equal to the Aggression die.
 
◆If the Random score is highest, the 
Contact moves in a randomly selected 
direction. It moves a number of inches 
equal to the Random die. This movement 
is not affected by terrain penalties, but 
Contacts cannot cross terrain that would 
otherwise be Impassable. Instead, they 
will move around it using the shortest 
route possible.
41
Combat Rules

=== PAGE 44 ===
 
◆Contacts that make base contact with the 
edge of the table or which would move off 
the edge will instead halt in place.
 
◆If the dice are equal, the Contact remains 
in place. If there are only 1 or 2 Contacts 
currently on the table, place a new Contact 
adjacent to it. 
Contacts and Other Interactions
The Enemy ignores Contacts for all intents  
and purposes. 
Contacts do not block movement for Enemy 
figures or other Contacts and they can move 
through each other freely. If two Contacts would 
end the Phase overlapping, move one of them 
slightly until they no longer overlap. 
Contacts cannot be attacked in any way, are not 
subject to any environmental hazards and are 
not affected by Area weapons of any kind.
Contact Detection
Contacts are detected under two circumstances:
If, during the movement of any player 
character or Contact, a clear Line of Sight with 
no intervening terrain exists between a squad 
member and the Contact. In this case, the 
Contact is detected automatically. This occurs at 
the earliest possible point of the move. 
At the end of each Enemy Phase, all Contacts 
that are in Line of Sight of squad members but 
are partially obscured by terrain must test for 
detection. You can test in any order you like. 
Each obscured Contact is detected on a D6 
roll of 4+. Note that only a single roll is made 
for each Contact regardless of the number of 
characters within Line of Sight. 
If a Contact is detected, proceed to the Contact 
Reveal section below. 
Contact Reveal
When a Contact is revealed, roll 1D6 to 
determine what you have just encountered.
The Contact marker is removed, and any 
revealed figures are placed where the marker 
was. If more than one figure is placed, the rest 
are placed as you see fit within 1” and in cover, 
if possible. 
Revealed enemies always act during the NEXT 
Enemy Phase you come to. This means if they 
are revealed during an Enemy Phase, they 
cannot act during the current Phase.
If a Contact is revealed while moving, the 
revealed figures are halted in place and do not 
complete the move. 
As Contacts revealed in the Quick Actions Phase 
would act right after you are done with that 
phase, you are encouraged to think carefully 
about when to move to get a ‘blip’ in sight.

The Slyn
Sec-Ops Lifeform Audit [Black Clearance Only]
Kensington Reach – File 103 [Closed]
There have been reports from some Unity 
administrators of hostile organisms known as 
the Slyn. Unity troopers have been reported to 
nickname them as “Raptors”, but this may be a 
confusing title given their reported abilities. 
5th Survey Group Black Clearance on the Slyn 
details a reptilian species not unlike wingless 
Swift. Tracing similar reports from other 
surveys, they appear to have originated in the 
periphery of the galaxy some two million years 
ago. The species is remarkable in its social 
cohesion but has exhibited limited interest in 
peaceful interaction, reacting with considerable 
hostility to any perceived incursions into their 
current territories. Ancient records from other 
civilizations are unreliable but, according to the 
Precursors, there is some data that, at intervals 
of roughly 3000 years, the entire culture 
opts to migrate, sending the Slyn across the 
stars in the search for a new sector of space to 
inhabit. K’Erin records show that during these 
migrations they abandon their old domain. The 
reason for these migrations is unknown, as the 
Slyn appear to have been good stewards of the 
worlds they inhabit, and resource depletions 
have not been witnessed.
While their remote position has generally 
allowed these migrations to occur peacefully, 
both Unity and the K’Erin have recently 
fought engagements with Slyn scouts to 
prevent incursions. Note these  
engagements have been classified 
Black Clearance. Currently the Slyn seem to 
be scouting near the Kensington Reach, their 
normal level of hostility greatly enhanced 
by some factor. The 2nd Fringe and 
27th Reserve Fleet Tactical 
Commands have been 
made aware of relevant 
information to help combat 
these incursions. 
The Precursors also noted that the Slyn normally 
exhibit a degree of natural psionic capability. 
It is theorized that the dampening fields in the 
Reach infuriates them, but all attempts to reach 
a negotiated settlement have so far been met 
with very aggressive responses.
Bio-Ops have noted from reports that the Slyn 
operate in pairs, with two warriors moving and 
acting as a cohesive unit, possibly in a mental 
link with each other. Whether this is strictly a 
tactical measure or has a deeper social function 
is unknown.
It is known that the Slyn will send out small 
scout ships to worlds throughout the Reach. 
Many of these scouts resemble raiding parties 
and they will attack Humans or other sapients 
on sight, unless completely overmatched. 
Unity troop details have been increased to 
provide protection but, so far, the Bureaucracy 
has not decided on a long-term plan. Initial 
conflicts have proved standard weapons 
inefficient, and colony administrators should 
report any R&D weapon developments that 
prove successful. 
43
Combat Rules

=== PAGE 46 ===
The Initial Missions
Before the campaign begins proper, you need 
to establish the landing site. Do so by playing 
through the following three missions, one for 
each of the specialties on your roster. You may 
play these missions in any order, though the 
recommended one is given below:
 
◆Beacons (Scout Mission)
 
◆Analysis (Scientist Mission)
 
◆Perimeter (Trooper Mission)
These missions will introduce you to the 
various elements of the game systems and 
combat mechanics. Once all three have been 
completed, proceed to Campaign Setup 
(see page 52).
Each mission is played on a 2x2-foot table, with 
terrain placed around the table in any manner 
you see fit.
Casualties during the initial missions do not 
require injury rolls. 
Beacons (Scout Mission)
The first to land are the scouts, establishing the 
beacons required for the rest of the colonization 
fleet to begin deploying. The scouts must brave 
terrible storms to complete their mission.
To play this mission you will need to learn how 
the Battle Round Sequence (page 28) and 
Movement rules work (page 34). 
 
◆Place 3 Beacon locations on any 
raised ground on the table. If you 
have no raised ground, place these 
locations in terrain features of your choice. 
 
◆Randomly determine which battlefield edge 
you will set up near and deploy any scouts 
from your roster within 1”.
Objective
The scouts must move to each of the Beacon 
locations. When a scout ends an activation 
on the location, a Beacon has been deployed. 
Once a Beacon is deployed on each location, 
the mission is completed.
Obstacles
At the start of each Enemy Phase, a 
storm cluster spawns at the center 
of the table unless there are currently 
5 storm clusters on the table (use markers for 
the center of these). Each storm cluster moves 
1D6” in a random direction during the Enemy 
Phase. Any that leave the table are removed. 
A scout that is contacted by a storm cluster as it 
moves takes a +0 Damage hit. 
Reward
If you place all 3 Beacons successfully, the 
mission ends immediately and when you 
establish your colony, you receive 2 units of 
Raw Materials as a bonus.
If the mission is a failure, you do not receive 
the bonus but may still proceed to the next 
mission, as a second wave of scouts succeeds.
PLANETFALL
Planetfall
44

=== PAGE 47 ===
Analysis (Scientist Mission)
The second landing is by the Science Team, 
establishing the parameters of the mission site 
and conducting detailed scientific scans. 
You will need to read the Contact rules to play 
this mission (see page 41).
 
◆Place 6 Contact markers fairly 
evenly around the table, then 
randomly select a battlefield edge.
 
◆Deploy your scientist characters within 1” 
of the selected battlefield edge. 
Objective
You must uncover at least 4 Contacts to 
complete the mission. Contacts that leave 
the table count against you, unlike in regular 
missions. Note that in this mission, revealing a 
Contact simply removes it. You do not have to 
roll on the Contact Reveal table.
Reward
If you successfully complete the mission, you 
will begin the campaign with 2 Research 
Points as a bonus (increased to 3 if you reveal 
all 6 Contacts). 
If the mission is a failure, you do not receive 
the reward as your initial scans are not 
sufficiently detailed, but you may progress 
to the next mission.
Perimeter (Trooper Mission)
The third landing is by the troopers of the 
team, establishing a defensive perimeter as 
hostile Lifeforms are rapidly headed towards 
your drop site.
You will need to read all the rules for combat to 
play this mission.
 
◆Set up any troopers from your roster. Each is 
armed with the standard trooper rifle from 
the Armory (see page 77). You may set up 
anywhere on the table you like.
 
◆After setting up, place 6 Lifeform miniatures 
(you can use anything you like, but insects 
and lizards will tend to look good) along 
a randomly selected battlefield edge with 
their figures placed within 1” of it.
Objective
You must kill all 6 to complete the mission.
Obstacles
The Lifeforms move 5” per action, have a +1 
Combat Skill and Toughness 4. They will 
move as directly as possible to attack you in 
brawling combat and will strike with  
+0 Damage if they do.
They are not subject to Panic tests.
Reward
Killing all 6 Lifeforms earns you +3 Colony 
Morale to begin the campaign (record this 
on your Colony Tracking Sheet). If you 
fail the mission, your troopers are 
evacuated, and a squad of grunts 
clears up the site. You may proceed to 
Campaign Setup in both cases  
(see page 52).
45

The Promise
Executive Briefing [Excerpt]
Unity Survey Sector KS092345
“Along the far edges of Unity-controlled 
space lie several sectors known officially as 
the Kensington Reach and unofficially as The 
Promise. This region is noted for having a 
particularly high concentration of habitable 
worlds, many of which already contain lifeforms, 
though no sapient inhabitants. 
Initial planetary surveys conducted by the  
5th Survey Group revealed rich mineral wealth 
and moderate climates suitable for habitation. 
Reports from early scout landings found signs  
of extensive prior terraforming, which appears 
to have been abandoned at various points 
along the process thousands of years ago.
Orbital scans indicate that the surfaces of 
multiple worlds have a high density of ruins and 
structures, some protruding from the ground 
and others hovering over the surface through 
as-yet unidentified means. Recommendations 
are for colony expeditions to handle on-the-
ground research to free up 5th Survey Group to 
continue scans of the Kensington Reach.
Precursor psi-veyers from P1 Division have 
noted that the worlds appear to be psionically 
dampened by unseen means. Not only do 
the worlds not appear to long-range psionic 
detection, but they are shrouded in a nullifying 
field which prevents many psionic abilities. 
Interestingly, some species and lifeforms can 
function normally despite this dampening, 
while it appears to inhibit most typical 
Precursor-derived psionic disciplines. (Note for 
Executive: this forms the basis for most psionics 
currently tracked in the galactic community.) 
Significant Unity fleet assets from the 2nd 
Fringe Fleet and 27th Reserve Fleet were 
deployed to provide a Stage 2 Cordon for the 
region interdicting major routes. Note that 
a Stage 4 interdiction would require at least 
70% of current Fleet Assets. It is recommended 
to advance the Magenta Colonization Plan 
to provide localized presence and protection 
of the most valuable assets. The 2nd Fringe 
Fleet reports long range K’Erin conquest 
and settlement attempts, with bloody but 
ultimately decisive skirmishes across the 
sectors and Unity success in all cases. Most 
galactic powers have been allowed to operate 
at least one research station or scientific facility 
within The Promise to aid our efforts under the 
Neptune 30 Treaty, but colonization is strictly 
limited to our Unity program. Despite this, 
continued land rights negotiations by Precursor 
and K’Erin ultra-corporations have been 
presented, although the DiploCorp forecast 
that this will take at least 3 Core years to allow 
fast-tracking time of colonization.  
Initial Colonization has been slow, with 
permissions granted only after careful 
consideration by the Colonial Settlement 
Bureau. Our recommendation is to fast-track all 
Unity-approved organizations to maximize our 
ownership of vital assets. It is recommended 
that settlement within The Promise be shown 
to be a high-prestige project with the colonies 
intended to act as ideal showcases in order to 
increase applications. This presents some risk 
given the known dangers of technologies and 
lifeforms, but UnityComms are confident this 
can be managed in public media.
Several special authorizations have been issued, 
allowing colonies to be established in the 
marked sectors in your report which until now 
have been considered medium- and even high-
risk. We have provided a list of capable crews 
who have been identified in Schedule 37C. 
Whilst they would normally not be considered 
for official Unity sanctioned programs, these 
crews have been noted to be very successful in 
the dangerous fringe. With the right support 
and controls, we feel they will be suitable 
candidates for these risky missions and will also 
act as deniable assets should these worlds prove 
to be too dangerous.”

=== PAGE 50 ===
The Campaign
48
Beginning your Campaign
Once you have introduced yourself to the 
tactical game rules in the Planetfall missions 
(page 44), you are ready to start the main 
campaign game. 
You don’t have to prepare your next battlefield 
just yet, but if you don’t have them to hand 
already, make sure you have:
 
◆pencils, erasers, dice (of course)... and a nice 
mug of tea or other drink of choice 
 
◆copies of the Colony Tracking Sheet
 
◆a notebook (analog or digital) for note taking 
and various incidental record keeping; and
 
◆a method for creating a map, whether 
drawing freehand or using a digital resource. 
Campaign Overview
Campaigns are played as a series of campaign 
turns (or simply “turns”). During each turn 
you will play one or more tabletop missions, 
whether planned or unplanned. 
Once you are situated, your colonists will 
start exploring the world, researching new 
technology, and constructing your home 
base. Each campaign turn you will undertake 
field missions to exploit the resources of the 
planet or to carry out other missions as they 
suggest themselves. 
The campaign is ultimately self-paced. You 
will reach Milestones through construction of 
particular buildings and by achieving important 
research, eventually leading to the campaign’s 
End Game. However, you may also continue 
playing as long as you like, if you prefer to 
continue building your own story. 
Milestones also function as a sort of game 
calendar. Each time you develop a new 
Milestone, the campaign advances and events 
occur such as new foes appearing and new 
resources becoming available to you. This 
Milestone System is discussed in detail on 
page 156. 
To access the End Game, you must complete a 
total of 7 Milestones. 
Campaign Character Miniatures
You may already have miniatures you would 
like to use. If not, Miniatures are discussed in 
more detail in the Appendices, page 177. Some 
players prefer waiting until after they have 
created their characters to decide on this.
The 
Campaign

=== PAGE 51 ===
49
The Campaign
Hostile Forces
The manpower you have to draw upon is 
limited, making it increasingly difficult to 
replace losses as the campaign goes on. The 
core worlds will support you but will demand 
progress to grant additional support (in the 
form of Milestone achievements) so you 
must balance the desire to improve your 
colony with the desire (or need) to progress 
in the campaign.
As you play the game, you will encounter three 
types of hostile forces:
 
◆Animal Lifeforms that are native to the 
world. You will generate the exact types of 
animals randomly, and will often have to 
contend with hostile lifeforms in the field. 
 
◆Tactical Enemies. Ranging from raiders to 
saboteurs, these are forces trying to attack or 
destroy your colony. You will engage them in 
tactical battles. As the campaign progresses, 
you will gradually eliminate these threats.
 
◆The Slyn. These enigmatic, highly aggressive 
and hostile alien species are intent on 
attacking your colony. Their motivations are 
difficult to discern, and your only hope is to 
fend them off long enough for the colony to 
achieve its destiny. 
If the campaign seems overwhelming, don’t 
worry, completely messing up is difficult. Take 
a look at the options you have available to you 
at any given time and pick one that sounds 
interesting, fun, or which seems to pertain 
to whatever you are hoping to achieve at this 
particular moment. As you take campaign turns 
and develop a feeling for the rhythm of the 
game, you will start to form your own goals and 
personal objectives.
Animal Lifeforms
In other 5X games, the term ‘lifeform’ has been 
used in the wider sense, covering intelligences 
from animal up to spacefaring species. In the 
context of Planetfall, ‘Lifeforms’ – capital L – is  
used only as the context of animal lifeforms  
and non-civilized native species.

=== PAGE 52 ===
The Campaign
50
Tactical Enemies
Over the course of the campaign, hostile 
forces will emerge on the world, whether from 
internal rebellion or external invaders, seeking 
to damage and pillage your colony.
In total you will have to contend with three 
such groups of what we call Tactical Enemies. 
They are distinguished from the various 
lifeforms you encounter by being sapient 
humanoids who (mostly) fight with weapons 
similar to a military force. These groups are 
detailed as part of the Enemy Generation 
chapter, page 146.
While you will have to undertake military 
duties, this is not a grand tactical campaign: 
you will not be asked to maneuver battalions 
around a war-torn battlefield. These groups 
rarely have the strength to coordinate a full-on 
assault, but will present a nuisance as the 
campaign continues. You will need to decisively 
defeat them in the end to secure the colony.
The enemy is not present on the campaign 
map until you complete the campaign’s first 
Milestone. An enemy will emerge on the map 
the first three times you complete a Milestone.
As the enemy is stronger than you, fighting 
mile after mile is not viable. Instead, you will 
have to track down and destroy their leadership 
with a daring strike to win the day.
Enemy Occupied Sectors
Initially, each enemy occupies a single sector of 
the campaign map, selected at random when 
they arrive in the campaign (see page 52).  
Occupied sectors are areas that the enemy 
can control by patrolling it. Any colonists 
attempting to explore or exploit the region 
would be captured or killed, making the area 
inaccessible to your personnel.
Each enemy may spread as a result of random 
events, occupying different or additional 
sectors. A sector that is occupied by the enemy 
is inaccessible: You cannot carry out Exploration 
or Delve missions there, for example. 
Resources and Ancient Sites do not disappear 
when occupied by the enemy, they are just not 
available to you until the sector is cleared out.

=== PAGE 53 ===
51
The Campaign
Taking the Fight to the Enemy
To defeat an enemy, you need to track down 
their Strongpoint and to do this, you need 
to find the relevant data by locating a Boss. 
The tracking of Bosses is done by a system of 
Enemy Information.
Obtaining Enemy Information
Enemy Information represents data that can 
help you in obtaining information on one 
of the Enemies in the campaign. This might 
be documents showing their hide outs and 
camps, intercepted communications, computer 
predictions run by the nerds back at base, a 
map dropped by one of the enemy grunts, or 
any number of other sources. 
Whatever the source, Enemy Information 
will help you determine where an enemy 
Boss can be located, and gathering Enemy 
Information is required to progress against a 
particular enemy.
Enemy Information are always specific to a 
particular enemy in the campaign and cannot 
be transferred. 
Enemy Information is obtained in the 
following ways:
 
◆You automatically obtain 1 Enemy 
Information each time you fight an 
enemy and win. 
 
◆Your scouts can earn Enemy Information 
by taking Scout actions.
Once you have obtained 6 Enemy 
Information, there is no need to get any more: 
You have gained a complete insight into the 
communications, habits, and plans of that 
particular enemy.
Hunting Down a Boss
To defeat the enemy, the first step is to find 
and capture a Boss. This requires a successful 
Enemy Information roll (see pages 60-61) 
and then carrying out a Strike Mission  
(page 127).
If you defeat the Boss, a search of their 
possessions will determine the location of the 
Enemy Strongpoint. This is placed in a random 
sector occupied by the enemy in question. 
The Strongpoint Attack
Once the Strongpoint is known to you, you 
may opt to carry out an Assault Mission at a 
moment of your choosing. Succeeding in the 
Assault destroys that enemy and removes them 
from the campaign. 
If the mission fails, the Strongpoint remains in 
place. Note that it will remain at full strength 
each time you attack, so make sure you are 
kitted out for a tough fight. 
The enemy will respond to a failed attack: 
Randomly select an unoccupied map sector 
adjacent to the Strongpoint which becomes 
occupied by that Tactical Enemy immediately.
The Enemy in Retreat
If a Tactical Enemy is defeated at their 
Strongpoint, all occupied sectors are 
abandoned immediately. That enemy will no 
longer return to bother you. 
You may immediately increase Colony Morale 
by +5.
Supremacy
You are not required to destroy all three 
enemies to complete the campaign. The End 
Game will require you to defeat some, however. 
If you are pursuing the Ascension, Isolation, or 
Independence endings you must have defeated 
at least one, while the Loyalty ending requires 
defeating at least two.
Consult page 160 for details on End Game 
mechanics.

=== PAGE 54 ===
The Campaign
52
Campaign Setup
Determining Your Colonization 
Agenda
Roll D100 on the Expedition Type table to 
determine why you are out here to begin with. 
Creating Your Map
Your map is the area where the campaign takes 
place. It cannot encompass the entire planet 
(even our own Moon has a surface area of some 
38 million square kilometers).
Instead, your map will depict the area near the 
site your colony touched down. The exact scale 
is dependent on your campaign intentions.  
A campaign depicting a smaller colony might 
only deal with an area 20-30 miles in every 
direction, while a campaign depicting a huge 
settlement might extend for hundreds of miles. 
Regardless of scale, keep track of the local area 
with a pen-and-paper map (or similar).  
It should be divided into a square grid. If you 
use computer software to draw up a game map, 
odds are it will have an option to produce a 
grid, but otherwise one can be added easily. 
Each square is known as a Sector in the game.
The default is a 6 x 6 grid, giving 36 sectors  
in total. If you want a larger map with more 
space to play on, you can make the grid 6 x 10 
(60 sectors) or even 10 x 10 (100 sectors).
The grid can have as many sectors as you 
like, but the rows and columns should be 
numbered, allowing you to select random 
sectors through dice rolls. For example, if your 
grid is 8 x 10, you would roll a D8 and D10: 
A 3 and a 7 would mean row 3 and column 7. 
If you need to pick a random sector adjacent to 
an existing one a D8 can be rolled. The top left 
adjacent square is numbered 1 and you then 
just count clockwise around the target.

=== PAGE 55 ===
53
The Campaign
Travel on the Map
Note that there are no travel rules in the 
game. As your colonists are well supplied with 
vehicles, they can always reach any destination 
on the map regardless of intervening terrain.
Initial Placements
Pick a home sector (or roll randomly) to 
determine where your colony starts out. The 
colony always takes up a single sector and all 
buildings you construct will fit within the same 
sector. Mark it with a capital ‘H’ or a star.
Next, mark 10 sectors on the map as 
Investigation Sites. You can write a capital 
“I” or “?” in those sectors or use a map symbol 
of your own creation. Determine the sectors 
randomly, but if you roll a sector that already 
has been selected, pick any empty sector 
adjacent instead.
Initial scans returned these sites as particularly 
noteworthy for exploration. Many show signs 
of strange but ancient construction, unusual 
bio-signals, or evidence of high concentrations 
of valuable resources.
All other map sectors are simply “unexplored” 
for now. 
Recording Map Factors
As you play the campaign, the map will start to 
fill up with information:
Resource Level
This value determines how likely it 
is that mineral wealth will be found 
in the area. Write this into a sector 
before the Hazard level.
Hazard Level
This value determines how 
dangerous the area is in terms of 
wildlife and natural hazards. Write 
this into a sector after the Resource level. 
Resource and Hazard Levels are discussed 
further on page 60.
Enemy Occupation
If the sector is under enemy control, 
this prevents you from carrying out 
any other missions in the area. Mark 
the sector in an appropriate manner such as 
using a specific color or symbol for each enemy.
Enemy Strongpoint
Once located, an enemy Strongpoint 
represents the headquarters and 
hideout of a particular enemy type.  
It must be assaulted and destroyed to put 
an end to that threat. Mark a Strongpoint by 
shading its sector in the appropriate color or 
marking it with an “S”.
Strongpoints are discussed further on page 51.
Ancient Signs
Scans and scout reports suggest that 
something unusual is located here. 
An Ancient Sign may turn out to be 
alien ruins, a strange natural feature, or similar. 
It can be investigated, provided the area has 
already been explored and cleared of enemies.
Mark this sector with an “A”.
Ancient Signs are discussed further on 
page 61.

=== PAGE 56 ===
The Campaign
54
Map Terrain
The map will also need to be populated with 
terrain. This can be broken out into broad 
types but should be interesting to look at, with 
mountain ranges and forests to explore. You 
may even have a map available that you would 
like to use such as a real-world map or a map 
from a science fiction world. 
During the campaign, the map serves to help 
inform your choice of terrain features when 
playing a game. If a mission takes place in a 
forested area, you should have lots of forest 
features and so on. It is helpful to try to focus 
the map on the type of terrain features you 
already have available in your collection, 
though you may enjoy a challenge or an excuse 
to build more terrain.
Investigation, Exploration,  
and Exploitation
Investigation sites can be subjected to an 
Investigation Mission during the campaign. 
This removes the Investigation marker and, 
as part of the campaign, allows your scouts 
to determine the Resource and Hazard levels 
of the sector. The sector’s status becomes 
Explored and eligible for an Exploration 
Mission.
Exploration Missions will reduce the 
Resource levels of a sector. Once Resources 
reach 0, the sector’s status changes 
to Exploited and, unless an event 
repopulates it, it will not furnish 
additional rewards.
To find out more about Exploration 
Missions, see page 117.

=== PAGE 57 ===
55
The Campaign
Other Colony Statistics
Colony Morale
Your Colony Morale score measures how well 
the colonists think you are doing. At the start 
of the campaign, it is set at 0 and can go both 
up and down. There is no limit to how high 
Morale can go. 
Any time Morale drops to -10 or lower, set 
Colony Morale back to 0, but roll for a Morale 
Incident.
For more on Morale, see page 89.
Campaign Milestones
The campaign rules use Milestones to 
determine your progress. These milestones are 
attained by achieving certain factors of scientific 
and colony development and are self-paced. 
If you prefer a more competitive experience, 
track how many campaign turns it takes you 
to complete the campaign game by achieving 
every Milestone.
If you prefer a more story-oriented experience, 
you may not worry about the Milestones much 
or at all. If you prefer to just play to see what 
happens, they are not mandatory. 
For more on Milestones, see page 156.
Colony Capabilities
Your colony is measured in a number of 
different abilities and capabilities, tracked 
on your Colony Tracking Sheet. If you are just 
starting your campaign, the default values are:
 
◆Initial Colony Roster size is 8.
 
◆Build Points per turn is 1.
 
◆Research Points per turn is 1.
 
◆Building Repair Capacity is 1.
 
◆Colony Defenses are 0. 
 
◆Colony Integrity is 0. 
Campaign Resources
As you play the campaign, you will obtain 
various resources representing raw materials, 
strange animal, plant, and mineral samples, 
and other data. Once sourced, resources can 
be saved up as long as you like. There is no 
maximum to the number of resources of any 
type that you can have stored at a given time.
You begin with the following resources set to 0: 
Raw Materials
These can be processed into Build Points for 
construction purposes or be used to repair 
damage to the colony. Up to 3 units of Raw 
Materials can be used for each of these 
purposes per campaign turn. 
For more on Raw Materials, see page 88.
Research Points (RP)
These represent scientific finds that can be 
used to develop new weapons, equipment, 
and medical cures. They are used to 
conduct research.
For more on Research, see page 91.
Build Points (BP)
These represent ready-to-use materials that 
either have already been processed or which 
require minimal processing. They are used to 
construct new buildings.
For more on Buildings, see page 97.
Augmentation Points (AP)
These represent the degree to which your 
colonists can adapt their biology to the 
new environment. Use AP to purchase 
Augmentations.
For more on Genetic Augmentation,  
see page 105.

=== PAGE 58 ===
The Campaign
56
Story Points
A randomly generated adventure means 
experiencing the unpredictable, with various 
tables and events coming together to form an 
amazing and exciting story. However, there is 
always the threat that something irrational and 
irritating gets generated when you know just 
what would make total sense. 
Of course, you could just change what happens. 
After all, it is your game, and you know better 
than anyone else what would be the most fun 
for you. However, some players balk at this, 
either because it feels like cheating or because 
they worry they might get carried away (the 
power of a Colony Administrator is enough, 
without becoming godlike!)
Others enjoy the challenge of the random 
environment but would appreciate just a tiny 
bit of direction and control. Story Points are 
the solution.
When your campaign begins, you typically start 
with a total of 5 Story Points – note this down 
on your Colony Tracking Sheet. (Note: this start 
number can be varied based on how easy or 
difficult you would like the campaign to be.)
If (when) a character dies, you receive  
1 Story Point as compensation, and there are 
a number of other events and functions in the 
game that can offer Story Points to represent 
the fickle hand of fate or the way heroes tend 
to make their own luck. 
Spending Story Points
A Story Point can be spent on any of the 
following benefits:
 
◆Roll again on any random events table 
and pick either result. If the event affects a 
random character or other subject, you may 
choose the character or subject.
 
◆Prevent a roll on the Enemy Actions,  
Morale Incident, or Integrity Failure tables 
from occurring.
 
◆Roll 2D6, pick the highest of the dice, and 
take any combination of Research Points, 
Build Points, or Raw Materials that add up to 
the highest die.
 
◆Ignore a roll on the post-battle injury table.
Narration
There is another way to use Story Points: To tell 
a story.
If you have an idea for something that would 
really make sense, you can pay a Story Point 
to make that happen. Whether it is a character 
customizing their gun to get a small bonus, 
developing a special skill to reflect how much 
they hate the Slyn after almost dying in battle, 
or simply having a specific event occur, the 
choice is up to you. 
You will want to keep the event roughly along 
the scale of events that are already in the game, 
if you wish the game balance to remain stable. 
In many cases, you can use an existing event or 
swap one out to get the story result you want.
This option is listed separately because it is a 
little more open-ended and you will probably 
already know whether you like this option 
or whether you want to stick to the more 
mechanical options presented above. 
Scenarios
You do not have to pay Story Points to play a 
special scenario. Create fun adventures all day 
long and have a great time exploring.
In a campaign that relies heavily on custom 
scenarios or Game Mastered play, Story Points 
may have to be modified somewhat to avoid 
causing difficulties with the story. If in doubt, 
consider sticking to the mechanical effects. 
A Game Master can award Story Points if it 
seems fitting, usually at the conclusion of 
a quest or epic mission. The reward should 

=== PAGE 59 ===
57
The Campaign
rarely be more than a single point, though 
if the players have blown through all their 
resources, 2-3 points is fine. Remember, it 
is easier to offer another point later than to 
realize you were too generous and they now 
have far too many.
The Lanse-effect
If you make a change that makes life more 
difficult for yourself, such as a more dangerous 
environment or a super-tough boss monster, 
you do not have to pay for the privilege. 
Final Review
When you have everything else in place, do a 
last check that you have gotten everything right 
and that you are happy with the characters you 
have, then dive into your first campaign turn.
Proceed to page 58 to begin the adventure!
Unity Colonial Security Internal Review
Nadir-Kensington-78E
“Despite the lack of major power influence, the 
region is not devoid of sapient life. A number of 
pirate and warlord groups have sought refuge 
in the region, and raids on colonies and orbital 
platforms are increasingly common. Assets from 
the 27th Reserve fleet have been directed to 
intervene but without significant reinforcements 
this is unlikely to achieve anything but a morale 
boost to the current established colonies.
As discussed in the security briefing, all 
expeditions are accompanied by an enhanced 
Unity security team to safeguard the investment 
in the colony assets. Another key priority is to 
prevent nuclear or plasma reactors from falling 
into the hands of unsanctioned groups. If 
technologies of Grade 3 or above are discovered, 
this should be reported. 
Some adversaries are previously colonists. 
Whether they have “gone nomad”, are reacting 
to the intrusion of new arrivals, or are defectors 
from existing colonies who have turned to radical 
actions, they represent tactical challenges.  
Sec-Ops are developing plans to dissuade 
existing colonists from following the same 
course, but a more wide-ranging plan is needed 
to deal with the risk to ongoing operations.
Sec-Ops refer to all of the above as “Tactical 
Enemies”: humanoid foes that use technology 
and function in a similar manner to standard 
opponents. Success for any colony will require 
engaging these foes in open combat and 
eventually knocking out their Strongpoints to 
eliminate their presence. We are recommending 
colony administrators treat security as a priority 
during their expansion phase.”
57

=== PAGE 60 ===
The Campaign
58
Campaign Turn Sequence
The campaign game is played as a series 
of turns. Each of these is divided into the 
following steps, which you must progress 
through in order. While the number of steps 
may appear daunting at first, at its heart, each 
turn is a tactical mission with pre-battle and 
post-battle stages.
PRE-BATTLE STEPS
1.	
Recovery
2.	
Repairs
3.	
Scout reports
4.	
Enemy Actions
5.	
Colony events
6.	
Mission determination
BATTLE STEPS
7.	
Lock and Load
8.	
Play out your Mission
POST-BATTLE STEPS
9.	
Injuries
10.	 Experience progression
11.	 Colony Morale adjustments
12.	 Track Enemy Information and Mission Data
13.	 Replacement Phase
14.	 Research Phase
15.	 Building Phase
16.	 Colony Integrity Phase
17.	 Character event
18.	 Update rosters
When you complete the last step, a new 
campaign turn can begin. 
We do not match campaign turns to a specific 
time frame. If you like to keep a game diary or 
captains log, roll 2D6 to determine how many 
days pass between turns. 
Step 1: Recovery
During this step, every character in sick bay 
will heal, reducing the remaining recovery 
time by 1 turn. Some events and features may 
speed up this recovery process. 
Any character that is fully healed at this 
stage is ready for deployment during this 
campaign turn.
Sick Bay
If a character has suffered an injury that requires 
recovery time, they are in Sick Bay. They cannot 
be selected for missions either by choice or 
randomly. They can be the target of random 
events, however. If an event results in additional 
recovery time, add the times together. Sick Bay 
time is always measured in campaign turns. 
If you are playing a special scenario where a 
character in Sick Bay is involved in a tabletop 
encounter, reduce their Speed by 1” and reduce 
their Toughness and Combat Skill by -1 each 
(but not below Toughness 3 and  
+0 Combat Skill respectively). If they are 
injured again, add any recovery times together.

=== PAGE 61 ===
59
The Campaign
Step 2: Repairs
If your colony has suffered damage, you may 
restore lost Integrity at this point (see page 87). 
Your colony repair rate indicates how many 
points of damage it can restore in one 
campaign turn. For a starting colony this is 
always 1 point per campaign turn, applied 
during this step.
If you have Raw Materials, you may spend up 
to 3 units to repair damage. Each unit repairs  
1 point of colony damage.
If your colony bot is currently Broken, it is 
repaired at this stage but cannot be deployed 
into combat this turn.
Step 3: Scout Reports
Each campaign turn, your scout teams are hard 
at work exploring the world. 
During this step you may perform one Scout 
Explore action and roll once on the Scout 
Discovery table below.
If you need additional Scouting, you can also 
deploy your scouts in the field during the 
Mission Determination Step (page 64) using 
the Scouting Mission tabletop battle (page 116).
Scout Discovery table
D100 
DISCOVERY
DESCRIPTION
01-10
Routine trip
The scouts find nothing at all.
11-20
Good practice
The scouts find nothing, but it is a good opportunity to rehearse the basics. 
If a scout was assigned, they receive +2 XP.
21-25
SOS signal
The scouts report a distress signal. This campaign turn you have the choice of 
playing a Rescue Mission. If you do not, Colony Morale drops by -3. 
Note: You do NOT suffer Morale loss from casualties sustained in this mission.
26-30
Scout Down!
The scout vehicle crashes. If you assigned a scout, they need rescue. 
You can opt to have them escape on foot. If so, they must roll on the 
post-battle injury table but earn +2 XP if they survive, or you can play the 
Scout Down! mission this turn to rescue them. 
If you did not assign a scout, the crash left no survivors. 
(If you would really like to play the mission, go ahead and do so this turn.  
You get +1 Colony Morale for your dedication). 
31-60
Exploration 
report
Select any sector that has not yet been Explored and generate 
Resource and Hazard levels. 
61-70
Recon patrol
If there are Tactical Enemies on the map, select one and add an Enemy 
Information for them. If there are currently no Tactical Enemies present,  
treat this as no event.
71-80
Ancient Sign 
Randomly select a map sector, then mark that it has an Ancient Sign within it. 
Completing any mission in the sector awards the Ancient Sign.
81-00
Revised survey
Randomly pick a map sector. If it is not yet Explored, generate Resource and 
Hazard levels normally. If it is Explored but not yet Exploited, 
increase the Resource level by +1. If it has already been Exploited, 
generate new Resource and Hazard levels. You can Exploit it again.

=== PAGE 62 ===
The Campaign
60
Scout Actions
Scout Explore: Select a map sector that is not 
enemy occupied, and which has not already 
been Exploited. Roll 2D6 twice and pick the 
lowest score each time. The first score is the 
Resource Level of the sector, the second score 
is the Hazard Level of the sector. 
For example, if the first roll is 2, 5 and the 
second is 4, 4 the sector has Resource Level 2, 
Hazard Level 4.
Mark the values on the map. 
The Resource Level determines how likely you 
are to find valuable materials in that sector 
while the Hazard Level is a measure of how 
dangerous the place is. These values are used 
during Exploration Missions in the sector. 
If you roll a double 4, double 5, or double 6 
when Exploring, the sector also contains an 
Ancient Sign (see page 61).
Scout Discovery (optional action): 
Your scout teams are constantly patrolling 
the area around the colony and scanning the 
countryside. After carrying out your Scout 
Explore action, you may roll D100 on the table 
on page 59 to determine what you find.
Note that you are not required to make a Scout 
Discovery roll if you do not wish to. 
You may assign one of your scout characters 
to lead the expedition. You are not required 
to do this, and it does not prevent them from 
undertaking a mission this campaign turn. 
Scouts in Sick Bay cannot be assigned.
Process Enemy Information: To track down 
and destroy each enemy, you will have 
to collect Enemy Information as to the 
whereabouts of their leadership and hideouts. 
Enemy Information represent snippets of 
intercepted communications, trails in the 
wilderness, interrogation results from captured 
enemies, and perhaps even infiltration and 
espionage.
If you have obtained Enemy Information 
regarding the plans of an enemy, you may 
now try to piece it all together: 
For each enemy for which you have gathered 
Enemy Information, roll 1D6 and compare to 
the number of Enemy Information you have 
for that enemy. Note that Enemy Information 
is specific to a particular enemy and does not 
transfer over in any way.

=== PAGE 63 ===
61
The Campaign
If the roll is equal to or below the number of 
Enemy Information, you have tracked down 
one of their Bosses and can attempt the Strike 
Mission to capture them. If you roll higher, the 
current position is unknown.
You may roll for each enemy you have Enemy 
Information for, but you can Strike only one of 
them during a given campaign turn.
If you do not attempt the mission this 
campaign turn, it fails, or you rolled above the 
number of Enemy Information, you can try 
again in future campaign turns, rolling against 
the number of Enemy Information again and 
attempting the mission if you succeed.
Note that if you have 6 Enemy Information 
already, you have completely deciphered the 
enemy communications channels and can 
always opt to carry out the Strike. Excess Enemy 
Information is ignored.
Once you have captured a Boss, there is no 
need to collect Enemy Information for that 
enemy any longer.
Randomly select which enemy held sector the 
Boss is present in this turn.
Step 4: Enemy Activity
While you are going about your business, the 
enemy has plans of their own. These schemes 
are not always directed at you: Enemies are 
often just as busy dealing with hostile wildlife, 
Slyn raids, and each other. 
If any Tactical Enemies are present on the 
map, randomly pick one and roll D100 on the 
Enemy Activity table below. If there are none 
currently on the map, skip this step.
Disruption
If you won a battle against the selected enemy 
last turn, they do NOT carry out an activity 
unless they are the Converted. 
Ancient Signs
The world is full of the remnants of incredibly 
ancient civilizations, but to locate the Ancient 
Sites and explore them, you must first obtain 
Ancient Signs either as a result of scouting 
activities or other campaign factors. 
Each time you obtain an Ancient Sign, you may 
roll 1D6. On a roll equal to or below the number 
of Ancient Signs you have so far, you succeed in 
locating an Ancient Site, allowing you to carry out 
a Delve expedition (see page 130).
Randomly pick a map sector the Site is inside. It 
can even be within your colony (located beneath 
it in a cave complex or similar).
If a Site is located in enemy territory, you must 
drive them out first. If it is not in enemy territory, 
you can attempt the Delve as the campaign turn’s 
main mission whenever you choose.
If you roll above the number of Ancient 
Signs, you are not there yet. Keep track of the 
number of Signs, rolling again when you next 
obtain one.
Once you successfully locate a site, all Signs you 
currently have are discarded. 
Ancient Signs Everywhere!
If you receive multiple Signs in a single 
campaign turn, they are tested one at a time. 
Once you have a success, all Signs tested 
so far are discarded but you may start over 
testing any remaining Signs to find the next 
Ancient Site. 
Waiting for the Right Moment
You may forego a roll on the Enemy Activity table 
on the next page by spending 1 Story Point. 
This is often an inefficient way of spending a 
point, but if you are in a situation where you 
really don’t want to risk an attack, it offers a 
chance to buy a little more time. 

=== PAGE 64 ===
The Campaign
62
Step 5: Colony Events
Life on Home is unpredictable, and anything 
can happen. Roll D100 on the Colony Events 
table to determine which event takes place 
this campaign turn and implement the result 
immediately. 
If the effects of an event turn out to be 
impossible to implement, ignore it. Instead,  
no event occurs during this campaign turn.
Remember: Colony Damage also results in a 
loss of Morale.

Normally this is not something you have to worry about but, if you wish, 
you may choose to play a Strike Mission this turn to track down an enemy 
Leader and gain 1 Enemy Information.

The enemy is shifting their deployments.

you have. Every die rolling a 4+ negates a point of damage.

(optional rule)
Sometimes the forces of the universe 
seem to align against you as a generator 
explodes, a colonist patrol is eaten by Vesirian 
Stalkercrawlers, and the research department 
explains that what they thought was a perpetual 
energy source was in fact no such thing
If you have 3 negative colony events in a row, 
add +1 Story Point.

Step 6: Mission Determination
Once you have reviewed your scout and enemy 
action reports, you can determine your primary 
mission for this campaign turn.
If you have been attacked, you may be required 
to play the Pitched Battle mission to defend 
your colony. 
If the choice is up to you, you may have a 
number of options available:
 
◆You may visit a sector that has not yet 
been Explored and explore it by playing a 
Scouting Mission.
 
◆You may go to a sector that is marked for 
Investigation to look for interesting and 
unusual items. This has the chance of 
finding Mission Data which can help you 
discover the truth about this world. Play an 
Investigation Mission.
D100
COLONY

DESCRIPTION

Report on 
progress
The report to Unity colonization headquarters highlights 
the hard work of a crew member.
Select one character to earn 1D6 XP. Increase Colony Morale by +1.

Animal 
migrations
The wildlife migrates according to changes in weather conditions.
Erase a random existing entry from your Lifeform Encounter Table and leave  
it blank. It is filled again when you roll that entry again.

Hostile virus
A particularly virulent infection has bypassed your 
biological containment systems.
Roll a D6 for each character on your roster; any who roll a natural 1 
have contracted the virus. Once you have rolled for every character, 
for each infected character pick a random non-infected character and 
roll again. Continue until no more natural 1s are rolled.
Each infected character must spend 1D3 campaign turns in Sick Bay.  
Roll separately for each.

Planetary phenomena change unpredictably.

=== PAGE 67 ===
65
The Campaign
 
◆You may go to a sector that has been 
Explored (meaning it has Resource values), 
but which is not yet Exploited (meaning the 
Resource value is 1 or higher). If so, play an 
Exploration Mission if you want to search 
for fresh resources to bolster the colony.  
You can instead play a Science Mission  
if you want to focus on research samples  
to improve your research programs.  
Typically, if you do not have more pressing 
needs, you will want to attempt this.
 
◆You might Hunt for Lifeforms in order to 
collect samples for bio-analysis.
 
◆You may opt to stay close to the colony and 
conduct a Patrol Mission. This can be used 
to explore map sectors near the colony. It 
can also be used to safeguard against future 
morale loss for the colony, provided it is 
done with minimal casualties.
 
◆If there are Tactical Enemies on the map 
(meaning you have completed at least one 
Milestone) you may try to capture a sector 
from the enemy. If this is your choice, play 
the Skirmish Mission. This is a good option 
to gain combat experience.
Some mission types are dependent on 
campaign events:
 
◆If a Tactical Enemy is conducting a Patrol 
or you have located a Boss, you may 
launch a Strike Mission. This will help 
you track down the enemy Strongpoint in 
preparation for a final attack. 
 
◆If you have located a Strongpoint of a Tactical 
Enemy, you may launch an Assault Mission. 
This is a major attack where the enemy is 
making their last stand. You need to bring 
everything you have for this one.
 
◆If you have located an Ancient Site, you may 
launch a Delve to look for treasures.
 
◆The results of the scouts’ report may result in 
a Rescue Mission or Scout Down! mission.
These missions are undertaken to avoid 
negative consequences.
For more information on all missions and to 
inform the next step, “Lock and Load”, see the 
missions detailed on pages 114-133.
Step 7: Lock and Load
When deploying for a mission, you may bring 
characters from your roster. The mission will 
state how many characters you can deploy and 
of which types. You are not required to bring 
characters of a particular class along, though 
having scouts and scientists on your team 
when exploring can assist you in gaining better 
rewards after the mission.
Characters in Sick Bay cannot be deployed. 
If a character completed their last point of 
recovery this campaign turn, they are ready for 
deployment, if desired.
You may fill any of your slots with bots or 
civvies, if you like. Civvies are less capable than 
any other character options, but you may be 
required to bring some, due to casualties and 
injuries or because you are hoping to receive 
a few promotions. (For more on civvies, see 
page 17).
If you have 2-4 grunts, they are organized into a 
single fireteam. If you have more than 4, divide 
them as evenly as possible into two fireteams.  
(For more on fireteams, see pages 16 and 30.)
You can always choose to bring fewer than the 
allowed characters if you would prefer to do so. 
There is no inherent advantage to doing so, but 
you may have a self-imposed challenge such 
as not using civvy characters or you may just 
prefer a greater challenge. 

=== PAGE 68 ===
The Campaign
66
Step 8: Play Out Your Mission
Set up your gaming table (see Battlefield 
Set-up, page 138) and play out the mission you 
selected to play this campaign turn.
Make sure to track any casualties and other 
events that occur during the game.
If you intend to share a battle report 
of your games online, make sure 
to take photos during the 
battle! It can be really 
frustrating to have had a 
great game and then 
realize that you got 
carried away and do 
not have any photos of 
the action.
Step 9: Injuries
Characters that became battle casualties may 
have suffered a range of fates: They may have 
been knocked out or become lost during the 
confusion, they may have been wounded, or 
they may have died outright. In this step you 
determine their fates. 
Injury rolls are made for any scientist, scout, 
trooper, or crew if they were removed from play 
due to becoming casualties in a tabletop battle.
Civvies do not roll on this table as they are 
never recurring characters (though you may 
like to do so for roleplay purposes). Bots do not 
roll for injuries. Instead, any bot that became a 
casualty is marked “Broken” on your campaign 
roster and cannot be used until repaired.
For each casualty, roll D100 and consult the 
Injury table below for their fate. 
Some abilities may permit rerolls for injuries. If 
this is the case, you may make all your required 
rolls first and then choose which to reroll. If a 
character has a reroll specific to themselves, it is 
only usable on them.
If a character is killed, any items they carried are 
destroyed. This is primarily of interest when it 
comes to artifacts you have found.  
Grunt Casualties
Grunts casualties are handled simply. Roll 1D6 
for each grunt that became a casualty; on 1-2, 
they have either died, their injuries were so 
severe they cannot continue the mission, or 
they opt to retire to less dangerous pursuits. On 
3-6, they are okay and recover in time for the 
next mission.
You begin the campaign with 12 grunts. 
Mark down whenever one becomes a 
permanent casualty.

The character must recover for 4 campaign turns.

The Campaign
Step 10: Experience Progression
Your characters will learn from their 
experiences as the campaign progresses, 
leading to improvements in their abilities on 
the battlefield.
Progression is measured with Experience 
Points (XP), which are earned after each 
mission.
A character only receives mission XP if they 
participated in a mission, but any character that 
was on the table for at least 1 round qualifies. 
XP can be awarded through random events and 
other factors, and these can be earned even if 
the character did not take part in a mission.
XP are tallied together for each character 
individually and may be spent to upgrade a 
character’s ability score. Each ability score can 
be upgraded to a maximum value as indicated 
below and each character may purchase only a 
single upgrade per campaign turn. 
A character “directly kills” a Leader if they 
carried out the attack that killed the Leader. 
Only Tactical Enemies have Leaders. A character 
that kills more than one Leader receives the 
reward only once.
Spending XP
Once a character has accumulated enough 
experience points, you may spend 5 XP to roll 
on the Advancement table below to gain 
an advance. This modifies the profile of the 
character as noted in the table. 
The number in parenthesis indicates the 
maximum value possible in a given ability 
score. If you roll an increase that is already 
maxed out, select any other advance of choice.
XP Points Buy – Option
If you prefer a nonrandom option, you may 
use the costs below. This allows you to buy 
upgrades directly. Players coming from Five 
Parsecs from Home will note that this is the 
same system used in that book.

A character that is moved off a battlefield edge 
in the first two rounds of a battle forfeits all XP 
awards from that battle. Note that characters that 
become casualties in the first two rounds are 
awarded as normal (they received a hard-won 
lesson about taking cover!).

Step 11: Colony Morale 
Adjustments
The morale of your colony is subject to  
constant changes as the campaign progresses 
(this system is explained in detail on page 89). 
In particular, your Colony Morale score 
automatically drops 1 point during this step, 
regardless of any actions taken. It will drop an 
additional point for every battle casualty. 
If you were playing a Rescue Mission, you do 
NOT suffer Morale loss due to casualties, except 
for the colonists in the mission.
If you are now at -10 or worse, you must now 
roll on the Morale Incident table (see page 90).
If a Crisis is ongoing, a test is now made to 
determine the resolution of the Crisis.
Step 12: Track Enemy Information 
and Mission Data
If you fought a Tactical Enemy and won, add  
1 Enemy Information for that enemy type. You 
should also note that they cannot take an action 
next campaign turn. If they are selected to take 
one, treat it as no event.
If you obtained any Mission Data, note it on 
your roster. It is resolved when you reach a 
Campaign Milestone (see page 156).
Step 13: Replacements
As you play the campaign, it is likely you will 
suffer casualties. Among your colonists there 
will be exceptional individuals who can take a 
place among your squad, but they are often few 
in number.
the number of replacements you can attempt 
per turn is 1 + 1 per Milestone achieved.  
If you have less than a full roster (8 characters, 
initially), roll 2D6, trying to obtain a 
replacement by doing surveys and interviews 
of your colonists:
Alternate Advancement Options
A character eligible for an advancement roll may 
instead pick one of the following options:
 
◆If currently Disloyal or Committed, increase 
Loyalty to Loyal.
 
◆If the character is a scientist, gain  
3 Research Points instead.
 
◆If the character is a scout, gain 3 units of  
Raw Materials instead.
Civvy Heroic Promotion
If any civvies participated in the mission, pick 
one who did not become a casualty and roll 
2D6. On 10-12, they have shown themselves 
to have what it takes and can be added to your 
roster of grunts.

=== PAGE 71 ===
69
The Campaign
2D6
REPLACEMENT
2-6
No replacement is currently available.
7-8
Roll 1D6 again:
1-2:	 You may take a trooper.
3-4:	 You may take a scientist.
5-6:	 You may take a scout.
Each begins with the standard profile.
9-12
You may take a replacement of your 
choice. 
All characters enter with a Loyalty level of 
Committed.
If desired for any reason, you may dismiss 
characters from your roster at this stage. 
Dismissed characters cannot be returned to the 
campaign later. 
Step 14: Research
During this step, you receive any Research 
Points (RP) you have earned from any sources 
this campaign turn, which are added to any 
points you had left over from prior campaign 
turns.
Any RP you have accumulated can now 
be spent, allowing you to research new 
technologies. This is explained in detail on 
page 91.
Step 15: Building
During this step, you receive any Build Points 
(BP) you have earned from any sources this 
campaign turn, which are added to any points 
you had left over from prior campaign turns.
Any BP can now be spent, allowing you to 
construct new buildings for your colony. This is 
explained in detail on page 97.
You may reclaim one building at this point, 
gaining BP equal to half the BP cost of the 
building, rounded down. These can be spent 
immediately.
Step 16: Colony Integrity
If your current Colony Integrity is -3 or worse, 
check to see what the consequences are for the 
damage your colony has taken. See page 87 
for more on Colony Integrity and the Integrity 
Failure table. 
Step 17: Character Event
Your characters have their own lives when 
they are not on mission. Randomly select one 
character and roll D100 on the Character 
Event table (see page 70) to see what 
happens to them.
All characters are potentially eligible even if 
they are recovering from injuries (adventurers 
rarely keep still just because they have a 
bandaged arm and a broken foot). 
If a character is currently absent from the 
campaign entirely, such as during the R&R 
event, they cannot be the target of events. 
(Note that your Administrator does not count as 
a character for event purposes.) 
In some rare cases, the effects of an event turn 
out to be impossible to implement. If this 
happens, ignore the event; instead, nothing 
happens this campaign turn.
Grunts cannot be the target of Character Events. 
Step 17a: Character Roleplay 
Events (optional rule)
If you choose to, you may generate roleplay 
events at this stage. Roleplay events have no 
effect on game mechanics but are included for 
players who wish to flesh out the ongoing story 
and for the benefit of those who enjoy writing 
up their campaigns online or in a notebook. They 
can also serve as inspiration for custom scenarios 
or side stories.
To generate roleplay events, see page 72.

=== PAGE 72 ===
The Campaign
70
Character Event table 
D100
CHARACTER 
EVENT
DESCRIPTION
01-05

The character undergoes a training course to hone their 
mission-relevant skills. 
The character gains +2 XP.
06-10
Minor promotion
Proficiency is ideally rewarded with improvements in rank and responsibility.
The character gains a level of Loyalty and +1 XP. If they were already Loyal, 
gain +2 XP (total).
11-15
Personal 
investigation
The character does a bit of information gathering.
Randomly choose a Tactical Enemy and receive +1 Enemy Information.
16-20
Something in the 
water, I suppose
The character comes down with a minor illness.
If the character is in sick bay, increase their recovery time by 1 turn. If they are 
not in sick bay, they get a tummy ache but are otherwise fine.
21-25
R&R
The character earns some time off world.
The character is gone for 2 campaign turns during which they cannot be 
the target of events. They recover completely from any injuries, 
regardless of recovery time.
If they are Disloyal, roll 1D6: On 1-2, they do not return; on a 3-5, 
they remain Disloyal; and on a 6, they become Committed.
26-30
Change of 
assignment
The character is offered a new assignment.
Decide if they accept. If they do, they immediately earn 5 XP and are 
transferred to another Five Parsecs game of your choice. If they decline, 
gain 1 level of Loyalty. 
31-35
Disputes with 
leadership
The character has a heated disagreement with Administration.
The character loses a level of Loyalty but cannot drop below Disloyal.
36-40
Commitment to 
the cause
The character is satisfied with recent Administration actions and is 
dedicated to the cause.
The character gains a level of Loyalty.
41-45
Dispute
A clash of personalities occurs on the team.
Randomly pick a second character. The two have a heated dispute over 
something and cannot both be assigned to the mission next campaign turn 
unless you are fighting a Pitched Battle.
46-50
Personal 
calibrations
Time spent customizing gun sights and ammunition loads pays off.
The next time the character is on a mission, select one weapon they carry, 
which receives a +1 hit bonus for the duration of that mission.
51-55
Sickness
The character falls ill.
They must spend 2 campaign turns recovering.
