The Campaign
The Armory
Your colonists are an exploratory force, not 
gun-slinging mercenaries expecting to suit 
up for war, but they must still be ready to 
defend themselves. While the firepower 
below is limited compared to that presented 
in Five Parsecs from Home and Five Parsecs: 
Tactics, research and discoveries may yield 
more advanced weapons as you play the 
campaign game. 
Note: Some weapons function differently in 
Planetfall from similarly-named weapons you 
may have experienced in other 5X games.
Each character must be armed for the mission. 
Each weapon type is limited to certain classes 
only, as indicated in its traits (see the weapon 
traits list on page 78). 
Civvies, Scientists, and Bots
Each character from these classes may be 
given one weapon requiring Civilian training: 
handgun, colonial shotgun, or colony rifle. 
Scouts
Each scout may be given one weapon 
requiring Civilian or Scout training: handgun, 
colonial shotgun, colony rifle, or scout pistol.
Troopers
Each trooper may be given one weapon 
requiring Civilian or Trooper training: 
handgun, colonial shotgun, colony rifle, 
trooper rifle, assault gun, or flame projector.
Grunts
Individual grunts are armed with infantry 
rifles. If you take a fireteam of at least 3, one 
grunt may take a light machine gun in place 
of their rifle.  
Crew
Crew characters may bring any weapon they 
own (imported from another campaign). 
In addition, they may bring one weapon 
requiring Civilian training: handgun, colonial 
shotgun, or colony rifle.
Campaign Play
As you progress in the campaign you will receive 
access to improved weaponry. Note that having 
access to improved items does not remove 
the original items. Not every upgraded item is 
strictly better in every regard, and you are free to 
use either option for a mission.
75

=== PAGE 78 ===
The Campaign
76
Campaign Armory
As you progress in the campaign you will 
receive access to interesting new weapons and 
gear. Some of these are based on technologies 
you discover on the new world, others are 
improvisations made from locally available 
hardware. Where this is the case, new options 
list the prerequisites to develop them. This is 
both the research required to develop the item 
and the Item Tier it belongs to.
Tier 1 upgrades require that you have built the 
Advanced Manufacturing Plant (see page 98), 
while Tier 2 upgrades also require the High-
Tech Manufacturing Plant (page 100).
Once these conditions are met, the weapon 
is available for use immediately and in any 
quantity needed.
Some characters do not have the training to be 
granted some weapons during a mission load-
out. Each item has a class type (or types) listed 
as a trait (Civilian, Scout, Trooper, etc). Check the 
weapon trait listing (page 78), as some classes 
can use weapons with traits other than just 
their own class title (for example, troopers can 
use Trooper and Civilian weapons).
Note: while some new weapons are modified 
versions of existing ones, weapons are not 
replaced in the campaign, and you can always 
pick and choose for each character and mission.
While many new weapons are superior, 
some have new drawbacks or are superior 
situationally but not universally.
Each weapon has the following profile:
Range: The range in tabletop inches. Targets 
cannot be selected beyond this range.
Shots: How many attack dice are rolled.
Damage: This is added to the die roll when 
rolling to overcome the target’s Toughness.
Traits: Some weapons have additional 
traits that modify their function or define 
prerequisites. These weapons traits are defined 
at the end of the weapons list.

Weapon Traits
AP Ammo: If stationary, the shooter may add 
+1 to damage. 
Area: Select a target point in range and sight. 
Each enemy within 2” is hit on a D6 roll of 4+. 
Cover provides no protection. The bio-gun does 
not affect your own characters.
Armor-piercing: Ignore Armor Saving Throws.
Burning: Negates Saving Throws.
Civilian: Open to all classes except grunts.
Critical: A natural 6 to hit inflicts 2 hits on the 
target figure. 
Cumbersome: Range is reduced to 12” when 
moving and firing.
Flexible: A character with this weapon can carry 
a handgun at the same time.
Focused: Both shots must be fired at the same 
target.	
Grunt: Restricted to grunt-class characters.
Hail of Fire: If all shots are aimed at the same 
target figure, 4 shots may be fired.
Hyperfire: If a hit does not destroy the target, 
the trooper may immediately fire again at the 
same target. Continue until a shot misses or the 
target is destroyed.
Knockback: A target that survives a hit is 
knocked Sprawling (see page 38).
Melee: +2 bonus when used as a brawling 
weapon.
Mind-link: Roll to hit using Savvy instead of 
Combat Skill. As this is a Savvy test, a scientist 
may roll twice and pick the best.
Phased fire: The target receives no benefits 
from cover or scatter terrain. The target must be 
visible, however.
Pistol: +1 weapon bonus when brawling.
Scout: Restricted to scout-class characters.

cannot save it up for some grunts and use it

The danger of wildlife necessitates the carrying 
of personal weaponry for all ranks.
Prerequisite: Shard pistol, Grunt
All grunts carry a handgun in addition to their 
normal weapons.

Soldiers worried about aggressive bio-forms 
begin to copy boarding teams in carrying blade 
weapons whenever possible.
Prerequisite: Carver blade, Grunt
One grunt per fireteam receives a Damage 
+1 Melee weapon in addition to their other 
weapons.
Compatibility Notes
Players of other Five Parsecs titles will notice 
that the weapons given do not always have the 
same profile and traits as in other titles. This 
is intentional, representing modified versions 
issued to colonist expeditions.

=== PAGE 82 ===
The Campaign
Enemies Among Us
Theodora called up the depot’s inventory report 
again. It was clever, she had to admit.
The missing items didn’t look suspicious at 
first: Petty things, black market resalable value, 
but in small enough quantities not to awake 
suspicion. Warehouse workers usually got by on 
graft, but among the regular minor stuff, these 
other items added up to improvised explosives 
– and a disconcerting amount, too. Running the 
numbers for the past quarter, there was enough 
missing to demolish an entire building. 
She closed the database and reached for her 
pen and notepad. Her Agency coworkers had 
made fun of her for keeping an old-fashioned 
habit, but it was the one thing that couldn’t 
be hacked. If the colony was housing a terror 
group, that would be an essential precaution.
The obvious question was “Who?”, but time and 
data would provide clues. The real question was 
“Why?” Separatists? Zealots of some type? Both 
were possible, but something wasn’t adding 
up. Terror groups would usually aim to strike a 
population center, but the suspicious sightings 
were all transport and communications related. 
Corporate sabotage? Unlikely. A hostile takeover 
of a Unity-funded colony was bold, even for a 
mega-corp. They’d want the facilities intact, too. 
She pushed the button on her wrist 
communicator. A moment later, Simmons 
strode in, looking somewhat self-conscious in 
an ill-fitting uniform. 
“Ma’am, what can…” he began. Theodora cut 
him off with a hand-wave.
“Run the shuttle crash report by me again”
“Ma’am, the data is—”
Theodora beetled her eyebrows – enough to 
stop him. The young man sighed and recited 
the information again. Young field agents were 
always so diligent. 
“Shuttle struck by small arms ground fire 
and crashed ... No survivors found ... Pirates 
suspected.” As Simmons rattled off the 
information, Theodora doodled on the notepad, 
raising her gaze only when the report was done.
“So, what’s your conclusion, Simmons?”
Simmons worked up some courage: “I think the 
security report is wrong. They said pirates but... I 
checked the logs. No off-world jump signal”.
Theodora nodded, folding the notepad, and 
slowly stood up. 
“Simmons, requisition a scout drop. We’re 
going to have a look for ourselves.”
80

=== PAGE 83 ===
81
Colonies
A Colonial Briefing
Colonization, as defined by Unity law, is the 
process of establishing a new, permanent 
population base on a world with no current 
sentient life. This does not mean that 
settlements are not created on populated 
worlds, but those fall under either co-
existence agreements, hostile acquisition, or 
flat-out invasion.
Colonization can occur for several reasons 
but, unless a prospective world happens to 
be supportive of the species in question, 
terraforming is required, demanding a 
substantial amount of monetary investment. 
Most colonization is planned by mega 
corporations or major galactic power bases 
who can fund the effort in expectation of future 
returns on that investment.
The major galactic powers lay claim to several 
unpopulated systems in anticipation of future 
colonization and, while treaties are clear 
regarding the legality of such claims, a general 
level of localized military action is accepted in 
galactic politics. In practice, a claim is rarely 
stronger than the ability and willingness of 
a galactic power to enforce it, and resolution 
through military action is often seen as 
preferable to a drawn-out legal process. The 
K’Erin, in particular, often engage in military 
action to contest land claims. This can be a 
dangerous gamble as the colonists are as likely 
to fight their brethren as they are any Human 
claimants, but the K’Erin don’t necessarily view 
the ensuing war as a negative outcome.
Cost and Scale
The cost and effort involved in colonization 
is immense. If terraforming is required, the 
staggering costs are usually associated with 
either strict major power control or mega-
corporate ownership leading to a de-facto 
corporate state when the colony is founded.
Often, secondary settlement agreements are 
sold off to interested parties, typically dispersed 
across the planetary surface. Independent 
groups, usually with a wealthy benefactor, can 
pool together resources to acquire colonization 
rights on an already inhabitable planet, but 
it is more common to purchase settlement 
on a corporate world. This is a popular option 
with splinter groups, political renegades and 
religious communities.
When worlds are assessed for terraforming 
viability, they are given a numerical grade from 
0 to 100 indicating the effort required to make 
them viable for organic habitation. This roughly 
corresponds to 1 year of required terraforming 
for every point short of 100.
While not every species has the same life 
support requirements, the species of the 
major powers share sufficiently common 
characteristics that terraforming machinery is 
rarely species-specific (customers can request 
specialized modules).
A world with some traces of atmosphere and 
gravity within typical parameters (0.8 to 1.2 G 
by Earth measurements) is usually assessed at 
a rating of 70-75.
Colonies

=== PAGE 84 ===
Colonies
82
Opposition
Colonial settlements can meet opposition from 
a range of sources: There are many Pristine 
Galaxy and de-humanization movements that 
seek to limit expansion of colonies, some of 
which are willing to turn to terrorist acts to 
achieve their aims. Meanwhile, the mixture of 
political and corporate interests can make any 
specific settlement hotly contested. Shooting 
wars between corporate armies for the fate of a 
promising chunk of rock are not uncommon.
Hostile Zones
Settlement along the K’Erin / Unity boundaries 
are almost always contested militarily and 
any new colonization attempt must be 
accompanied by considerable military force. 
The fact that distinguishing between “civilian 
colony” and “heavy armed military base” 
is difficult in K’Erin space has led to several 
shooting wars in the past.
The Precursors maintain a sector of space near 
the galactic center into which they strictly 
prohibit incursions. The reason this area 
is being safeguarded is only known to the 
highest-ranking members of the Precursor 
navy, but any attempts to cross the sector 
boundaries in either direction are met with 
immediate attack, if detected.
Whatever the reason, a Precursor naval 
officer can only ever do a single two-year 
tour of duty in the region. As a result, in 
recent years, Soulless ships have been seen 
carrying out patrols as well, seemingly with 
the understanding and cooperation of the 
Precursors.
Worlds that are near Horde- or Converted-
contested space are not typically approved for 
colonization and access to such worlds may 
be subject to Unity naval interdiction. After all, 
each failed colony means more resources and 
recruits for the enemy. This does not stop the 
foolhardy, however, and rogue expeditions are 
not unheard of.
This is not the only reason a world can fall 
under interdiction. Whether to contain an 
outbreak of a virulent disease or for more 
clandestine reasons, Unity may place a 
world under quarantine, enforced by sentry 
platforms or active naval patrols. While this is 
typically intended to be for a distinct length 
of time, requests to lift the quarantine often 
go unnoticed in the Bureaucracy and, in a few 
instances, a fully inhabited colony has found 
itself mistakenly interdicted, functionally 
isolating it from the rest of the galaxy.
Colony Types
Colonies are constructed for an array of 
purposes but can broadly be categorized  
as follows:
Resource Extraction
The most immediate way of getting a return 
on investment is through the extraction of 
resources, mineral or otherwise. Due to the 
significant cost of transporting large amounts 
of materials off world, this is primarily of value 
on planets where rare resources can be found 
in large quantities, though many colonies 
begin as resource extraction facilities before 
transitioning into a different type over time.
Production
Manufacturing allows the creation of an 
economic basis for a colony and is often one of 
the earliest functions to be established. Many 
colonies are primarily industrial hubs, and 
some remain that way indefinitely.
In some cases, planetary conditions can aid 
manufacture of specific goods, allowing the 
new colony a competitive advantage. Of course, 
being able to manufacture legally or ethically 
suspect goods well out of sight is a competitive 
advantage all on its own, leading to many 
mysterious industrial outposts heavily patrolled 
by corporate troops.

=== PAGE 85 ===
83
Colonies
Population
Some colonies are established primarily for the 
purpose of new living space, especially when 
established by government bodies. This is also 
the primary type of independent colony found 
across the galaxy. Most colonies will transition 
to this type over time when a significant 
population base has started to develop, and a 
more nuanced economy exists.
Research
As the cost of establishing an advanced 
laboratory in a remote location is considerable, 
this tends to be of interest mainly if the planet 
has promising or peculiar characteristics 
or resides in a system with strange cosmic 
phenomena. Unity-founded research facilities 
can be found littering all manner of worlds in 
a perpetual attempt to catalogue everything 
scientists lay eyes on.
Many corporations have an interest in 
conducting research in locations where it is 
relatively difficult for competitors to acquire it 
(as well as out of sight of prying eyes, especially 
when conducting illegal research).
Outpost
Outposts can serve several purposes, but the 
term generally refers to an installation with 
a limited population and not designed to 
produce anything of financial or intellectual 
value. They are often military refueling bases 
or staging grounds, various scanning and 
communication facilities, and similar. A wide 
array can be found almost anywhere you look 
in space, with many being installed on moons 
or particularly large asteroids for ease of access.
Isolation
A key factor for many minor and independent 
colonies is the desire to be where nobody 
else is around. Isolationist enclaves, zealot 
organizations, and all manner of idealistic 
communities gather everything they have and 
find a piece of rock to call their own.
16

=== PAGE 86 ===
Colonies
84
Colonial Life
Depending on the nature of a colony, life can 
vary considerably from oppressive iron-fisted 
regimes to relatively idyllic experiences. 
It is rarely an easy life, however. Many 
colonies are dependent on off-world supplies 
for survival and disruptions in trade networks 
can be fatal.
Even if a colony is primarily concerned with a 
function such as military research or mining, 
it will usually strive to establish at least a 
basic level of self-sufficiency from the local 
environment, if possible.
As a colony grows, it tends to expand and 
become more complex. A research outpost 
might develop a small farming community 
to become self-sustaining. As the population 
base increases, a civilian economy begins 
to develop which in turn attracts more 
colonists and so on. Over the years, the 
original purpose of the colony may remain, 
or it may slowly disappear, subsumed by 
the general economic and civilian life that 
develops around it.
Hazards
During its lifetime, a colony will have to 
weather many challenges and dangers.  
These may result in the colony being destroyed 
or abandoned, while others may inflict long-
lasting damage or change the nature of the 
colony in response to events.
The more common risks are:
Natural / Cosmic Disasters
While survey programs and 
terraforming technology allows 
colonies to be placed in regions that are 
geologically safe, a wide range of disasters 
can strike, especially when a planet exhibits 
unexpected natural phenomena. These can 
range from seismic events, eruptions, or an 
unexpectedly toxic environment.
Many worlds exhibit unexpected reactions 
to terraforming, while others harbor secrets 
that are not uncovered until someone makes 
landfall. Unity doctrine states a world must be 
surveyed for 10 years prior to any permanent 
colonization, but this is often not adhered to 
in the interests of profit and expansion.
Resource Exhaustion
While depletion of a natural resource can 
have financial impacts, it can also be life 
threatening. A self-sufficient colony can 
become dependent on external supply if it 
becomes unable to sustain agriculture or 
other food production, for example.
In some cases, formerly independent 
colonies have had to accept protectorate 
status from one of the major galactic powers 
to continue its existence, while others have 
slowly been abandoned except for a few 
remaining die-hards.

=== PAGE 87 ===
85
Colonies
Isolation
The drifting of Galactic travel tunnel points is 
usually predictable, but a world can become 
isolated from easy galactic access. Worlds 
can also become isolated due to political 
circumstances or may even choose isolation 
for themselves. An isolated world can rapidly 
deviate from Unity cultural norms, leading to 
considerable tension if it is reacquired.
Invasion
In a dangerous galaxy, external threats abound: 
K’Erin demands, Converted assaults, and any 
number of space-borne horrors may strike. 
Smaller colonies are also vulnerable to pirates 
and other raiders, with some of the renegade 
forces in Fringe space having the same military 
force as a small empire.
A successful invasion often results in the colony 
being destroyed or rendered unviable.
A colony that falls to the Converted is 
completely taken over, with the remnants 
of the crew, dead or alive, turned into more 
Converted. And many a colony has been wiped 
out after an unsuspecting deep space trader 
accidentally brought the Swarm to their world.
Dangerous Wildlife
A side effect of terraforming on a world with 
an active biological eco-system is that it can 
result in runaway evolutionary processes, as the 
wildlife and fauna undergo an accelerated (and 
unpredictable) pace of development. This effect 
is often allowed to play out with an eye towards 
future exploitation of resources but can also 
result in distinctly unfriendly creatures.
Roughly 3% of full-scale terraforming attempts 
result in what is termed a “Hostile Biosphere” 
where animals, plant life, or both are too 
dangerous to permit civilian colonization, 
though with Humans being an intractable 
species, this rarely prevents attempts. Precursor 
scientists maintain that these reactions are part 
of a sort of global psionic defensive mechanism 
by the world as a whole, though Unity psionic 
testing has revealed no such evidence.
Strife
The most common hazard to a colony tends 
to be internal strife. As factions emerge (or 
attempt to secure power) the colony can 
become split, dysfunctional, or even succumb 
to outright fighting. Outside control, such 
as corporate control may suppress internal 
disputes but often leads to protest, insurrection, 
or armed conflict against the controlling body.
While a controlling body is usually incentivized 
to avoid damage to infrastructure, the civilian 
population is replaceable and appeals to 
Unity mediation (or peacekeeping) are 
usually submitted in advance of a planned 
insurrection.
Colonial Security
Security forces are maintained for several 
purposes: Internal order, protection from 
threat, and as a general, deployable body 
with a high degree of readiness. The 
controllers of a colony project will be intent on 
managing social unrest, while even peaceful 
communities will maintain some level of law 
enforcement presence.
As response times to a distress signal can be 
slow, colonies must maintain at least token 
military forces. On an important world, this may 
be conventional Unity (or other) military forces, 
but the standard is some form of colonial 
militia. On Unity worlds, the militia is part of 
the Unity military hierarchy though for practical 
purposes they tend to operate independently.
The militia can range from a few squads with 
old hunting weapons to a battalion of well-
equipped troops substantially equivalent to 
conventional armed forces, depending on the 
wealth and technology available to the colony.
Controllers will want to protect their 
investments: Colonies in dangerous areas of 
space may sport corporate security teams, Unity 
combat detachments, and more, depending on 
who is doing the building. Many worlds hire 
additional forces through mercenary troops.

=== PAGE 88 ===
Colonies
86
Colony Ruins
Abandoned colonies are not an unusual find 
within many star systems. These may originate 
from failed colonization attempts, worlds that 
were abandoned or died out or from more 
mysterious causes. 
In harsh living conditions, a military attack 
that is fended off may still inflict enough 
damage that the remnants opt to flee or end 
up dying from a failure of systems required 
for survival. Other attackers are after the 
colonists themselves and will leave behind 
the structures. 
Such sites attract scavengers, salvage teams 
and squatters, all for their own reasons. They 
also make popular hideouts for criminals. 
Some colony sites will even make ruins of 
older attempts into memorial sites or tourist 
attractions depending on the general degree 
of reverence, cunning, or ghoulishness by the 
particular colonial administration.
Civilizations Long Gone
The galaxy has been teeming with life since a 
very early age and many such civilizations have 
come and gone, some leaving only elusive 
fragments of their passing and others leaving 
extensive ruins.
Such sites are usually subject to government 
rules when they are discovered, but until the 
authorities can secure the site, any discovery 
is likely to result in a frenzy of salvage crews, 
treasure hunters, and eccentrics trying to 
pick the place clean. When local preservation 
groups, militant colonial nomads, and esoteric 
cults get involved, the combination can become 
violent very quickly. 
While most such locations are unlikely to have 
much of value – after all, several thousand years 
is likely to degrade all but the most substantial 
(and resilient) artifacts – there is always a buyer 
interested in whatever can be hauled away 
if the prospective treasure hunter can dodge 
other treasure hunters as well as the various 
belligerent locals that are liable to show up and 
take a dim view of the looting.
17

=== PAGE 89 ===
87
Colonies
Colony Integrity and Damage
Your colony may sustain damage from a variety 
of sources such as random events and enemy 
attacks. Preventing enemies from expanding 
too far is key to limiting your risk of colony 
damage, while a number of buildings you can 
construct will help mitigate incoming damage.
Colony Damage represents an array of 
concerns: Physical damage to structures, loss of 
resources, and injury to personnel. 1-2 points 
of damage may be a minor facility operating 
at reduced capacity or a few colonists injured, 
while 7-8 points may represent widespread 
damage and multiple fatalities. 
We do not track damage by individual 
buildings. Instead, the colony sustains damage 
as an entity measured by a numeric value.
Colony Integrity
A more advanced colony has redundant 
systems, backup facilities, and extensive 
procedures in place to deal with emergencies. 
This is measured in the Colony Integrity.
A brand-new colony always begins with an 
Integrity of 0, with certain buildings improving 
your Integrity score. 
Any time your colony sustains Damage, it is 
subtracted from the Integrity total. This can (and 
often will) produce a negative score.
For example, if you have Integrity 5 and take 
8 points of Colony Damage, you now have an 
Integrity of -3.
Some buildings allow mitigation of damage. 
Reduce the amount of Colony Damage you 
would have taken by the amount of mitigation. 
If you get a bonus to Integrity during the 
campaign, you increase both the maximum 
Integrity score and the current value. For 
example, if you have a maximum Integrity of 
5 and a current score of -2, a 3-point increase 
in Integrity would put your new maximum at 
8 and the current score at 1.
Repairs
Your colony has a certain repair capacity 
available. This allows a number of damage 
points to be repaired each campaign turn, 
beginning with 1 point per turn when you 
begin the campaign. Simply increase Integrity 
by the repair amount until Integrity returns to 
its maximum value (initially set to 0). 
Repairs can never increase Integrity above the 
maximum value allowed by the advances and 
buildings you have achieved. 
Colony Integrity Failure
If Integrity is negative, you are at risk of 
additional mishaps befalling your colony. In the 
Colony Integrity Step of the campaign turn, if 
Integrity is -3 or worse, you must take a test for 
Colony Integrity Failure. 
Roll 3D6. If the roll is equal to or below the 
negative Integrity score “flipped” (for example 
if Integrity is minus 8, treat it as an 8), you 
suffer an Integrity Failure. Consult the score on 
the table below. This means Integrity of -1 or -2 
has no risk (as you cannot roll that low on 3D6).
Remember that Colony Damage also affects 
Colony Morale. 
Colony Damage and Colony Morale
Seeing their livelihoods directly threatened has 
a dramatic effect on the trust of the colonists. 
Any time Integrity drops for any reason, Colony 
Morale drops by the same amount (see page 89). 
For example, if you take 4 Colony Damage, you 
will also lose 4 points of Colony Morale. 
Damage that is mitigated by factors such as 
defensive buildings does not affect Morale 
(seeing the automatic sentry turrets operating  
at full capacity is reassuring!).

Emergency Repairs
If you are required to roll on this table, you may 
spend 1 Story Point to avoid doing so. This 
does not change the underlying situation, but 
hopefully next campaign turn you will be able 
to whittle away at the amount of damage.
Raw Materials
You may obtain Raw Materials during your 
explorations. These are stockpiled and can be 
expended to make emergency repairs. Spend 
1 unit of Raw Material to conduct 1 point of 
damage repairs. You can spend up to 3 units of 
Raw Materials in one campaign turn.
The Death Spiral
Once damage reduces you to around -10 
Integrity or worse, you are at risk of suffering 
increasing damage each campaign turn as 
most rolls for Integrity failure will result in 
additional negative effects, which may well 
outpace your ability to repair it.
Review the building options you have available 
to see if there is anything that can help you 
out. Otherwise, getting out into the field and 
doing Exploration missions is 
often your best bet for 
finding new Raw 
Materials.

=== PAGE 91 ===
89
Colonies
Rescue Missions
You do not have to deduct Morale if your squad 
suffer casualties during a Rescue Mission. 
Instead, you are penalized if the colonists 
become casualties.
Personal Appeals
When forced to test for a Morale Incident, you 
may intervene directly. Spend 1 Story Point 
and count the test result as “nothing happens”. 
This still sets the Morale score back to 0, so it is a 
potent but precious resource.
Difficulty Adjustments (optional rule)
The Morale system works both as a pacing 
mechanic as well as a potential failure state. If 
you find that you would rather play a game that 
is less punishing, omit the Crisis test or allow BP 
and RP to be accumulated during a Crisis. (The 
latter can be particularly appealing if you are 
near the end of the game. You will know whether 
you would enjoy the increased risk of the colony 
possibly collapsing at the critical moment).
Colony Morale
Colony life is hard, dangerous, and often lacks 
both prosperity and individuality. Some colony 
administrators run democratic systems, others 
are technocratics, while some function as 
corporate boards or military facilities. 
Colonists are inherently hardy: Everyone 
who signs up is tested to ensure they have 
compatible attitudes and are able to cope with 
high degrees of hardship and privation. 
However, this does not make colonies immune 
to dissent. A string of poor luck can result in the 
colony developing dissident factions which can 
threaten to burst out into open rebellion.
Likewise, if the colonists feel progress towards 
the goals of the colony has stalled, their spirits 
and faith in the administration will deteriorate, 
reflected in Morale worsening each campaign 
turn. This in turn leads to fallout when groups 
decide to protest their lot in life.
Tracking Morale
Colony Morale is tracked on your Colony 
Tracking Sheet. It begins at 0. A positive Morale 
score means everything is fine. A 0 means 
things are okay. If the score is negative, people 
are starting to grumble in the corners.
Colony Morale is modified as follows:
Every time a casualty is sustained in battle, 
Morale is reduced by 1 point. This does not 
apply to any other source of injuries such as 
during events.
Each time your colony buildings sustain 
damage, Morale is reduced by 1 point per 
point of Colony Damage. Mitigated damage 
and damage assigned to “damage sink” 
buildings does not reduce Morale.
Each campaign turn, Morale is reduced by 1 
automatically. It’s a tough life.
Several events in the game can modify Morale 
up or down. There is no maximum Morale. 
Morale Incidents
If Morale reaches -10 or worse during the 
Colony Morale step of the campaign turn 
sequence, an Incident occurs. Roll D100 on the 
table below and apply the result. Morale is set 
back to 0, as the colonists vent their frustrations 
and go back to normal (at least for now).
Add +1 to Political Upheaval every time you 
roll on the Morale Incident table.
The Crisis Check
Each time you receive a point of Political 
Upheaval, you must make a Crisis check. 
Roll 2D6. If the roll is equal to or below the 
amount of Political Upheaval, the colony suffers 
a collapse of government and enters a Crisis.

=== PAGE 92 ===
Colonies
90
While in Crisis, Colony Morale is fixed at 0 and 
cannot be changed for any reason. All modifiers 
are ignored. All sources of Research Points, 
Build Points. or Raw Materials provide 1 point 
less than usual during a Crisis. 
Each campaign turn, during the Colony Morale 
Adjustments step, roll 2D6 to determine the 
outcome of the Crisis. A Story Point may be 
spent to roll twice and pick the better score.
Campaign Failure
If the Crisis Outcome results in campaign 
failure, Unity forces move in to secure the site 
and take over control. Players of Five Parsecs 
from Home: Tactics may enjoy playing out a 
combat campaign set on your former colony, 
while others may take some of their loyal (or 
disloyal!) characters and set off into a Five 
Parsecs from Home adventure.

=== PAGE 93 ===
91
Colonies
Research
Research Basics
To succeed on a new world, you will need new 
and improved technologies, weapons, and 
tools. Many of these will be based on local 
conditions, problems, and available materials. 
We call this process Research in game terms.
In the Research step of the campaign turn, 
you can pursue scientific advances. There are 
three types:
 
◆Theories are broader fields used to unlock 
new technologies.
 
◆Applications provide specific benefits, 
whether in combat or when managing  
your colony. 
 
◆You can also pursue Bio-Analysis of hostile 
Lifeforms located on the planet.
The Research Process
Conducting Research is done through 
Research Points (RP). Your colony receives 
1 RP per campaign turn by default. Various 
factors will grant additional points or increase 
the rate per turn.
All RP earned are tallied up and added to any 
you have currently accumulated. You may then 
spend RP to “buy” advances. You can spend RP 
on multiple items in the same campaign turn. 
You may obtain additional RP through Post-
Battle Finds and random events. These are 
one-use bonus points and are deleted when 
they are spent. 
Note that many items will cost more than what 
you can afford in a single campaign turn. In this 
case note down how many RP you have already 
spent. Once the full cost has been paid, the 
advance is made.
18

=== PAGE 94 ===
Colonies
92
Bio-Analysis Research
Cost: 3 Research Points
You may research any Lifeform on your 
campaign encounter chart to help find a more 
effective way to combat the creatures. 
This is effective against Lifeforms but not 
against other foes such as Tactical Enemies, the 
Slyn, or the Sleeper robots.
You must collect a sample from the Lifeform in 
question by successfully completing a Hunt 
mission.
Once a sample has been collected, you may 
conduct Bio-Analysis by spending 3 Research 
Points. Roll 1D6 on the table below to 
determine the benefit gained. The indicated 
benefit is applied whenever you do combat 
with the specific Lifeform in the future. 
If the profile of the Lifeform ever changes for 
any reason, including evolution, any Bio-
Analysis benefits are lost. You can analyze the 
new form of the creature by following the same 
process again. This will produce a new benefit 
rolled below.
Theories and Applications
You may spend RP on any Theories you have 
currently unlocked. 
When you begin the campaign, the following 
Theories are available:
 
◆AI Theories
 
◆Advanced Manufacturing
 
◆Environmental Research
 
◆Genetic Advancement
 
◆Infantry Equipment
 
◆Military Doctrine
 
◆Social Theories
 
◆Theoretical Physics
Five more Theories list prerequisites: Theories 
that must be fully researched before the new 
Theory becomes available. They include:
 
◆Adapted Combat Gear 
(prerequisite: Infantry Equipment)
 
◆Environmental Adaptation 
(prerequisite: Environmental Research)
 
◆High Level Adaptation 
(prerequisite: Genetic Advancement)
 
◆Non-Linear Physics 
(prerequisite: Theoretical Physics)
 
◆Psionic Engineering 
(prerequisite: Genetic Advancement)
Bio-Analysis Table
D6
BIO-ANALYSIS
DESCRIPTION
1
Hit bonus
Analysis of evasive measures improves hit rates. 
All ranged attacks against the Lifeform receive +1 to hit.
2
Brawling bonus
Development of close quarter techniques. 
All brawling combat against the Lifeform receives +1 to the roll.
3
Critical hits
Knowledge of vulnerable locations. 
If both the hit and damage roll is a natural 6, the creature is slain 
regardless of KP, Saving Throws, or other defenses.
4
Defensive reduction
Improved understanding of weak points. Reduce any Saving Throws the 
creature has by 1 step. If the creature does not have a Saving Throw, 
treat as a +1 Hit Bonus to all attacks against the creature.

Better understanding of the attack forms of the creature. All characters 
receive a 6+ Saving Throw against all attacks from the Lifeform.
6
Predictive movement
You understand how the creature moves and can anticipate it in combat. 
Reduce the movement speed of the Lifeform by 1”. 

=== PAGE 95 ===
93
Colonies
Each Theory has two costs listed: The first is 
the RP cost to fully research it. The second cost 
listed for each Theory is the RP cost to gain an 
Application in that field (see below).
For example, 5/3 means it costs 5 RP to develop 
the Theory and 3 RP for each Application.
Applications
Each Theory unlocks a number of Applications 
when it is fully paid.
To then acquire an Application, you pay the 
Application research cost. However, there is 
no guarantee that this research produces your 
highest priority outcome first. Instead, when 
the cost has been paid, randomly select from 
the Applications you have not yet discovered to 
see which outcome the research has produced. 
Use any appropriate die type to select. 
 
◆Applications marked with (Bonus) provide 
an increase in colony capabilities.
 
◆(Building) allows a new building to be 
constructed.
 
◆(Milestone Building) indicates that this is 
one of the buildings that must be completed 
to finish the campaign.
 
◆(Weapon) allows a new weapon to be 
deployed by your squads. New weapons are 
available immediately.
ADVANCED MANUFACTURING
Cost: 3/3 RP
For the colony to prosper, it must both employ 
cutting edge manufacturing techniques 
but also adapt them to local conditions and 
materials. Pursuing Advanced Manufacturing 
allows you to bolster the ability of the colony to 
meet its construction goals.
 
◆Damage containment centre (Building)
 
◆High tech manufacturing plant (Building); 
Colony bots receive 1 KP
 
◆Improved construction materials (Bonus): 
+1 to Colony Integrity
 
◆Orbital facility (Milestone Building)
 
◆Resource substitution (Bonus): 
+1 to Colony Integrity
AI THEORIES
Cost: 4/4 RP
With the number of workers strictly limited,  
AI must be pressed into a wider range of fields. 
This field serves to expand your capabilities in a 
variety of supporting fields.
 
◆AI-assisted school (Building); 
Colony bot improves Combat Skill to +1
 
◆Bot maintenance bay (Building)
 
◆Cybernetics facility (Building); 
Colony bot improves Speed to 6”
 
◆Enhanced predictive simulation (Bonus): 
Increase per campaign turn RP by +1
 
◆Social disorder prediction (Bonus): 
Increase Colony Morale by +4
Specific Research (optional rule)
The random roll for Applications reflects that 
your research team has their own agendas and 
ideas. It also lessens attempts to “optimize” the 
path through the game. If you find that to be 
less appealing, as an optional rule pick the exact 
Applications you want to develop. A fun middle 
ground is to roll for the first Application in the 
Theory and then pick from that point onwards. 
Flavoring Research
As with buildings, research topics are really 
game functions, and you should absolutely 
rename them to fit your setting, especially if the 
basic technological assumptions differ markedly 
from those of the Unified Space setting. As 
the author found writing this game, inventing 
technobabble is also a lot of fun!

Colonies
94
INFANTRY EQUIPMENT

Our troops face the need for equipment 
tailored to their specific environment and 
the threat profiles present here. This will 
expand the tactical combat options available 
to our colonists.
 
◆Carver blade (Weapon); 
Sergeant weaponry available as  
Grunt upgrade.
 
◆Kill-Break shotgun (Weapon)
 
◆Ripper pistol (Weapon)
 
◆Shard pistol (Weapon);

◆Steady rifle (Weapon); 
Colony bot able to use Trooper weapons.
Infantry Equipment also acts as a prerequisite 
to developing Adapted Combat Gear (below).
Adapted Combat Gear
Cost: 2/2 RP 
(Prerequisite: Infantry Equipment)
The next step in tactical assimilation is to 
utilize the resources of our new environment 
to produce military hardware. This will further 
expand the tactical combat options available to 
our colonists.

Buildings
Building Basics
Your colony will require a variety of facilities 
and workshops to function and prosper. Most 
are abstracted into the background and don’t 
need tracking, such as habitation or shop stalls, 
but structures that influence campaign play are 
collectively known as Buildings. 
During the Building step of the campaign 
turn you have the opportunity to construct 
new Buildings. Each Building provides 
benefits explained in this chapter. Many have 
prerequisites that need to be met before they 
can be constructed.
In game terms, only one of each specific 
Building can be constructed, representing that 
type of facility becoming available throughout 
the colony as it grows. (In your own mind, your 
colony may feature several such structures, but 
you gain an in-game benefit only for the first of 
its kind – think of it as the administrative hub of 
any further buildings.)
We recommend keeping a drawn map of the 
colony as it develops. 
Construction
Constructing new Buildings is done through 
Build Points (BP). Your colony receives 1 BP 
per campaign turn at the start of the 
campaign, but various factors in the game  
will increase this rate. 
Tally incoming BP, adding them to any you had 
saved up, then spend as many as you like (up 
to the amount you have available) on a single 
new Building. 
Each Building has a BP cost that must be paid 
to construct it. This can be spread over multiple 
campaign turns if required, and you may put 
points towards a different Building next turn 
if needed, even if you have not completed the 
current project yet. Simply track the points you 
have spent so far.
Partially constructed Buildings cannot lose the 
committed BP, and they cannot be reallocated 
once spent. 
You can only put BP towards a single Building 
per campaign turn. 
19

=== PAGE 100 ===
Colonies
98
Raw Materials can be used to increase 
building rates.
You can convert 1 unit of Raw Materials  
into 1 BP, with up to 3 units converted 
per round.
Reclaiming Buildings
In the Building step of the campaign turn, you 
may also choose to reclaim a single building. 
The building is destroyed, and you receive half 
the BP cost, rounded down, in Raw Materials.
Colony Defenses
Some buildings provide Colony Defenses. 
These are rolled for when the colony is 
subjected to an enemy attack. Each point of 
Defenses allows a D6 roll, with 4-6 negating 
one point of Colony Damage. 
The Buildings
What follows is a list of every Building available 
in the game. Each Building lists the BP cost, 
and the rules benefits you receive from 
completing it. 
Also listed are any prerequisite research 
applications that must have been completed to 
access that Building option.

Non-Linear Physics
Educating young colonists to be scientists is an investment in the long-term 
survival of the colony.
Earn +1 Research Point every campaign turn.

shield installation

Locally adapted weapons and hardware require specialized 
manufacturing techniques.

Genetic Advancement
Additional sick beds combined with newly developed medical remedies 
will increase recovery from trauma.
In the Recovery step, you may select any one character and 
reduce their injury recovery time by 1 turn. 
Flavoring Buildings
The buildings are, as far as the game is 
concerned, primarily mechanical features that 
provide you with a new ability or bonus in the 
game system. 
As such the names and background information 
can be taken as inspirational. If you would rather 
reskin a particular building into something 
different that either fits your story or your game 
setting better, please feel free to do so. This sort 
of customizing is a fantastic way of making the 
game feel more like your own.

Genetic Augmentation
As you explore and research this new world, 
you can adapt to it using genetic modification. 
Unity scientists have long perfected “online 
gene modding” – allowing the physical frame 
to be modified and rewritten to add new 
features to a person’s physical form. 
While primary uses have been medicinal and 
fashion-oriented, this ability an be taken to new 
heights when fighting on an alien world.
Several Research applications and buildings 
grant you Augmentation Points. You can 
used these to purchase Augmentations from 
the options below at any point when you have 
accumulated the required points. However,  
you can buy only one per campaign turn. 
The cost is always equal to the number of 
Augmentations you already have, including 
the current selection. For example, the first 
Augmentation in the campaign costs  
1 Augmentation Point, the second costs  
2 Augmentation Points, and so forth.
Once selected, an Augmentation applies to 
every current and future character you have, 
starting from the following campaign turn. 
Bots are unaffected (and if importing characters 
from other campaigns, neither are Soulless).
Note: You typically will not achieve every 
Augmentation in a single campaign. 
Boosted Decision Making
Psionic engineering improves preternatural 
senses.
Each battle round, you may pick up and reroll 
one of your Initiative dice. 
Boosted Recovery
Implantation of carefully selected plant DNA 
accelerates recovery from injury and illness.
When a character is sent to Sick Bay, reduce the 
initial recovery time by 1 turn. This has no effect 
on Grunt characters.
Claws
Retractable claws are convenient for labor tasks 
as well as enhancing defensive capabilities.
All characters count as having a Damage +0 
Melee weapon which cannot be lost in any way.
Enhanced Mobility
Adaptation of animal DNA to improve short-
term running speed.
Characters moving in a straight line may 
increase their base move by 1”. Character Dash 
moves are now 3” instead of the customary 2”.
Enhanced Vision
In addition to improving conventional vision, 
genetic enhancement improves latent psionic 
senses, adapting them to local conditions.
All visibility limits are increased by +3”.
Inherent Protection
Adaptive skin that can harden in response to 
minor injuries, halt bleeding and otherwise 
prolong combat endurance.
All characters receive a 6+ Saving Throw 
against incoming damage. The normal rules 
for combined Saving Throws apply. 
Mental Links
Connecting individuals together in a manner 
that allows rapid, consensus-based decision 
making. A first step towards a new future?
This counts as a Milestone for campaign 
completion purposes.
Selecting this also adds 1 Story Point. 
Psionic Cohesion
Finding ways to improve psionic connections 
despite the natural dampening effect can 
greatly increase cohesion and morale. 
Any character that would lose a level of Loyalty 
rolls 1D6. On 5-6, they do not lose a level.

=== PAGE 110 ===

108
Close Encounters
“We have a lot of contacts out here!”
The voice in the headset was calm on the 
surface, but Theodora knew the tone. They 
wouldn’t have called it in if they didn’t think it 
was a serious problem.
“Hold your position. Me and Jacques will be 
there in a moment!”
She picked up her rifle. Jacques looked over, 
his own rifle in hand. He always had a sense for 
these things. 
Theodora nodded and they jogged to the 
entry hatch, which opened as it detected their 
approach. The blue light of the afternoon sun 
gave the scene an oddly serene feel, in spite 
of the chaos unfolding at the perimeter 
guard post.
She could see two of the security troopers 
down there, frantically aiming and firing into 
the fog at hunched shapes. One shape stood  
up and a moment later a searing bolt of light 
arced towards the guard post, shattering part of 
the barricade. 
“Slyn!”, Theodora shouted to nobody in 
particular. The lizards had attacked them twelve 
times in the last two months and they didn’t 
seem tired of it yet. Jacques fell to one knee 
and started firing into the fog too, the tracer fire 
barely perceptible through the roiling mist. 
Theodora trusted that Jacques could keep them 
pinned down and began moving towards the 
guard post. As she ran, two bolts of light flashed 
past her. They were soundless unless very close, 
in which case there came a sort of high-pitched 
whine or momentary static noise through the 
headsets, giving battle communications a 
strange, choppy ambience.
As she slid into cover besides the troopers, 
Theodora levelled her rifle at a shape in the fog 
and squeezed the trigger. The gun’s computer 
micro-calibrated the aim and followed up 
the shot with two more rounds. The shape 
staggered as the projectiles hit home, but the 
Slyn remained standing. They always did for  
the first strike. 
Theodora spat on the ground and tried to follow 
the wounded creature as it staggered towards 
cover. Somewhere out there was its partner, 
keeping it alive.
As the fog cleared for a moment, she couldn’t 
help but gasp. The high-caliber ammunition 
had torn one of its arms clean off and blood  
was spraying from the creature’s ruptured 
throat. Though they looked like reptiles, they 
were humanoid enough that it was not difficult 
to envision the same wounds on one of her  
own troopers.
There was no sign of the partner even though 
she knew it must be somewhere nearby. She 
mouthed the word “auto” and the helmet AI 
adjusted her rifle. The hail of fire struck the 
shattered Slyn and tore it to pieces. As the gore 
smeared the ground, she thought she saw 
another shape fall. That’s how they worked:  
They were linked in pairs and as long as one 
survived, the other could keep going despite 
grievous wounds. Up to a point, at least.
As she ducked back to reload, she gave a bitter 
smile. You had to admire their loyalty to each 
other. She might even do so later when they 
weren’t busy trying to kill her.

=== PAGE 112 ===
Battlefield Conditions
Every planet selected for exploration has its 
own unique environments and phenomena 
that the field teams must contend with. We 
refer to these as Campaign Conditions (or 
simply Conditions). Although you generate 
Conditions as part of mission briefings, you 
should track these local peculiarities as part of 
the ongoing campaign game.
When you begin the campaign, you will have 
a Campaign Condition table with 10 blank 
entries on it. Every time a mission instructs 
you to determine a Condition, roll D100 and 
consult the table. 
If the roll indicated a blank entry, roll on the 
Master Condition table below, and follow the 
instructions (some entries require additional 
rolls). Write the Condition you have generated 
into the spot on the Campaign Condition table 
exactly as you rolled it. Going forward, that 
result will always be what is written on your 
Campaign Condition table. 
If the roll on the Campaign Condition table 
indicates an entry that has been filled out, you 
receive the same Condition again, including 
any secondary rolls. 
Unless stated otherwise, battlefield conditions 
apply to the enemy as well as your own troops.

Note on Variety
The same Conditions can be added to your 
Campaign Conditions table more than once. You 
can either accept this as a common occurrence 
on your particular world or you can treat the 
second roll as No Condition.

Master Condition table
D100
RESULT
DESCRIPTION
01-08
Visibility limits
Your vision is limited due to impenetrable darkness, fog, psionic 
interference, or visual distortions. This seems to defeat all conventional 
technological solutions. 
The visibility limit is the maximum range at which you can fire on 
targets or carry out any other tasks that requires visibility. Research can 
improve this limit. 
Roll D100 again:
01-30	 Variable by round: Roll 1D6+8 at the beginning of the  
game and again at the end of each battle round to determine 
the limit in inches next round.
31-70	 Fixed: The limit is 1D6+8”. Roll once to determine what it  
will be in every instance this condition applies.
71-00	 Variable by battle: When setting up a battle, roll 1D6+8 
to determine the visibility limit for this battle.
09-13
Shooting penalties
Your ability to track and shoot targets is affected by a natural 
phenomenon such as psionic disturbances, malfunction due to 
background “haywire signals”, clouds of gas, or similar. 
A -1 penalty applies to all ranged attacks in certain circumstances.  
Roll D100 to determine those circumstances:
01-35	 Random by round: Each battle round roll 1D6; on 5-6,  
the penalty applies that round.
36-65	 Range: All attacks over 15” range are penalized.
66-00	 Certain terrain types: When setting up, randomly pick a 
terrain feature. All features of the same broad type (plants, 
rocks, water, etc.) apply the penalty to any shot that originates 
from, targets inside, or passes through the feature.
14-19
Movement penalty
Due to gravity distortion, slippery surfaces, crumbling ground material, 
or grasping plants, you are unable to Dash under certain circumstances. 
Roll D100:
01-25	 Table surface: Anywhere on the table surface that is not a 
terrain feature. Figures in or on terrain features are not affected.
26-60	 Terrain features: Anywhere that is in or on a terrain feature.
61-80	 Climbing: While climbing up or down things.
81-00	 Crossing obstacles: When crossing any kind of obstacle.

Uncertain terrain 
features
Long range scans are particularly unreliable here.
If you use the Uncertain Terrain Features optional rule (see page 137),

result is instead treated as “Unstable Terrain,” below.
26-30
Unstable terrain
Many seemingly solid features seem extremely unstable, and the 
slightest noise could collapse them.
When setting up, randomly select a terrain feature. All features of 
the same general type (rock, plant, etc.) are unstable. Each time a  
figure moves adjacent to or on the feature, or is fired upon while on  
the feature, roll 1D6. On a 1, the feature collapses and is removed 
from the table. Any figures that were in or on the feature will be 
knocked Sprawling.
31-35
Shifting terrain
At the end of each battle round, randomly select a terrain feature and 
then move it 1D6” in a random direction. Features can move off the 
table, in which case they are simply gone. Figures in or on the feature 
move with it but must each roll 4+ on 1D6 or be knocked Sprawling 
(see page 38). 
Features will stop if they would otherwise collide with each other.

Hazardous 
environment
Randomly select two terrain features which become Impassable.
42-49
Resource rich
If you win the mission, roll one additional time on the Post Mission 
Find table.
This roll CAN be a duplicate of a result you obtained from any rolls 
normally awarded by a mission.
50-55
Heavy scanner signals
If the mission uses Contacts, place one additional Contact marker 
initially. This has no other effect.
56-61

roll D100 again.
01-55	 Modify the Aggression die by +1 when testing for Contacts.
56-00	 Modify the Aggression die by -1 when testing for Contacts.
This has no other effect.
62-68
Enemy activity
The area is heavily patrolled, or the enemy is not here in great numbers.
This affects the enemy number encountered when fighting Tactical 
Enemies. Roll D100 again:
01-45	 Encounter sizes reduced by 1.
46-00	 Encounter sizes increased by 1.

Drifting clouds
Great clouds of gas, vapor, or pollen drift through the area.
When setting up the battle, place three cloud markers 6” from the 
center of the table in random directions. Each produces a 2” radius 
cloud. These clouds cannot be fired through. Figures within a cloud  
can fire and be fired upon but it counts as cover. 
At the end of each round, roll 1D6” and a random direction. Every cloud 
drifts the same distance and direction.

01-70	 Safe: The clouds interfere with visibility, but are not dangerous.
71-85	 Toxic: Each battle, roll two D6 and pick the highest of the

that makes base contact with a toxic cloud at any point must

become a Casualty / lose 1 KP.

Clear escape paths

Confined spaces
Randomly select two points on the battlefield edge when you set up 
terrain. The table can only be entered or exited at these two points.

All clear! No special conditions apply.

Missions

=== PAGE 116 ===
Mission Briefings
Mission Types
A mission, in game terms, means that you 
will be playing out a tabletop battle with your 
miniatures. Some missions are selected by 
you while others are mandated by events 
in the game. If you end up with multiple 
mandated missions, randomly pick which one 
you will be playing.
When conducting a mission, you will assemble 
a team of characters from your roster, as 
explained in the “Lock and Load” step of the 
campaign turn sequence (see page 65). The 
number of characters will depend on the 
mission in question. 
There is always one primary mission every 
campaign turn. If none are mandated, you 
must select a mission to play.
Enemy Deployments
Each mission gives a general guide to how the 
enemy is set up, but this is provided in broad 
terms since it has to fit on a near-infinite array 
of gaming tables.
As the player you are responsible for the 
exact placements of each enemy figure. A 
few guidelines can be kept in mind when 
you are deploying to prevent the game from 
becoming unsatisfactory.  
Enemy figures should always set up as if they 
are ready for combat. This means they should 
be set up in or behind cover or behind terrain 
features that conceal them, whenever possible. 
If there are positions of cover within 3” of the 
intended deployment point, set them up in the 
covered position instead.
If there is high ground available for the enemy 
to deploy on, try to position about a third of the 
enemy on raised positions. The same 3” rule 
can be applied here. 
If you are setting up the enemy after placing 
your own figures, all enemies should be placed 
so they are in cover or out of sight.
When placing individual figures in a group, 
place them with figures about 1” apart. 
Specialists and leader figures should be about 
1” behind the regular grunts. 
Enemies arriving during the game are usually 
placed at the center of a battlefield edge. They 
will arrive at that location even if it otherwise 
lacks cover or puts them directly in sight of 
your characters. If multiple characters arrive at 
the same time, deploy them in a line with 1” 
between figures and the midpoint of the line 
being on the center of the battlefield edge.
Terrain Placement
Each mission will indicate if any particular 
features must be placed on the table. You 
can consult page 138 for general guidelines 
on how to build a good wargames table and 
page 143 if you want to randomly roll up your 
tabletop terrain.
Objectives and LoS
The Mission descriptions below 
include Objectives, an object or location usually 
represented on the table by an Objective 
marker. Note that Objective markers themselves 
do not “exist” within the game world: They do 
not affect Line of Sight or movement in any way, 
though any terrain feature they are placed within 
will have the normal effects on Line of Sight. 
Missions
114

=== PAGE 117 ===
Investigation Mission
Long range scans show interesting sights in this 
sector. Your team is being deployed to search 
the area, look for features of interest, and secure 
any valuable finds. You are going in quick with 
a smaller team, trying to avoid detection by any 
potential hostiles.
Map Location
The map sector must be an Investigation site. 
Table Setup
Use a 3x3-foot table populated with terrain. 
This type of mission will look better the more 
weird and alien terrain there is, particularly if 
you have odd-looking buildings or ruins to use.
Place a Discovery marker in the center of each 
table quarter. If a terrain feature covers the 
exact location, place the marker in the center of 
that terrain feature instead.
If you wish to use the Uncertain Features rule, 
place one in each table quarter.
There are no Battlefield Conditions.
Player Forces
You will deploy 4 characters from your roster. 
Select a battlefield edge and roll 1D6. On 4+, 
you may arrive from this edge, otherwise pick 
randomly from the remaining edges. 
Your crew sets up within 1” of each other and 
within 1” of the battlefield edge. 
Opposition
Due to your quick approach, the Slyn will not 
attack you.
Place 1 Contact marker at the center of each of 
the battlefield edges you did not deploy from. 
The first time a Contact is revealed to be hostile 
Lifeforms, roll on your Campaign Lifeform 
Encounters table to determine the encounter. If 
the result is a blank entry, generate the creature 
now and add it to your encounter table.
At the start of each Enemy Phase, label the 
battlefield edges 1 to 3, excluding your 
deployment edge. Roll 1D6. If the die result 
matches an edge, place a Contact there. If the 
die is a 4, 5, or 6, do not place a Contact.
Objectives
Your goal is to search the area for interesting 
sights. Each of the Discovery markers can 
be investigated by moving within 2” of the 
marker. The marker is removed, and you 
immediately roll 1D6 on the table below:
Mission Objectives table
1D6
OBJECTIVE
DESCRIPTION
1
Enemy
Enemy sentries! Place a Sleeper (see page 154) in the most distant 
terrain feature the investigating figure can see. 
2
Rewards
You tag the location for later extraction. You may roll once on the Post-Mission 
Finds table (see page 134) after the mission is over.
3
Contact
Lifeforms are lurking here. Place a Contact in the terrain feature closest 
to where the Discovery marker was. 
4
Action required
A closer look is required. A character must move into base contact and take an 
action. Roll 1D6+Savvy. A score of 5+ will earn you 1 unit of Raw Materials.
5-6
Data
You have found Mission Data. A character must move into base contact and 
spend an action to collect the data. It is then transmitted to your colony where 
it will be analyzed during the post-battle campaign sequence.

=== PAGE 118 ===
Ending the Mission
The mission ends when your squad is no longer 
on the table, either due to being casualties or 
moving off any battlefield edge. You are not 
required to exit from the edge you arrived from. 
Rewards
You receive any rewards you acquired  
during the mission. If you completed at least 
1 Discovery marker, the mission is no longer 
marked as an Investigation site on your 
campaign map.
Note that investigating a sector does not mean 
it has been Explored or Exploited. 
Scouting Mission
A small scouting mission is being deployed 
to assess the area for further exploration and 
exploitation.
Note: This is an alternative to the Scout Action 
in the campaign sequence, allowing you to 
speed the process of determining the Resource 
and Hazard levels of map spaces.
Map Location
Any sector that has not been Explored and 
which is not an Investigation site. 
Table Setup
Use a  2x2-foot table. Populate it with lots of 
terrain. 
After setting up, put a Recon marker in the  
6 largest terrain features. 
If you use the Uncertain Features rule, place 
one in each table quarter. Battlefield Conditions 
are not used in this mission.
Player Forces
You may deploy 2 characters from your roster. 
You are not required to deploy a scout, but it is 
highly recommended. 
Select a battlefield edge and roll 1D6. On 4+, 
you may arrive from this edge, otherwise pick 
randomly from the remaining edges. 
Your crew sets up within 1” of each other and 
within 1” of the battlefield edge. 
Opposition
As you are deploying quietly, the Slyn will not 
attack you. 
Place 1 Contact marker in the center of the 
table. 
The first time a Contact is revealed to be 
hostile Lifeforms, roll on the Campaign 
Lifeform Encounters table to determine what 
you encounter. If the result is a blank entry, 
generate the creature now and add it to your 
encounter table.
At the start of each Enemy Phase, roll 1D6.  
On a 6, another Contact is placed in the center 
of a random battlefield edge. 
For this mission, when rolling for Contact 
movement, if the Aggression and Random 
scores are equal, do not place a new Contact. 
Instead, the Contact remains in place and takes 
no actions. 
Objectives
You are here to collect recon data. To do this, 
you must move into base contact with the 
Recon markers. 
A scout can recon automatically by moving 
into base contact. Any other character must 
move into base contact and roll 1D6+Savvy.  
A 5+ succeeds. On a failed test, the recon is  
not possible. 
Recon data is sent back to your colony 
automatically.
Ending the Mission
The mission ends when your squad is no longer 
on the table, either due to being casualties 
or moving off any battlefield edge. You do 
not have to exit via a specific edge. Typically, 
once you have completed your tasks, you will 
move to evacuate off the battlefield. Any crew 
member that leaves the table is assumed to 
return safely to your vehicle.

=== PAGE 119 ===
Rewards
For each Recon marker successfully 
investigated, roll 1D6. For every 6, the 
Resource level of the sector is increased  
by +1. 
If you cleared at least 3 Recon markers, you 
may also roll for a Post-Mission Find (see  
page 134)
Campaign Factors
The sector is considered to have been Explored.
Roll two D6 and pick the lowest number, then 
add any bonuses from the Rewards section.  
This is the Resource level.
Roll two D6 and pick the lowest number. 
This is the Hazard level. 
Mark the values on the map.
Exploration Mission
You are tasked with sweeping the area and 
dealing with any opposition or hazards that 
present themselves. The job may or may not be 
a simple one – you won’t know until you land. 
Map Location
The Exploration mission takes place in any  
map sector that your scouts have explored,  
but which has not yet been Exploited. 
Table Setup
Use a  3x3-foot table populated with terrain. 
Place Objective markers equal to the Resource 
Value of the map sector, distributed around the 
table fairly evenly.
Objectives will look best if they are in or near 
interesting terrain features.  
If you wish to use the optional Uncertain 
Features rule, place 2 Uncertain Features on 
the table.
Roll once for Battlefield Conditions. 
117
Missions

=== PAGE 120 ===
Player Forces
Deploy 6 characters from your roster. 
Randomly select a battlefield edge (if your table 
is placed against a wall, just pick from the edges 
you can sit at). Your crew sets up anywhere you 
like within 4” of the selected battlefield edge.
Opposition
After deploying, roll 2D6. If the roll is 2-4, you 
are attacked by the Slyn.
Determine the opposition as explained on  
page 152. The Slyn will set up in randomly 
selected terrain features near the opposite 
battlefield edge. Locate the 6 terrain features 
closest to that battlefield edge and roll 
randomly for each Slyn pair to see which they 
deploy into.
On any other result, the threat is from wildlife, 
and the Contact system is used (page 41). Place 
a number of initial Contact markers equal to 
the Hazard level. Each Contact is placed in the 
nearest terrain feature to the center of the table, 
one Contact per feature. 
The first time a Contact is revealed to be 
hostile Lifeforms, roll on your Campaign 
Lifeform Encounters table to determine what 
you encounter. If the result is a blank entry, 
generate the creature now and add it to your 
encounter table.
Objectives
Your objective is to conduct a sweep of the area 
to assess it for value to the colony. The Objective 
markers represent locations that the scout 
teams have assessed will need to be scanned to 
determine their resource worth. 
If the Slyn are attacking, they are carrying out 
scans of their own. Should they succeed, they 
will attack the area again, preventing your 
colonists from exploiting any natural resources.
Each Enemy Phase, a random Slyn pair will 
move towards the nearest Objective to their 
current position. The rest will move and fight to 
engage your forces. 
To sweep an Objective marker you must end 
a battle round with a figure within 3” and no 
enemies closer than you. The Slyn use the same 
rules to secure Objectives for themselves.
For both sides, only one Objective can be 
secured by each side in one battle round. If you 
are in range of two or more Objectives, you  
can choose which one to secure. The Slyn pick 
at random. 
Once secured, an Objective is removed from 
the table. 
Lifeforms will ignore the Objectives. 
Ending the Mission
The mission ends when your squad is no longer 
on the table, either due to being casualties 
or moving off any battlefield edge. You do 
not have to exit via a specific edge. Typically, 
once you have completed your tasks, you will 
move to evacuate off the battlefield. Any crew 
member that leaves the table is assumed to 
return safely to your vehicle.
If you are fighting the Slyn, the mission also 
ends if you drive them off. If the Slyn abandon 
the battle, you can claim any remaining 
Objectives that were not claimed already. 
Rewards
You may roll on the Post-Mission Find table 
(page 134) once per Objective you completed. 
Campaign Factors
Roll 1D6 for each Objective you complete. 
 
◆Every 1 increases the Hazard level by  
+1 point. 
 
◆Every 2 has no effect.
 
◆Every 3-6 reduces the Resource value of the 
sector by -1 point. Once the Resources reach 
0, the sector is Exploited and can no longer 
be Explored or subject to Science missions.
Special Rules
No additional special rules apply.

Science Mission
You are carrying out a quick mission to obtain 
scientific samples, hopefully without attracting 
too much attention.
Map Location
Any sector that has a Resource value of  
1 or more.
Table Setup
Use a  2x2-foot table. Populate it with lots of 
terrain. 
After setting up, put a Science marker (use an 
Objective marker or Delve Device marker) in the 
6 largest terrain features. 
The Uncertain Features and Battle Conditions 
rules are not used in this mission.
Player Forces
You may deploy 2 characters from your roster. 
You are not required to deploy a scientist, but it 
is highly recommended. 
Select a battlefield edge and roll 1D6. On 4+, 
you may arrive from this edge, otherwise pick 
randomly from the remaining edges. 
Your crew sets up within 1” of each other and 
within 1” of the battlefield edge. 
Opposition
As you are deploying quietly, the Slyn will not 
attack you and you should be able to avoid 
most opposition. 
Place 1 Contact marker in the center of the 
table. 
The first time a Contact is revealed to be 
hostile Lifeforms, roll on your Campaign 
Lifeform Encounters table to determine what 
you encounter. If the result is a blank entry, 
generate the creature now and add it to your 
encounter table.
At the start of each Enemy Phase, roll 1D6  
(2D6 if the Hazard level is 4+). On a 6, place 
another Contact marker in the center of a 
random battlefield edge. If this results in two 
Contacts being placed, roll randomly for the 
placement of each.
For this mission, when rolling for Contact 
movement, if the Aggression and Random 
scores are equal, do not place a new Contact. 
Instead, the Contact remains in place and takes 
no actions. 
Objectives
You are here to collect scientific samples. To do 
this, you must move into base contact with the 
Science markers. 
A scientist can collect the sample automatically 
by moving into base contact with it. Any other 
character must roll 1D6+Savvy. A 5+ succeeds 
and collects the sample. On a failed test, the 
sample is ruined. 
Once collected, the sample data is 
automatically sent back to your colony. 
Ending the Mission
The mission ends when your squad is no 
longer on the table, either due to being 
casualties or moving off any battlefield edge. 
You do not have to exit via a specific edge. 
Typically, once you have completed your tasks, 
you will move to evacuate off the battlefield. 
Any crew member that leaves the table is 
assumed to return safely to your vehicle.
Rewards
For each sample collected, roll 1D6. On 4-6, 
you receive 1 Research Point. If you roll more 
than a single 6 when doing this, you also 
receive 1 unit of Mission Data. 
If you cleared at least 3 Science markers,  
you may also roll for a Post-Mission Find  
(see page 134).
Campaign Factors
If you collected any samples, the Resource 
value of the sector is reduced by 1.

Hunt Mission
The researchers want you to acquire a specimen 
of a local Lifeform for investigation.
Map Location
Select any map sector within 4 spaces of your 
colony.
Table Setup
Use a  3x3-foot table populated with terrain.
If you wish to use the optional Uncertain 
Features rule, place 2 Uncertain Features on 
the table.
Roll once for Battlefield Conditions. 
Player Forces
You will deploy up to 6 figures which can be 
any combination of characters from your roster 
or grunts. 
Select a battlefield edge and deploy your 
characters anywhere within 3” of the edge. 
Opposition
After deploying, roll 2D6. If the roll is 2-4, you 
are attacked by the Slyn.
Determine the opposition as explained on page 
152. The Slyn will set up in randomly selected 
terrain features near the opposite battlefield 
edge. Locate the 6 terrain features closest to 
that battlefield edge and roll randomly for each 
Slyn pair to see which they deploy into.
On any other result, you will be hunting for 
Lifeforms.
Roll 1D6 for each terrain feature that any of 
your characters have a Line of Sight to. Each 6 
results in a Contact being placed in or directly 
behind the feature. As you play the game, the 
first time each terrain feature is spotted for the 
first time, the same roll is made to determine 
if it contains a Contact. The mission will always 
feature at least 3 Contacts. If not enough have 
been generated, the last 1-3 terrain features to 
be spotted will each house a Contact.
The first time a Contact is revealed to be 
hostile Lifeforms, one scout on the table may

attempt to roll 1D6+Savvy. A 5+ means you

If the Slyn are attacking, you are trying to fight

If you are facing Lifeforms, the goal is to kill two

Special Rules

Patrol Mission
Survival on a hostile world requires vigilance. 
You send out a team to conduct a patrol near the 
colony to keep everyone safe and have a look at 
conditions in the field. 
Map Location
Select any map sector within 2 squares of your 
colony. The sector must not be enemy-occupied.
Note: You are patrolling a part of the sector that 
your colonists have begun utilizing. The Hazard 
and Resource scores are not used in a routine 
patrol and completing the patrol does not 
make the sector Exploited.
Table Setup
Use a  3x3-foot table populated with terrain.
Place a few features to represent the colony, 
such as a parked vehicle, building outpost, 
fencing, or similar. (A few work robots always 
help give a fun science fiction vibe.)

Uncertain Features should not be used.

Player Forces
You will deploy 2 characters from your roster

anywhere you like within 4” of the selected

The Slyn will set up in randomly selected terrain 
features near the opposite battlefield edge. 
Locate the 6 terrain features closest to that 
battlefield edge and roll randomly for each Slyn 
pair to see which they deploy into.

Skirmish
Enemy activity has been intensifying, and it is 
time to fight back. You deploy from your shuttle 
to take on the enemy directly. 
Map Location
Any map sector that is occupied by the enemy. 
Table Setup
Use a  3x3-foot table populated with terrain. 
Roll 1D6 twice on the Skirmish Objective 
table below to determine what you are here to 
do. Each objective is placed randomly 1D6+1” 
from the center of the table.
If you wish to use the optional Uncertain 
Features rule, place 2 Uncertain Features on 
the table.
Roll once for Battlefield Conditions.
Several Objectives depend on carrying items. 
A character can carry any number of mission 
items. If the character is a casualty, the 
item is dropped where they fell and can be 
picked up by moving into base contact with 
it. Retrieving or deploying an item does not 
require an action.
Items are sensitive and cannot be voluntarily 
dropped or handed off. The enemy always 
ignores any items on the ground.
Player Forces
You may deploy 4 characters from your roster 
as well as a full fireteam of 4 grunts.
Randomly select a battlefield edge (if your 
table is placed against a wall, just pick from 
the edges you can sit at). Your crew sets up 
anywhere you like within 4” of the selected 
battlefield edge.
Opposition
The Slyn will not interfere in this mission.
Your opposition is generated normally using 
the appropriate Tactical Enemy type (see 
page 148). 
Select the 6 terrain features closest to the 
opposite battlefield edge from where you set 
up. For each enemy, roll a D6 to determine 
which terrain feature they begin the game 
within (directly behind if unable to be placed 
within the feature).
Skirmish Objective table
