Missions

=== PAGE 126 ===
Objectives
The enemies ignore the Objectives and will 
focus on killing your squad. 
Ending the Mission
The mission ends when your squad is no longer 
on the table, either due to being casualties or 
moving off any battlefield edge. You do not 
have to exit a specific edge, unless a Battlefield 
Condition mandates it. Typically, once you 
have completed your tasks, you will move to 
evacuate off the battlefield. Any crew member 
that leaves the table is assumed to return safely 
to your vehicle.
Rewards
You may roll once on the Post-Mission Finds 
table if you complete the mission. 
Campaign Factors
If you complete both Objectives, the enemy 
will no longer occupy this map sector. If this 
was their last sector, they move to a random 
adjacent sector. Note that you cannot destroy 
the final map sector occupied by a particular 
enemy without playing the Assault mission.
Special Rules
The longer the mission takes, the more 
enemies are likely to arrive. At the end of each 
battle round, roll 1D6. If you have completed 
any Objectives, roll an additional D6 for each 
completed Objective. 
 
◆For every 6 rolled, a regular enemy arrives 
and is placed at the center of a random 
battlefield edge. All enemies arriving at 
the same time will arrive from the same 
battlefield edge. Since you are in enemy 
territory, they may deploy behind you. 
 
◆If you roll two 6s at once, one of the 
enemies placed will be a specialist and the 
other a regular enemy. 
 
◆If you roll three 6s, the enemy receives 
one regular, one specialist, and one 
Leader enemy. 
Rescue Mission
Colonists have sent up an SOS signal, and your 
squad is headed out to rescue them before they 
get overwhelmed. 
Map Location
Select any map sector within 3 squares of  
your colony. 
Table Setup
Use a  2x2-foot table populated with terrain.
Place three colonists in the center of the table 
(you can use figures or markers to represent 
them). If possible, place them near a wrecked 
vehicle, crater, a piece of machinery, or a small 
colony building. 
The Uncertain Features option is not used.
Roll once for Battlefield Conditions. 
Player Forces
You may deploy a total of 6 figures which can 
be any combination of characters from your 
roster and grunts. Grunts are organized into 
fireteams normally with any grunts past 4 
forming their own team.
Your squad deploys from the shuttle directly 
into the action. Set up your squad within 3” of 
the center of the table. 
Opposition
The Slyn will not interfere in this mission.
You are fighting Lifeforms. Place 2 Contacts at 
the center of each battlefield edge. 
The first time a Contact is revealed to be 
hostile Lifeforms, roll on the Campaign 
Lifeform Encounters table to determine what 
you encounter. If the result is a blank entry, 
generate the creature now and add it to your 
encounter table.
Missions
124

=== PAGE 127 ===
Objectives
The Objective is to save the colonists. A colonist 
is saved if they exit any battlefield edge and 
were within 3” of a squad member either at the 
start of that move or at the point of exit. 
As your shuttle pilot will circle to pick up, you 
can exit across any edge.
A colonist that exits without being near a squad 
member must roll 1D6. On 5-6, they make it 
to the nearby shuttle and are saved, otherwise 
they are killed by animals. 
Ending the Mission
The mission ends when your squad and the 
colonists are no longer on the table, either 
due to being casualties or moving off any 
battlefield edge.
Rewards
No special rewards.
Campaign Factors
You will lose 1 Colony Morale for each colonist 
that is not saved. (note that if you opt not to 
carry out the mission when generated by the 
Scout Discovery table, you lose 3 points, as all 
of the colonists are assumed to perish). 
Squad casualties do not affect Colony Morale 
during a Rescue mission. 
Special Rules
At the end of each battle round, roll 1D6 
for each battlefield edge. On 6, a Contact is 
placed in the center of that edge. Contacts 
cannot exit the table in this mission and will 
halt in place instead.
The colonists have civvy stats (see page 18) and 
act as part of your squad. They do not carry any 
weapons though they could pick up a weapon 
from one of your casualties if you choose to 
allow this.
Scout Down! 
One of your scouts have been shot down with 
enemies closing in. You deploy to rescue them. 
Map Location
Select any unexplored map sector.
Table Setup
Use a  2x2-foot table populated with terrain.
Place a scout in the center of the table. If 
possible, place them near a wrecked vehicle or 
crater to represent where they were shot down.
The Uncertain Features option is not used.
Roll once for Battlefield Conditions. 
Player Forces
You may deploy a total of 6 characters which 
can be any combination of characters from 
your roster and grunts. Grunts are organized 
into fireteams normally, with any grunts past 4 
forming their own team.
Select any battlefield edge you like. Your squad 
moves on to the table from the edge in the first 
battle round. 
Opposition
Roll 2D6. On 2-3, the Slyn are attacking. 
The Slyn will set up in randomly selected terrain 
features near the opposite battlefield edge. 
Locate the 6 terrain features closest to that 
battlefield edge and roll randomly for each Slyn 
pair to see which they deploy into.
If the Slyn do not attack, you are fighting 
Tactical Enemies: Roll for the enemy strength 
normally. The enemy will be whoever is closest 
on the map or can be picked at random.
In the event you do not have any Tactical 
Enemies on the game map, randomly pick an 
encounter from the Tactical Enemies list (see 
page 149). This does not add that enemy to 
your map – you are fighting a small raiding 
party or patrol group.

Pitched Battle
The enemy is launching an attack on you and 
will have to fight to see them off. Significant 
damage to the colony can ensue.
Map Location
Your colony sector.
Table Setup
Table size: A 3x3-foot table populated with 
terrain.
The table should have several colony-style 
features such as buildings, parked vehicles, 
cultivated fields and similar.
The Uncertain Features option is not used.
As the battle takes place within the colony 
perimeter, Battlefield Conditions are not used. 
Player Forces
You may deploy up to 4 characters from your 
roster and up to 8 grunts, formed into two 
fireteams.

anywhere within 8” of that battlefield edge. 
You may place small barricades or fox holes for 
your characters to set up within. 
Opposition
You are fighting Tactical Enemies. The Slyn  
will not interfere in this mission.
The enemy will consist of two enemy forces 
each with their own leaders and specialists. 
Each force is generated normally using the 
Tactical Enemies rules (see page 148).
When rolling for the first force, reduce the 
number encountered by 1. 
When rolling for the second force, increase it 
by 1.
The enemy moves onto the table from the 
opposite battlefield edge from where you set 
up. Divide the battlefield edge with 3 markers 
fairly equally distanced from each other.

Opposition
You are fighting Tactical Enemies. The Slyn  
will not interfere in this mission.
Roll normally for the enemy numbers, then 
add 2 additional enemies to the number 
generated. 
If you have tracked an enemy Boss using 
Enemy Information, the enemy will be 
accompanied by a Boss who is in addition to 
the regular opposition. The Boss is a leader with 
2 KP, +1 Combat Skill and +1 Toughness. 
If the Strike mission is in response to an enemy 
patrol, you will face the normal number of 
enemies. There will always be one Leader.
The Boss, if present, sets up in the center of 
the table. If a Boss is not present, the Leader 
is set up in the center instead. Set up a third 
of the remaining enemies within 3” of the 
centers, then set up the rest in pairs with 
each pair placed 2D6” from the center in a 
random direction.
Objectives
You must either defeat the Boss (if present) 
or Leader (otherwise) in a brawl or kill them 
and move a character to the location where 
they fell. This allows you to secure the required 
data. You may then end the mission by 
retreating or fighting it out. You do not have to 
move the character that collected the data to a 
battlefield edge. 
If the enemy Boss/Leader becomes a casualty, 
during each Enemy Phase, the nearest enemy 
will try to move to where they fell. An enemy 
reaching the casualty location picks up the data 
and will then move as fast as possible towards 
the nearest battlefield edge to escape.
Ending the Mission
The mission ends when your squad is no 
longer on the table, or every enemy has  
been defeated. 
Rewards
 
◆If you secure the data from a Boss, you 
now know the location of the enemy 
Strongpoint. 
 
◆If you secure the data from a Leader, 
you receive one additional Enemy 
Information. 
Campaign Factors
No special effects.
Special Rules
Any squad member that only moves but does 
not take an action is Undetected, until they 
either take an action or an enemy has Line of 
Sight to them outside of cover at any point.
The enemy AI works differently in this mission. 
Enemies will fight defensively remaining in 
cover and firing on you. Enemies that are out of 
range will move towards positions where they 
can get lines of fire and then halt there while 
they are in range and sight.
The enemy will ignore Undetected squad 
members for all purposes. 
Assault Mission
It is time. You have tracked down the enemy 
Strongpoint and are ready to take it by storm. 
Check the ammunition one last time – it will be 
a hot landing.
Map Location
A sector containing the enemy Strongpoint.
Table Setup
Use a  3x3-foot table populated with terrain.
The table will look best if it features a lot 
of structures and signs of habitation such 
as crates, barrels, camp site elements, and 
machinery. Alternatively, military-style terrain 
such as trenches, barricades, and sandbag walls 
also work well, and both can be combined for a 
war-like table feel.

128

=== PAGE 131 ===
If you have floor tiles available, you could 
make part of the scenario take place indoors or 
underground as well. 
Mark off roughly 12”x12” as the core of the 
enemy base. This should be the most densely 
populated with urban style terrain.
Uncertain Features and Battlefield Conditions 
are not used in this scenario.
Player Forces
You may deploy up to 6 characters from your 
roster and up to 8 grunts formed into two 
fireteams. 
You set up AFTER the enemy has been placed. 
Choose any battlefield edge you like and set up 
within 3”. 
Opposition
You are fighting Tactical Enemies. The Slyn will 
not interfere in this mission.
There is no roll for the enemy numbers.
Start with the maximum enemy encounter 
size (for example if this is normally 1D3+5, it 
would be 8). Then add 2 additional regular 
enemies, 2 additional specialists, and 1 
additional leader. The additional leader 
receives +1 KP.
The enemy is deployed fairly evenly throughout 
the central 12”x12” area, in positions of cover. 
They should cover each approach evenly as if 
positioned as sentries. 
Objectives
The mission is a straight fight to the death. Kill 
or drive off everybody you encounter.
Ending the Mission
The mission ends when your squad is no longer 
on the table or every enemy has been defeated. 
Rewards
You may roll twice on the Post-Battle Finds 
table if you complete the mission.
Campaign Factors
If you win, you eliminate this enemy from 
the campaign. All sectors they occupy are 
relinquished. You may also immediately roll for 
the Resource and Hazard values of this map 
sector, as if it had just been Explored.
If you are defeated or retreat off the table, the 
mission is a failure. The enemy will be restored 
to full strength the next time you attack them. 
In addition, a random map sector adjacent to 
the enemy Strongpoint becomes immediately 
occupied by the enemy you attacked. 
Special Rules
 
◆At the start of the first Enemy Phase, the 
enemy is checked for their state of readiness.
 
◆Any enemy that had a Line of Sight to a crew 
figure that fired a weapon or could see a 
crew member with no intervening cover at 
any point is ready and may activate normally.
 
◆Any enemy that did not meet one of those 
conditions is not ready and cannot activate 
in the first game turn. They are either still 
scanning for targets, unaware of the attack or 
confused about what is going on. 
 
◆From battle round two onwards every enemy 
becomes ready and will fight normally. 
All enemy Panic ranges are reduced by 1 as 
they are defending their home turf. 
The enemy AI is defensive in this mission:
 
◆Enemies will remain in cover and fire on 
you when possible. Enemies that are out of 
range will move towards positions where 
they can get lines of fire and then halt there 
while they are in range and sight.
 
◆The enemy will try to defend their initial 
positions. They will move to brawl if they 
have a higher combat bonus and you are 
within one move. 
 
◆Should the enemy be taking fire from 
unreachable opponent, they will try to move 
to positions where they cannot be seen.

The enemy is willing to sell themselves 
dearly and the longer the fight drags on, the

Delve Mission
You have located and gained access to a 
site left behind by inhabitants of this world, 
thousands of years ago. As you enter, strange 
power systems spring to life and indecipherable 
glyphs illuminate the halls.
Map Location
A map sector with an Ancient Site.
Table Setup
Use a  3x3-foot table.
The table should be set up to resemble the 
interior of an ancient alien facility. You can use 
factory or urban style terrain or build strange-
looking walls and halls. If you have floor tiles, 
you can also use them instead, though in this 
case make sure to have plenty of rooms and 
connecting corridors. When playing on floor 
tiles, we suggest measuring distances normally 
instead of converting to grid movement. You 
can even combine tiles and regular terrain to 
represent a delve that combines labyrinthine 
and open terrain.
After setting up the terrain, place a Delve 
Device marker in the center of each table 
quarter. These represent machinery that you 
must interact with to unlock the location of 
the artifact. You can shift these somewhat if 
they would look better within or adjacent to a 
particular terrain feature. 
There are no Uncertain Features or Battlefield 
Conditions. 
Player Forces
You may deploy 6 characters from your roster. 
Randomly select a point along any battlefield 
edge by rolling a random direction die in the 
center of the table. The indicated point is where 
your characters enter from during the first 
battle round.
At the end of the first round, randomly 
determine a second point along any battlefield 
edge. This is an additional exit that your 
scanners pick up.
Opposition
In the first Enemy Phase, place 4 Delve Hazard 
markers on the table with 1 marker in each 
table quarter. Each is placed 1D6” randomly 
from the center of the table quarter. If this 
would place one within 3” of a crew member, 
move it towards the center of the table until it is 
no longer within 3”.
At the start of each Enemy Phase, if there are 
currently less than 4 Delve Hazard markers, 
place one addition marker in the center of 
the table (or as close as possible). Only one new 
marker is placed per round and only if there is 
not currently 4 on the table.
From Round 2 onwards, each Delve Hazard is 
moved 3” in a random direction, halting if they 
make base contact with an obstacle. 
Delve Hazards are revealed if at any point a 
crew member is within 3” and Line of Sight. 
Roll 1D6 immediately:

Place a Sleeper figure 
(see page 154). 
The Sleeper will 
immediately fire 
its weapon.

D100
DELVE 
TRAP
EFFECTS
01-08
Turret
Place something to indicate a gun turret where the Delve Hazard was. 
At the end of each Enemy Phase it fires at the closest visible non-Sleeper, 
non-bot figure. Roll to hit with +0 Combat Skill. 
It strikes with +0 Damage but negates Saving Throws. 
The turret can be knocked out. Attackers must roll a 6 to hit with ranged attacks 
or a 5+ with a Melee attack (add Combat Skill to the roll normally).  
A damage roll of 4+ destroys the turret.
A scientist moving into base contact can shut down the turret by rolling 
4+ on a Savvy test.
09-19
Paralysis
The character that revealed the Delve Hazard cannot move or take any actions 
when next activated. They may roll 1D6+Toughness with a score of 8+ shaking 
off the paralysis. Scouts may roll twice and take the best die result. 
20-30
Blockage
The area within 1” of the Delve Hazard becomes Impassable. A scout may move 
into base contact to remove the Blockage by rolling 5+ on a Savvy test. 
31-40
Laser beam
The character that revealed the Delve Hazard takes a weapons hit with  
+0 Damage.
41-46
Explosive trap
Every character within 3” of the Delve Hazard takes a weapons hit with  
+0 damage. If there is partial cover between a character and the Hazard,  
a 4+ roll on 1D6 is required to score a hit.
47-60
Sleepers!
Randomly place 2 Sleepers within 6” of the Hazard. 
They do not activate this round. 
61-68
Alarm
Until the alarm is shut down, a Sleeper arrives at the center of a random 
battlefield edge each future Enemy Phase. Shutting down the alarm requires 
moving into base contact with the Delve Hazard and rolling 6+ on a Savvy test.
69-80
Gas
Everyone within 1” of the Delve Hazard is affected by the gas. 
At the start of the Enemy Phase in the next battle round, the gas expands to 
2” radius and then 3” radius during the following battle round.
Affected characters must roll 5+ to avoid being overcome. Scouts may add +1 
to the roll. Overcome characters will move in a random direction when they next 
activate and cannot take any actions. If an overcome character finishes their 
move outside the gas cloud, they must roll 3+ to recover (+1 bonus for scouts). 
81-92
Security 
systems
Place one additional Delve Hazard marker in the center of the table and one 
at the center of a random battlefield edge. 
93-00
Lock-down
One of the Delve Device (determined randomly) is locked down. 
To unlock it, you must make base contact with it and roll 6+ on a Savvy test. 
Once successful, you can then roll on the normal Delve Device table to 
determine how to activate it. A natural 1 on the unlock roll spawns a Sleeper 
at the center of a random battlefield edge.

=== PAGE 134 ===
Environmental Hazard table
D100
ENVIRONMENTAL 
HAZARD
EFFECTS
01-15
Confused signal
A random Delve Device is moved 1D6+2” in a random 
direction. Reroll if it would be off the table. 
16-24
High security
The area within 4” and Line of Sight of the Delve Hazard becomes 
high security. Any non-Sleeper figure that is within this area at 
any point of their activation must make a D6 roll.  
On 6, a Sleeper is placed at the nearest battlefield edge point. 
The status continues for the rest of the mission.
25-39
Unstable Area
The area within 3” of the Delve Hazard is unstable 
(even if not in Line of Sight of the marker). Any figure moving 
through this area or firing a weapon into, out of, or through the 
area must roll 1D6. On 6, the area partially collapses. All figures 
in the area take a Damage +0 hit and the ground is Difficult  
for the rest of the mission. A second collapse makes it  
Impassable and causes all in the area to become casualties. 
Scouts can move through an unstable area without risk but 
must roll if they begin or end a move in the area.
40-50
Shock discharge
Place a marker where the Delve Hazard was. Immediately and at 
the start of each Enemy Phase, roll 1D6. All characters within that 
distance in inches, regardless of intervening terrain, are unable 
to move or take any actions when next activated. Sleepers and 
bots also take a +0 Damage hit, ignoring Saving Throws. 
A roll of 6 for the distance ends the Shock discharge.
51-67
Toxic fog
The fog expands 2” in every direction (blocked by solid walls) 
and moves at the end of each Enemy Phase 1D6” in a random 
direction, moving along walls if it would otherwise be stopped. 
Any non-Sleeper/bot character touched by the fog takes a  
+1 Damage hit, ignoring Saving Throws.  
Scouts are hit for +0 Damage instead.
68-79
Sleeper reset
All Sleepers on the table are unable to activate in the next  
Enemy Phase. At the end of that Phase, place an additional 
Sleeper in the center of two random battlefield edges. 
80-88
Collapse
The marker is replaced with rubble, blocking all access.  
If the rubble falls in a corridor, it blocks the width of the corridor. 
If it falls in a room or open space, it blocks an area 
1.5” radius centered on that location. 
Any figure within the blocked area can leap out of the way. 
89-00
Radiation build-up
The area within 3” and Line of Sight of the Delve Hazard 
is radioactive. Any non-Sleeper/bot that begins or ends an 
activation in the area takes a Damage +0 hit, ignoring any 
existing Saving Throws. Instead, a special Saving Throw is 
allowed for scouts (5+) and scientists (4+).

=== PAGE 136 ===
Post-Mission Finds
If directed to by the mission rules, you may 
receive a number of rolls on this table after 
winning an encounter. Finds can include 
actual materials found in the battle area or it 
can represent the less substantial gains of a 
successful field mission.
Each mission will detail how many Find rolls 
you are permitted.
Some finds will indicate a bonus for having a 
scientist or scout on the mission. This requires 
at least one must still be on their feet at the end 
of the battle and not a casualty.
Exploitation
Completing an Exploration mission that 
reduces the Resource level to 0 will mark a 
sector as Exploited. Unless it is repopulated 
again, it cannot be the subject of another 
Exploration mission.
This does not affect any other sources of Finds 
or other mission types.

Alien Artifacts
Truly Unique Artifacts 
(optional rule)
Each Artifact can be found only once... ever! 
If you begin a new campaign, all Artifacts you 
found in prior campaigns remain unavailable.
You may decide when to reset this (if at all). In 
the event you find every artifact, future Artifact 
Finds are treated as 1 Research Point, 1 Build 
Point, and 1 unit of Raw Materials.
Searching Ancient Sites may allow you to locate 
an Alien Artifact. If permitted, roll D100 on the 
Artifacts table below to determine what you 
have located. Only one roll is made per Ancient 
Site you successfully investigate. 
Artifacts can belong to one of three types:
Single-Use: Once they have been activated to 
receive the bonus in question, they are gone. 
You can choose when to use them, however 
and can hang on to the item as long as you like. 
Equipment: Artifact equipment is given to 
a specific character and cannot be traded 
away later. If the character dies or leaves the 
campaign, the item is lost. 
Artifact weapons can be used by any character, 
regardless of class. 
Colony item: The Artifact becomes part of your 
colony. Think of it as a type of building.
As Artifacts can take almost any form, they are 
described by the letter assigned by the research 
department and not their form or appearance. 
You are free to imagine each Artifact taking 
whatever form fits your campaign world the 
best, from a tiny orb of coalesced psionic 
energy to a rune-carved pedestal in a temple. 
Artifacts are Unique
Each Artifact can be found only once in a 
campaign. If you roll an artifact you have 
previously found, you instead receive the next 
item down on the list (wrapping back around to 
the beginning if needed).
Bringing Artifacts to other Campaigns
Unused Single-Use Artifacts can be brought 
along to a future Planetfall campaign.
Artifact Equipment can be brought along into 
any campaign the character is later exported to.
Colony Item artifacts cannot be carried over into 
any other campaign.

Uncertain Terrain Features 
(optional rule)
The ambient features of the world often 
interfere with accurate scans and you may not 
have a clear picture of the battlefield ahead.
For a more unpredictable battlefield, use this 
option to place several so-far-undetermined 
terrain features on the table. The Mission 
Briefings (see page 114) explain the suitability 
of this option. If the mission allows the use of 
Uncertain terrain features, it will instruct you on 
how many to place during setup.
Uncertain features are assumed to cover a circle 
of roughly 3” diameter. They can be indicated 
with a marker or by cutting out a piece of paper 
of 3” diameter or similar. Place the Uncertain 
features as you would any other terrain feature, 
making sure to leave some space around them. 
Uncertain features cannot be fired across, 
though enemies can move into and across the 
area as if it was open ground until revealed. If 
your table is crowded, it may be difficult to fit 
Uncertain features in the spaces available. If so, 
reduce the footprint to whatever size will fit.
Revealing Uncertain Features
Check for Uncertain terrain features at the end 
of each battle round. For any feature within 9” 
of any crew members or within 18” and in  
Line of Sight, roll D100 on the table below. 
Replace the marker with a suitable terrain piece 
and treat it as the listed terrain thereafter. You 
can adjust the size and shape of the feature to 
match terrain you have available. 
Uncertain Features table

=== PAGE 140 ===
Battlefield 
Setup
Preparing a Wargame Table
Whether you buy terrain kits, build your own, 
or use pieces of paper, the core functionality 
of terrain is to help produce a fun and 
exciting game. 
Once the tabletop battle begins, the terrain 
is “locked in”. It won’t change, and any 
placements are going to dictate where you 
can move (and how easily), and how effective 
various units will be. 
What is the Point of Terrain?
Before we start, let’s take a moment to 
consider why we put terrain on our tables. 
There are three:
1.	 It channels and impedes movement. 
When you have terrain features, troops must 
move around or through the features, some 
of which will prevent certain routes. Always 
consider what happens to those routes when 
the enemy is occupying one of them. 
2.	 It provides cover. Troops not only receive 
cover protection from weapons fire but 
terrain can block Lines of Sight (see  
page 35), helping to maneuver tactically 
around the table. As a game, Planetfall is 
lethal and troops trying to move across big 
open spaces tend to get shot to tiny bits.  
If you are to advance on an objective or try 
to get into close combat, you need terrain  
to enable that.
3.	 Terrain gives context and visual appeal. 
This sounds like a soft factor that doesn’t 
impact tactical gameplay, but it does, 
indirectly: A table with lots of boulders 
and big rocks presents hard obstacles to 
movement, requiring lots of short, sharp 
exchanges of fire as characters turn a corner 
and find enemies in their sights. A table that 
is heavy on jungle and forest will tend to 
have more close quarters fighting within the 
terrain features with few areas truly off limits. 
A table with a river crossing the table and 
two established crossing points will funnel 
the action towards those two points (or a 
firefight breaking out across the river as the 
two sides fire from cover). 
Do not underestimate the aesthetic value 
of visuals on your overall experience, either. 
A miniatures game is a story unfolding and 
the difference in feeling when you know 
that your characters are moving around alien 
foliage and strange rock formations is very 
noticeable. While better-made terrain will 
of course look better, you may be surprised 
how evocative even a simple foam board 
ruin can be when you are slugging it out 
against alien mercenaries. 
Battlefield Setup
138

=== PAGE 141 ===
Terrain Types
Terrain falls into one of four categories:
 
◆Area features allow figures to shelter within 
them but are not safe from close combat 
attacks, as attackers can enter the feature. 
This makes Area features both valuable to 
a defender who can use them for cover, 
while firing from a range of positions, and 
to the attacker who can obtain cover while 
advancing towards a position. 
 
◆Block features can sometimes be traversed, 
but are often Impassable, forcing gameplay 
to flow around them. They greatly affect 
Lines of Sight and thus provide indirect 
protection, but they are more difficult to use 
for cover. As such they tend to direct traffic 
during the game, more than providing 
fighting positions.
 
◆Linear features are primarily defensive 
in nature and are of the most value in a 
firefight. They make natural defensive 
positions for a couple of characters with 
long-range weapons, though they can be 
outflanked, negating their use on the table. 
However, excessive use of linear features can 
result in a static experience. 
Most tables will have a mixture of these first 
three terrain types to provide a range of effects. 
When used in combination, interesting things 
can happen. For example, having several 
smaller patches of bush (Area features) near 
each other will naturally provide pathways 
and clearings that can be navigated (or used 
as kill zones), while a Block feature with Linear 
features extending out from it can become a 
strong focal point for a battle.
 
◆Finally, there is Scatter terrain. These 
small individual pieces serve to provide 
protection for a figure (often temporarily). 
Moving from terrain to terrain feels very 
“tactical-cool” when you can make it work 
and having terrain being blown apart 
around you is cinematic. Don’t be afraid to 
add lots of scatter. 
The ways in which these terrain types 
specifically affect combat is discussed in the 
Shooting chapter, page 35.
29
139
Battlefield Setup

=== PAGE 142 ===
Coverage
Miniatures games almost always benefit from 
having a lot of terrain on the table: The game 
will be more fun and enjoyable if you must 
move around to accomplish things. 
Rather than dictate a norm in terms of how 
many individual pieces you need, it is more 
helpful to think of overall “coverage” of the 
table. When you are looking at the table 
surface, you typically want at least a third of it 
covered by terrain features and half is often a 
superior choice if you can manage it. This does 
not have to be exact in terms of square inches. 
A cluster of scatter terrain will “cover” the area 
it encompasses, even if there is empty ground 
between each tree, for example.
When placing terrain, using several smaller 
features is often better than a single huge 
piece. This is especially true for area features 
like forests: A single massive forest path will not 
play as well as replacing it with three or four 
smaller pieces arranged near each other.
The same applies to solid features. A huge 
boulder is less interesting in play than three 
modest-sized ones, since you can now move 
between them, fight from corner to corner, and 
otherwise interact with the feature.
The Theme
A table will almost always look better if you 
establish a theme with the terrain. This can 
be as in-depth as you like, particularly if you 
are building to a specific story. When you are 
140

=== PAGE 143 ===
starting out, this does not have to be any more 
involved than a basic terrain type that you use 
more of than the rest. If the table has a lot of 
jungle features with a few boulders, you have a 
visual theme going already. 
Putting terrain into clusters of similar pieces 
can help reinforce or create a theme. If you 
are using a mixture of alien ruins and jungle 
pieces, consider putting the ruins on one 
side of the table and the jungle on the other, 
producing a natural-feeling environment. 
This can be achieved even with only a couple 
of pieces, as the Human mind is inclined to 
recognize patterns on sight. Boulders placed 
as points of a triangle look immediately 
interesting, even if it has absolutely no other 
function or purpose. 
The Key Feature
Often, your collection will have some 
particularly cool and impressive terrain piece. 
You might have a huge alien building, a space 
port landing pad or a huge rock formation with 
a waterfall modelled down the side. 
Whatever it is, it takes up a big chunk of 
gaming space. Impressive features like this 
should always be placed near the center of 
the table, where most of the action is likely 
to take place. This ensures that characters will 
have time and motivation to move around the 
feature, that objectives might be placed on it, 
and so forth.
If a major feature is positioned along the 
battlefield edge, it will look impressive, but 
odds are it will not feature in the game much or 
141

=== PAGE 144 ===
even at all. This can be okay if you are content 
with it being a visual piece to add atmosphere, 
but this is often disappointing for features that 
are made to be interacted with, such as the 
spaceport.
Terrain features with limited interaction 
potential (such as a waterfall) are fine along 
the side, since they are mostly being added 
to give visual appeal to the game. When used 
like this, they can be thought of as a backdrop 
to the game table rather than acting as terrain 
features in their own right.
Some players hesitate in placing a lot of terrain 
along the edges, since it is at risk of being 
knocked off. If you are concerned, don’t be 
ashamed to leave an inch or two empty along 
the edges!
Checking your Work
When you have filled your table with terrain, 
stand back and have a look at how things 
turned out. Check for lines of fire: Long sight 
lines where a figure in one position could fire 
across most of the table, especially if they can 
be in cover while doing so.
A few such positions are not a huge problem 
– it helps characters with heavy weapons find 
something to do – but too many can result in 
a static game where there is not much reason 
to move.
When fighting enemies with close-range 
attacks, it can also result in them having a 
difficult time getting anywhere and, while 
the shooting gallery is fun occasionally, it is 
hardly dramatic.
Ensure you don’t make the table too cluttered. 
Sometimes, removing one or two pieces can 
help encourage figures to move around the 
table. Also, be especially careful not to fill 
the table with terrain that is difficult to move 
within. This can cause the game to slow to a 
crawl and is often not very exciting.
Using Terrain Tiles
Many board game and roleplaying game 
floor tiles are available, and these are great 
for games set inside buildings, alien ruins, or 
crashed ship interiors.
Tiles are usually modular and can be arranged 
to taste, producing a wide range of different 
setups. You can even combine exterior and 
interior areas by placing the tiles on a gaming 
table, surrounded by normal terrain. The edge 
of the tiled area is assumed to be walls. 
While tiles tend to be marked with a square 
grid, it is usually preferable to continue using 
measurements on the tiles. This has the 
advantage of not requiring conversion rules, as 
well as allowing more flexibility when placing 
figures. For example, on most grids it is difficult 
to place figures firing from corners of rooms, 
due to how the grid is usually aligned to the 
corridors. 
You will also find that most tiles have one-
square-wide corridors, which are difficult to 
use in tactical combat. When measuring, two 
figures can often fit in a corridor and both fight, 
making the area more dynamic. 
When placing terrain tiles, try to emphasize 
having lots of rooms with shorter corridors 
connecting them. Rooms are more interesting, 
as they offer more tactical possibilities for 
movement and fighting. Many tile sets have 
machinery and obstacles drawn on the tiles, 
giving more opportunities to seek cover.
Terrain Tiles and Movement
When using terrain tiles for building interiors, 
the default is to continue using measurements 
and ignore the grid on the tiles. If you prefer 
to use the grid, on a large interior floor plan 
1” = 1 square will work fine. If you need to 
condense things, use 2” = 1 square instead.
Battlefield Setup

=== PAGE 145 ===
Random Table Setup 
(optional rules)
If you prefer to have an unpredictable gaming 
table, you can use the following system. Follow 
each step in turn. You may have to make 
adjustments to fit the terrain pieces you have 
available to use. You could even modify the 
table to produce your own custom terrain table 
matching either your current terrain collection 
or the terrain you are building to play Planetfall. 
All dice rolls use D100.
It is generally accepted that due to limitations 
presented by your terrain collection, a few 
rerolls here is fine and you certainly do not 
need to use Story Points for that purpose.  
Of course, some players will gain enjoyment 
from trying to stick strictly to the results of the 
dice and, if so, no rerolls can be made. After 
all, the world is the way it was created before 
you ever got here.
Step 1: Roll for Ground Type
This determines the general surface you will be 
fighting over and is included for players who 
own (or want to buy or build) multiple gaming 
mats or suitably colored tablecloths. 
D100
GROUND
01-15
Sand or gravel
16-30
Dirt or mud 
31-80
Plant growth such as grass
81-95
Rocky and hard ground
96-00
Alien surface such as remnant 
construction
Step 2: Organizing the Table into Clusters
Form your gaming table into a number of 
spaces, each of which will hold one terrain 
cluster. The size of a cluster can vary but should 
be enough that it can hold the largest terrain 
feature you intend to use. If in doubt, you can 
make the clusters 12” square, fitting 9 of them 
in a 3x3 foot table. 
Organizing clusters into a grid is easiest, but 
you can also have them staggered in each row. 
You don’t have to painstakingly measure this 
out – eyeballing the size of each cluster is fine. 
With experience you might even place the 
clusters fluidly as you go. 
If you prefer a more open table, you can place 
fewer or smaller clusters than the table could 
take. For example, on a 3x3 foot table you may 
use 9 clusters but make some of them only 8” 
Cluster Type table

FEATURE

06-10

Hills, dunes, or other raised ground

Streams, lakes, or swamp. Can be water or other liquids

66-80

86-95

D100 
SUB-FEATURE
DESCRIPTION
01-14
None
No additional feature is present.
15-20
Crystals
Man-sized crystals.
21-27
Probe
The wreck of a probe, drone or similar.
28-37
Bushes
Some shrubs or bushes.
38-49
Rocks
A few big rocks.
50-60
Trees
A few single trees.
61-67
Puddle
A small liquid feature.
68-73
Crater
A crater from a meteor strike.
74-80

Plants forming a natural barrier.

Sign placed by colony scout.

or 10” along each side. Alternatively, you might 
choose to use only 7 clusters instead of 9. 
Table space that is not within a cluster is empty. 
If you prefer to randomize every aspect, tally up 
the number of clusters that would fit, then roll 
1D6-3. If the result is negative, reduce the total 
number of clusters by this amount.
Step 3: Primary Terrain for each Cluster
For each cluster you have, make a roll to 
determine that cluster’s primary terrain type. 
This is open to interpretation (and your terrain 
collection). You can take it as the primary 
feature placed in the cluster, or you can try to 
fill the cluster if you like. 
For game play purposes it is usually better to 
break large areas such as forest into two smaller 
features with some space between them to 
resemble a path or clearing. 
You can use one of three methods here:
Method 1: Roll for each cluster one at a time, 
placing terrain as you go.
Method 2: Roll a terrain feature, then choose 
which cluster to put it in, placing as you go.
Method 3: Roll multiple features up front and 
then arrange them in clusters. You can do 3 or 
more or even roll every feature in advance and 
then place them.
As terrain placement is highly individual, you 
may have your own preference for this. 
Step 4: Roll for Sub-features
If any clusters have open space within them 
at this point, roll again to determine what 
sub-feature may be present. Clusters that are 
completely filled skip this step.
As with the last step you can roll for one cluster 
at a time or roll them in advance and then 
place them.
Make Adjustments
Once you are done determining your terrain, 
take a few moments to review your table. You 
may find that two features would look better if 
they were swapped in position, for example to 
have areas heavy in plants near each other. 
You may also have a piece that just doesn’t 
look right, and the table would be improved by 
replacing it with something else.
Also review any wide-open spaces. A few of 
these are not a problem and can add visual 
Battlefield Setup
144

=== PAGE 147 ===
appeal, but if they are too large, they can be 
difficult in the game as figures trying to cross 
them get shot too easily. This is realistic, but 
can make the game too dominated by heavy 
firepower, so be careful about big spaces that 
will take more than two rounds to cross. 
If there are still some large empty spots, add 
a few extra scatter features: rocks, crystals, or 
trees. This will help break up the long lines of 
fire a bit more. Once everything looks good, 
you are ready to play!
Customizing the Terrain Tables
You can create your own custom terrain tables 
by copying the existing tables and then 
swapping out some entries for other types of 
terrain. This can be used, for example, to adapt 
to your particular terrain collection or to create 
something unique.
Examples might include:
 
◆Duplicating certain entries to account for 
your collection (or your vision of a science 
fiction world) featuring a lot of a particular 
terrain type such as multiple High Ground 
entries for a more hilly, broken-up world.
 
◆Narrowing what some of the entries mean. 
Instead of “Liquid” meaning any pool of 
liquid, you might replace it with “Swamps” 
because you think it would be cool to play 
on a fetid, marshy planet.
 
◆Replacing some entries with unique features 
from your collection. Maybe you have some 
huge plastic crystal pieces and would like to 
use those, so you swap out the “Liquid” entry 
for “Huge crystal”. 
If you wish the game world to evolve as you 
play, replace the first entry you roll each 
time with something new for the next game, 
allowing the terrain table to morph as you play 
the game, offering a balance of continuity and 
evolution as your crew explores the world.
Map Adjustments
If your location on the world map is characterized 
by a particular terrain type such as rocks, forest, 
or swamp, one cluster at the center of the table 
will always feature the terrain type on the map. 
You can either skip the roll here (if the map says 
you are in the mountains, put a bunch of rocks 
here) or roll and then try to amend the feature 
(a roll of high ground could become a rock 
formation on top of some high ground).

Battlefield Setup

=== PAGE 148 ===
Enemy Generation
Generating Lifeforms
When you have a Lifeform encounter, roll D100 
on the Campaign Lifeform Encounters table on 
your Colony Tracking Sheet (see page 190). If 
the entry is filled, you encounter that Lifeform.
If the entry is blank, proceed to generate a new 
Lifeform and enter its data into the encounter 
table. Going forward, every roll of that entry 
produces the same type of creature.
As your campaign progresses, you will have a 
unique encounter table that is specific to your 
gaming world.
Roll D100 on each of the tables in turn and 
note the results on the Campaign Lifeform 
Encounters table. Name the creatures as you 
generate them. An example Campaign Lifeform 
Encounters table is shown below.
If you encounter 6 or more Lifeforms during a 
mission, the 6th one encountered during the 
mission will be a pack leader. Add +2 KP to 
their normal profile. 
Campaign Lifeform Encounters table (example)
D100
NO.
ENTRY
01-18
1

Step 2: Offensive Abilities

has a Special Attack as well, which is rolled 
separately.

The final D100 roll (see table, page 148) 
determines if the creature has any unique 
abilities.
If it does, randomly pick one creature at the

Unique Ability table

UNIQUE 
ABILITY
DESCRIPTION
01-30
None
No special abilities of any kind.
31-40
Pull
The closest character in sight is pulled 1D6” closer. If this pulls them into contact, 
brawl immediately. If the Lifeform inflicts any hits, they are resolved with 
+2 Damage (in place of any normal attack capability the Lifeform has).
41-50
Jump
The Lifeform jumps 1D6+1” towards the nearest character. It will clear 
Human-height obstacles and if the jump takes it into or past an enemy, 
they are knocked Sprawling (see page 38).
51-55
Teleport
Teleports to a location 2D6” away in a random direction. If it would teleport off 
the table, replace the figure with a blip at the battlefield edge.
56-60
Paralyze
All characters within 6” and Line of Sight must roll 1D6+Savvy. 
If they fail to score a 4+, they cannot act next time they are activated.
61-70
Terror
All characters within 6” and Line of Sight must roll 1D6+Savvy. 
If they fail to score a 5+, they retreat 1D6+Speed” and become Stunned.
71-80
Confuse
The closest character must roll 1D6+Savvy. If they fail to score 6+, 
they immediately take a full move in a random direction. 
If they move off the table, they cannot return during the battle.
81-90
Hinder
All characters within 8” have their movement halved next time they activate.
91-00
Knock Down
Roll 1D6. Any characters within this range in inches are knocked Sprawling.
Generating Tactical Enemies
Use the Tactical Enemies Table to determine 
Tactical Enemy profiles. When a campaign 
begins, you will face three such enemies, each 
generated from this table. You can choose 
to roll for each enemy as they appear in the 
campaign or roll upfront so you can plan your 
miniatures purchasing or painting in advance.
You may encounter the same enemy more than 
once, representing either rival groups or a foe 
present in strength. (If you prefer more variety, 
reroll until you have three different foes.) 
Number: Use the roll indicated to determine 
how many enemies you are facing. Note that 
some missions may modify this.
 
◆One is always a specialist.
 
◆If there are 6 or more, one of them will be a 
leader (see sidebar, page 149).
 
◆All others are grunts.
Lifeform Miniatures
Some players may prefer to plan their miniatures 
selection, purchasing and painting in advance. If 
this is the case, you can roll up multiple (or even 
all of) the Lifeforms in advance and use the rolls 
to help decide what figures to use
The Lifeforms are described here only in terms 
of their tabletop abilities and traits. You are 
completely free to select any figures you would 
like to represent them on the table. You may look 
at the profiles and try to choose based on that, 
though you can interpret things as you see fit.
If you have creatures with similar profiles or 
abilities, they might be interpreted as similar 
creatures just as our world has different breeds 
of sharks. Alternatively, you may find it more 
interesting to have every creature be distinct. 
To save work, you could rely on the fact that two 
sub-species of the Agarion Crawler look identical, 
despite some key biological differences!

Speed: The movement rate in inches.
Combat Skill: If this is listed as a split value, 
the first value is used for regular enemies while 
the second value is used for any leaders and 
specialist figures.
Toughness: If this is listed as a split value the 
second value is used for any leader present. 
Panic: The Panic range used when making 
Panic tests (see page 40). If this is 0, the enemy 
is not subject to Panic tests and will instead 
fight to the death.

Leaders
If you encounter 6 or more enemies during a 
mission, one of them will be a leader. Note that 
because of how the random generation works, 
this means some enemy types will always be 
accompanied by a leader. Leaders are one of the 
generated figures, not an additional figure. For 
example, if you encounter 7 enemies in total 
there will be 6 regular enemies and 1 leader.
Leaders have 1 KP and tend to have different 
weapons available. 
Bosses are a sub-class of leaders and are only 
present if you track one down and then play a 
Strike mission. Bosses have 2 KP and add +1 to 
both their Combat Skill and Toughness.

The Slyn Threat
The Slyn appear to be the result of heavy 
genetic modification and exhibit considerable 
variation, though all are basically humanoid. 
The most common version encountered is 
reptilian in form and the prevailing theory is 
that this is the closest to whatever may have 
been their original form. 
They do not appear to have any natural means 
of reproduction, and no recorded encounter 
has resulted in sightings of Slyn occupying 
civilian roles. While Slyn landing craft have 
been accompanied by individuals who 
performed the roles of pilots, technicians, and 
other specialist tasks, these also engage in 
direct combat in a manner unlike most armed 
forces of the galactic powers. 
No indication of a “creator species” has been 
detected, but as the Slyn are reluctant to 
communicate with outsiders, evidence has 
not been forthcoming. It is possible that the 
current state is a result of the Slyn extensively 
modifying themselves. 
Slyn exhibit intense psionic manifestations 
but do not appear to access psionic wave-flows 
the same way precursors do. As the precursor 
method is the underpinning for almost all 
psionic institutes in known space, this is of 
great interest to researchers. 
The Slyn appear to have very strong psionic 
capability, allowing them to function even 
within dampening fields, but they do not 
exhibit the wide range of capability that other 
sapient beings do. A Slyn with a psionic ability 
is unlikely to ever develop a second type, 
giving rise to the theory that their talents 
may be a result of deliberate engineering, 
biological or otherwise. 
In battle, Slyn operate in pairs with the two 
individuals linked through a strong psionic 
bond that seems to sustain them in combat. 
Unity troopers have reported individual 
Slyn continuing to fight with missing limbs 
or massive bleeding as long as their linked 
partner is still active. Once both are injured, 
the pair collapse and the life force seems to 
dissipate quickly.
Battlefield Setup
152

=== PAGE 155 ===
153
Battlefield Setup
Slyn are tool users and, as evidenced by 
their landing craft, possess a high level of 
technology. They possess a natural ability to 
focus energy, manifested as a high-powered 
but somewhat short-ranged beam weapon, 
projected from their hands. They rarely use 
conventional infantry weapons as a result. 
Slyn Aggression Check
The Slyn attack unexpectedly but there is 
a pattern to their depredations, and some 
activities seem to draw their attention. 
Each mission description will explain if it is 
subject to attack from the Slyn. If it is, follow 
the dice roll instructions explained in the 
mission. This is generally a 2D6 roll with a 
score within a certain range meaning you will 
fight the Slyn instead of the regular enemy 
you would have faced.
The Slyn do not maintain headquarters or 
bases. While they do operate ships, it is 
unknown how they maneuver on the planet 
surface, and they can appear and disappear in 
the blink of an eye.
As the Slyn do not maintain local facilities 
or headquarters, they cannot be defeated in 
the course of the campaign. Achieving one of 
the End Game conditions will permit you to 
eliminate the threat. 
Slyn Encounters
There is not a distinct Slyn encounter table. 
They do not have leaders or special weapon 
troopers, and all Slyn soldiers use the same 
profile and are subject to the rules explained in 
this section. 
Speed:	
5”
Combat Skill:	
+1
Toughness:	
4
Brawling:	
Claws: Melee, Damage 1.
Ranged Attack:	 Beam focus: 18” range,  
Shots 1, Damage 1. 
Slyn Encounter Sizes
Slyn encounters in normal missions will be 
either 6 or 8 individuals formed into 3 or 4 
pairs respectively. Roll 1D6 when setting up 
the mission:
1-4: 	You encounter 6 Slyn.
5-6: 	You encounter 8 Slyn.
Slyn on the Battlefield
When deploying, Slyn are always set up in pairs 
with the two figures within 1” of each other.
Each pair will move in order to remain within 
1” of each other. Should they be separated for 
any reason, they will try to move back in range 
again as quickly as possible.
When firing, pairs will always attempt to fire at 
the same target figure and will select a target 
visible to both if possible. If this is not possible, 
they will each fire normally.
Slyn do not become Stunned for any reason. 
They take damage as a pair: If a single casualty 
is inflicted, put a marker by the pair. They will 
continue to move and fight normally. Once 
a second casualty has been inflicted (which 
can happen simultaneously with a multi-shot 
weapon) to either figure in the pair, they are 
both destroyed and removed from play.
Slyn Withdrawal
Slyn are not subject to Panic tests. They will 
withdraw when only a single pair is left on 
the table at the end of a battle round. The 
remaining Slyn vanish without a trace. 
Slyn Short Range Distortion
The Slyn can move using close distance 
teleportation. At the start of each Enemy Phase, 
randomly select a Slyn pair and move them 
2D6” in a random direction.
If this would take them off the battlefield 
edge, they are removed from the table (which 
can result in withdrawal at the end of the 
round). At the start of the next Enemy Phase, 
the pair reappears 2D6” from the center of the 

=== PAGE 156 ===
Battlefield Setup
154
table instead. This counts as the teleport for 
this round.
Slyn can move and fight normally after a 
teleport. It does NOT replace their normal 
movement. 
Slyn that emerge within 1” of an opponent will 
emerge directly into brawling combat. If they 
win, the figure cannot take any other actions 
this round.
The Sleepers
As you explore ancient alien ruins, you will 
come across the defenders that were once set to 
keep intruders out. While their systems are in 
poor states of repair, the fact they still function 
after several thousand years is a testament to 
the technology involved in creating them.
Troopers have nicknamed these robots 
‘Sleepers’ as they seem to activate only when 
near an organic presence.
Sleepers use the following profile. They do 
not distinguish between types: All Sleepers 
encountered in a mission are identical.
Speed:	
5”
Combat Skill:	
+1
Toughness: 	
4
Sleepers have a directional force screen, 
granting them a 6+ Saving Throw against all 
weapons fire, including weapons that would 
normally negate Saving Throws. They do not 
receive this protection against Brawling hits, 
however. 
When each Sleeper is placed on the table, roll 
1D6 to determine what weapon it is carrying:

Sleepers cannot be Stunned and are 
Fearless, immune to Panic. 
A Sleeper that scores a hit when firing 
immediately enters Rapid Fire mode: On its 
next activation, it will not move but will fire 
with 1 additional shot (2 for a Beam, 3 for a 
Heavy beam). If no targets are visible, it leaves 
Rapid Fire mode but does not otherwise move 
or act. If it has targets, it remains in Rapid Fire 
mode. A suitable counter can be placed next to 
the figure to remind you.
Using the Slyn in 5PFH
The Slyn can be used in games of Five Parsecs 
from Home. Substitute any one entry on one of 
the encounter tables for the Slyn. Their weapons 
and abilities should not require modification.
 
◆If the Slyn withdraw from a battle, you have 
Held the Field.
 
◆The Slyn cannot become Rivals of your crew.
 
◆As they are usually on the attack, they cannot 
be the defenders in Stealth missions, nor are 
they encountered in the hordes suggested by 
the Bug Hunt rules.

Campaign Development
156
The Milestone System
A campaign can continue as long as you like. 
However, the pressures of declining Colony 
Morale will push you towards crisis points. 
To complete the campaign, you need to pick 
the path forward and to be able to do so, you 
must achieve a number of Milestones during 
the campaign.
These can be earned from several sources, 
including the construction of Buildings and 
particular Research topics. Each Milestone 
represents a new stage in the development 
of the colony. In total, 7 Milestones must be 
achieved to set up the End Game. 
As you achieve each Milestone, particular 
effects take place in the campaign game, 
explained below. 
Note: It does not matter which Milestones you 
achieve: The game includes more than you 
need to finish the game and the particular ones 
you chose are part of your story but do not have 
a mechanical effect (though they may guide 
which ending you want to achieve). 
The Campaign Story Track
As you progress through the campaign, for 
every Milestone completed:
 
◆Roll a Lifeform Evolution (page 158); and
 
◆You may recruit one additional 
replacement for your roster.
In addition, particular events will happen as 
indicated below. These occur immediately upon 
achieving that Milestone:
1st Milestone Achieved
 
◆Roll 2 Character Events immediately (see 
page 170). This is in addition to the normal 
event you would receive each turn.
 
◆Add 1 point of Mission Data and check for 
Mission Data progress (see page 169).
 
◆Add 1 Calamity Point and check for 
Calamity progress (see page 165).
 
◆One Tactical Enemy emerges. Randomly 
select a map sector and mark that the enemy 
now controls it. This cannot be in or adjacent 
to your colony.
2nd Milestone Achieved
 
◆Immediately roll for a Colony Event (see 
page 62). This is in addition to the normal 
event you would receive each turn.
 
◆Every enemy on the map expands to occupy 
a random map sector adjacent to them.
 
◆You obtain 1 Ancient Sign (see page 61). 
 
◆Add 1 point of Mission Data and check for 
Mission Data progress (see page 169).
 
◆Add 2 Calamity Points and check for 
Calamity progress (see page 165).
 
◆One Tactical Enemy emerges. Randomly 
select a map sector and mark that they now 
control it. This cannot be in or adjacent to a 
sector held by another enemy or your colony.
Tech that Grants Milestones
The following Buildings grant a Milestone:
 
◆Galactic comms relay
 
◆Orbital facility
 
◆Genetic adaptation facility
 
◆Terraforming control centre
The following Augmentation grants a Milestone:
 
◆Mental links
The following Alien Artifact grants a Milestone:
 
◆Neo-ancient data cache
The following Research Applications grant a 
Milestone:
 
◆Frontier world doctrines
 
◆Integrated post-organic demarcation
 
◆Psionic personality integration

=== PAGE 159 ===
157
Campaign Development
3rd Milestone Achieved
 
◆Gain 3 Story Points.
 
◆Increase Colony Integrity by +1.
 
◆Immediately roll on the Enemy Activity 
table (see page 62). This is in addition to the 
normal event you would receive each turn.
 
◆From this point onwards, Tactical Enemies 
reduce their Panic range by 1. All tactical 
specialists receive 1 KP.
 
◆Add 2 points of Mission Data and check for 
Mission Data progress (see page 169).
 
◆Add 2 Calamity Points and check for 
Calamity progress (see page 165).
 
◆One Tactical Enemy emerges. Randomly 
select a map sector and mark that the 
enemy now controls it. This cannot be in or 
adjacent to your colony or a sector held by 
another enemy.
4th Milestone Achieved
 
◆Erase one campaign battlefield condition 
from the table on your Colony Tracking  
Sheet and generate a new condition to 
take its place.
 
◆You receive 1 Augmentation Point.
 
◆You may add 2 grunts to your list.
 
◆Add 1 point of Mission Data and check for 
Mission Data progress (see page 169).
 
◆Add 1 Calamity Point and check for 
Calamity progress (see page 165).
 
◆Track how many times you beat the Slyn 
from this point onwards. Each time you do, 
roll 2D6 and, if the roll is equal to or below 
the tracked victories, the Slyn decide to leave 
you alone. They will no longer attack for the 
rest of this campaign. Victories prior to this 
point are not counted.

=== PAGE 160 ===
Campaign Development
158
7th Milestone Achieved
 
◆The End Game now launches. See page 160 
for how to proceed.
 
◆Add 2 points of Mission Data and check 
for Mission Data progress (see page 169).
 
◆Add 1 Calamity Point and check for 
Calamity progress (see page 165). If no 
Calamity occurs, you no longer have to 
contend with Calamities in the campaign.
Lifeform Evolutions
As your colonists acclimate to the world, so it 
seems to become acclimated to you. Scouts 
report the planet’s resident lifeforms adapting 
more rapidly, often for the worse, exhibiting 
new methods of attack and defense when 
encountered.
The scientists suspect this may be an 
engineered defensive mechanism, though 
this leaves the question of how it was created 
and by who. The process seems random in 
nature but occurs both much faster and in 
a more direct fashion than what is normally 
understood as “evolution”. (The grunts in the 
field insist on calling it that, despite the science 
team’s protestations. As is often the case, such 
terms stick around even if people know better.) 
Evolutions occur each time you achieve a 
campaign Milestone. Randomly select an entry 
on your Colony Tracking Sheet to evolve. If 
your roll selects an entry that has not yet been 
filled, put a note on the encounter table. When 
you fill this entry, the creature will already be 
evolved and should roll on the table below after 
determining the basic profile and abilities. 
Once an evolution has occurred, all future 
encounters with the creature will feature the 
improved profile or abilities. 
Roll D100 for the evolution. It is possible for 
the same Lifeform to be selected multiple times 
in a campaign, though exact duplicates require 
a reroll of the evolution. For example, the same 
Lifeform cannot develop Summoning twice. 
5th Milestone Achieved
 
◆You receive 1 Augmentation Point.
 
◆Add 1 point of Mission Data and check for 
Mission Data progress (see page 169).
 
◆Add 2 Calamity Points and check for 
Calamity progress (see page 165).
6th Milestone Achieved
 
◆All Tactical Enemy encounters add  
1 additional specialist figure to the 
number encountered. 
 
◆For each turn of the rest of the campaign, 
roll on the Enemy Activity table (see  
page 62) for every enemy on the map.
 
◆Add 2 points of Mission Data and check 
for Mission Data progress (see page 169).
 
◆Add 2 Calamity Points and check for 
Calamity progress (see page 165).

Lifeform Evolution table
D100
EVOLUTION
DESCRIPTION
01-10
Enhanced Profile
The creatures become more adapted to your specific combat methods. 
If the Lifeform would normally have a Combat Skill of +0, it is raised to 
+1. This includes reduced Combat Skills for Lifeforms capable of shooting.
Add +1” to Speed.
If the creature has a Strike Power of +0 damage, it is raised to +1.
11-20
Poison
All of the creature’s attack forms apply a slow-acting nerve toxin.
Any character struck by the creature receives a Poison marker per hit. 
When the character activates, roll 1D6 for each Poison marker. If any of 
them result in a natural 6, the character becomes a casualty. If they do not, 
remove ONE Poison marker.
A scientist within 2” can attempt to help by spending their action and 
rolling 6+ on a 1D6+Savvy test. This removes one Poison marker.
21-30
Spines
The creature becomes covered in sharp spikes.
Any character that loses a brawl against the creature takes 
an additional hit at +0 Damage. 
31-40
Duplication
Through a psionic manifestation, the Lifeform is capable is reproducing 
spontaneously and immediately. 
At the start of each Enemy Phase, roll 1D6. If the roll is a 6, a random 
Lifeform duplicates. Place a second Lifeform adjacent to it. This ability does 
not activate if only Contacts are present on the table. 
41-50
Dramatic 
transformations
The creatures are changing rapidly in an unpredictable fashion
Delete any Unique Ability the Lifeform lists on the Unique Abilities table 
(page 148). Instead, each battle where the creature is encountered, 
roll on the Unique Abilities table again and use the 
indicated ability during that battle.
51-60
Summoning
The creatures emit a strange call that attracts more of them 
to the battlefield. 
At the start of each Enemy Phase, roll 1D6. If the roll is a 6, 
place a Contact on a random table edge.
This ability does not activate if no Contacts are present on the table.
61-70
Evasion
Whether due to agility, chemical fog or psionic effects, the creatures 
are becoming difficult to target.
Each time a creature is fired upon, roll a D6. On a 6, it evades and is 
not hit. A creature that evades remains unable to be hit for the rest of the 
current battle round. If a creature evades in the Quick Actions Phase, 
it cannot attack when it activates in that Enemy Phase. 
Evading creatures can be hit by brawling attacks but cannot 
initiate it themselves.

160
D100
EVOLUTION
DESCRIPTION
71-80
Darts
The Lifeform can now launch razor-tipped darts at nearby opponents.
Each creature will launch a dart at the end of its activation after all of its 
attacks and brawls have taken place. Darts have Range 9”, target the 
closest opponent visible, hit on a D6 roll of a 6, and inflict a 
Damage +0 hit. 
81-90
Bigger Leaders
There are more pack leaders, and they are getting bigger as well.
The 4th Lifeform encountered in a mission is now a secondary leader with 
+1 KP. The 6th Lifeform encountered in a mission is a pack leader as 
usual. They receive +3 KP instead of the customary +2.
91-00
Tougher Specimens
The creatures are getting bigger and meaner.
Each time a Lifeform is placed on the table, roll a D6. On 5-6. it has +1 KP.

The End Game
Upon achieving the Final Milestone, the 
characters on your roster will meet to debate 
what course the colony should take. The 
summit takes place at the end of the current 
campaign turn and cannot be delayed.
The Summit
Everyone knows that a moment of decision 
has been reached. Conversations have been 
had, and predictions have been run. It is time 
to bring it out into the open. You summon 
your most trusted companions and advisors to 
discuss the fate of the colony.
Each character on your roster voices or supports 
an opinion on which path to pursue. Roll a D6 
on the table below for each character (not 
counting any bots or grunts), plus an additional 
die to represent the general population.
You may select freely from opinions that are 
supported. Any opinion that does not have 
support from any character or the general 
population cannot be selected. Having dealt 
with colony security (see below), progress to 
the appropriate selection in ‘Final Milestones’ 
(page 161).
Colony Security
Once the summit has taken place, you must 
establish colony security before you can make 
final preparations. 
The Ascension, Isolation, and Independence 
options require defeating at least one Tactical 
Enemy by tracking down and destroying 

=== PAGE 163 ===
161
Campaign Development
their Strongpoint, while Loyalty requires 
defeating two of them in this way. If you have 
previously achieved this, you already meet 
the requirement. 
Once your colony is secure, you can then 
proceed to construct the final milestone.
Final Milestones
The cost of the final milestone must be paid in 
both Build Points and Research Points. 
Independence
(Cost: 15 BP, 5 RP)
With the colony set on a path of independence, 
you order the large-scale construction of 
defenses and issue a proclamation that 
the world is now independent and will no 
longer accept the dictates of a remote and 
unaccountable government.
You may end the campaign as a victory here. 
After all, it is your story, and you may have an 
idea for how it ought to end. Narrate your story 
as you see fit.
If you prefer, you may work through the 
outcomes below instead: Roll 2D6 to 
determine the Unity response.
If you developed the Defense Network 
Mission Data Breakthrough, add +1 to rolls 
on this table unless the dice show a double. 
2D6
UNITY RESPONSE
2
Total war. Unity decides to make 
an example of you. The colony is 
obliterated unless you developed the 
Artificial Construction Mission Data 
Breakthrough. If so, you must go to 
War instead (see below).
3-7
War. Unity responds with military force. 
See the “War” sidebar.
8-10
Negotiations drag on. Roll again on 
this table. Any result of an 8 is counted 
as “War” (above) while any roll of 
10 is counted as Unity granting you 
“Recognized status” (below).
11-12
Recognized status. After hearing your 
pleas, the Unity Bureaucracy grant your 
request and offer semi-autonomous 
status. Your colony will go on to become 
an independent, Unity-Allied world. 
Congratulations.
War
A single colony cannot hope to defeat the might 
of the Unity armies, but you may be able to 
outlast them in a colonial struggle. The war is 
fought in a number of rounds during each of 
which you will roll 1D6 for your colony and 1D6 
for the Unity expeditionary force.
Each round, the side with the lowest score 
suffers 1 level of War Fatigue. 
On a draw, one random character on your 
roster is killed in the fighting, and you roll 
again. If you developed the Warzone Mission 
Data Breakthrough, Unity also takes 1 War 
Fatigue on a draw.
If a side accumulates a total of 3 War Fatigue, 
they surrender. If Unity surrenders, you are 
now independent! If your colony surrenders, 
it is incorporated into Unity forcefully. Every 
character on your roster may roll a 5+ on 1D6 
to escape, but are otherwise arrested. 
Campaign Play
If you wish to incorporate your world into a 
Five Parsecs from Home campaign, roll twice 
on the World Trait table and apply both results. 
Worlds that underwent a war are subject to the 
Fringeworld Strife rules (5PFH Compendium, 
page.148), if you use that option.
