=== PAGE 166 ===
Campaign Development
164
Characters after the End
Your characters may be in all manner of places 
after they complete the campaign. Take a 
moment to evaluate any survivors. This is 
mostly left up to the player. By the time you 
complete the campaign, you will likely have a 
good sense of what you want to do and what 
feels natural. 
The following guidelines apply for characters 
that move into campaigns of Five Parsecs from 
Home, Bug Hunt, or Tactics:
 
◆You may take any artifact weapons, armor, 
and equipment with you, but each character 
can take only one such item. 
 
◆Upgraded equipment developed during 
the campaign can be taken with you as well. 
These items are not in limited supply, but a 
character can only take items they were able 
to be used in Planetfall. 
 
◆Characters bring along all abilities obtained, 
and profile increases they have earned. 
Five Parsecs from Home-specific notes:
 
◆If you completed the Loyalty endgame, you 
can begin the campaign with a random ship 
and no debt.
 
◆If you completed the Independence 
endgame, but lost the war, any characters 
that escaped captivity begins the campaign 
with 1 Rival (with an even chance of 
Enforcers or Bounty Hunters). These Rivals 
will pursue you from world to world but can 
otherwise be defeated normally.
 
◆If you won, you begin the campaign with 
a ship. You must pay off the debt normally, 
but you begin with 2D6 Credits of it already 
paid off. 
In addition (win or lose), you may begin the 
campaign with 2 additional Story Points.
If you completed the Isolation endgame, 
select one character: They gain 1 point of 
Luck. You may only bring one character from 
an Isolation victory into each new campaign.
 
◆If you completed the Ascension endgame, 
select one character: They gain psionic 
abilities (see 5PFH Compendium, p.17).
What about the Slyn?
The Slyn remain a mystery to be unraveled. You 
are encouraged to think of your own solution to 
the issue of their attacks: 
 
◆Does Unity fleet presence finally drive them 
off? 
 
◆Does Ascension permit you to finally 
understand them? 
 
◆Does an independent colony policy allow 
you to make a deal with the otherwise 
hostile creatures?
 
◆Does the conflict continue until it escalates 
into large scale warfare?
 
◆Maybe the mysterious creatures disappear 
as suddenly as they appeared? 
You may prefer the Slyn to remain a mystery as 
well. Did they feature heavily in your campaign 
or were they only encountered once or twice? 
Perhaps they were simply passing through 
and your fights were chance encounters? Or 
perhaps they have a more nefarious scheme 
that connects into these strange worlds, the 
future history of your characters and the fate of 
the galaxy as a whole?

=== PAGE 167 ===
165
Campaign Development
Calamities
“The uncharted worlds are strange. 
Sometimes it feels like the world itself is 
conspiring against us.” 
– Head researcher for ultra-flora 
in the Soissons Quadrant
As your colony develops, you will have to 
contend with moments of crisis as they occur. 
These can take several forms and are known 
collectively as Calamities.
The Calamity Check
Check for Calamities each time you achieve a 
Milestone. Add up the number of Calamity 
Points listed in the Milestone progress and 
immediately roll 1D6. If the roll is equal to or 
below the total Calamity points, subtract the roll 
from the total Calamity points and then roll on 
the table below to see what occurs.
If the roll is above the Calamity points total, no 
Calamity occurs, and the points remain. 
For example, if I have 4 Calamity Points and 
roll a 3, a Calamity will occur, and 1 Calamity 
Point would remain to be carried forward.
Calamity Points are not gained or lost in any 
other fashion. 
D100
CALAMITY
01-11
Swarm Infestation
12-25
Environmental risk
26-36
Enemy super weapon
37-48
Virus
49-63
Mega predators
64-77
Wildlife aggression
78-91
Robot rampage
92-00
Slyn assault
The same Calamity cannot occur twice in a 
single campaign. If you experience a Calamity 
that has already happened to your colony, take 
the next option on the table (wrapping around 
to the start if needed). 
For the details of each Calamity, see the 
entries below.
Enemy Super Weapon
A random Tactical Enemy is building a super 
weapon that they intend to shoot at your colony. 
The enemy will complete 1D6 points of 
progress at the end of every campaign turn. 
When the total progress reaches 15, the 
weapon is launched and will inflict 3D6 
damage to your colony. Every 6 rolled on the 
damage roll also causes a randomly selected 
character to make a roll on the Post-Battle 
Injury table (see page 66).
When the weapon is launched, the enemy 
begins building the next one, starting again 
from 0.
Tracking down the weapon site requires an 
Enemy Information roll (see page 60). Once 
successful, you can then play out a Strike 
mission, with success destroying the weapon 
production capability. This ends the Calamity. 
If no Tactical Enemies are on the map currently, 
randomly roll on the Tactical Enemies table 
to determine what you end up fighting. The 
enemy will be a minor scouting force and not 
connected to any other group.
As you loot the enemy site, you may roll twice on 
the Post-Mission Finds table (see page 134).
Environmental Risk
Long range scans have detected strange 
atmospheric anomalies that appear to be 
intensifying storms and radiation.
Randomly select 3 map sectors. They can be 
enemy held. These sectors are experiencing the 
anomalies.
At the end of each campaign turn, roll a D6 
for each anomalous sector on the map:  
1-4 indicates no effect; 5-6 results in an 
environmental flare-up, in which case, roll again:

=== PAGE 168 ===
Campaign Development
166
D6
FLARE-UP
1-2
A random crew member on your 
roster is injured and must spend  
one campaign turn in Sick Bay.
3-4
The colony sustains 1 point of Colony 
Damage, bypassing defenses.
5
No effect
6
Another random sector develops 
an anomaly.
To clear each anomaly, you must carry out a 
Patrol Mission in the sector with the following 
modifications:
 
◆1 additional Contact is placed on a random 
battlefield edge every battle round. 
 
◆Once all three Objectives are clear, a 
character must move to one of the Objectives 
(your choice) and roll 1D6+Savvy, scoring 
a 6 or better (remember, scientists may roll 
twice and pick the highest result). When this 
succeeds, you have collected the samples 
needed to counteract the anomalies in this 
sector, clearing it. 
The Calamity ends when every sector has been 
cleared. Each anomaly you clear allows you to 
gather 2 units of Raw Materials. 
Mega Predators
Your scouts report seeing particularly large and 
menacing wildlife when they are patrolling.
Each time a Lifeform is revealed, roll 1D6. On a 
6, the specimen has 1 additional KP and the 
die is rolled again, adding KP until you don’t 
roll a 6.
To eradicate the nest, you must carry out  
Patrol Missions (see page 121). Each time you 
complete an objective, an additional Contact is 
placed on a random battlefield edge. Mark this 
Contact in some fashion so you can tell it apart. 
When it is revealed, all Lifeforms placed have 
+2 KP added to their profile (this is total, not 
cumulative with the +1 for this Calamity). You 
must kill 5 such Lifeforms to end the Calamity. 
Your incessant patrolling has brought some 
rewards. Randomly select three map sectors 
and increase their Resource ratings by  
+1 each. 
Robot Rampage
Suddenly, angry robots spill across the surface. 
They resemble the Sleepers detected before but 
appear to be acting as sentries.
In every mission you play, place a marker in 
each of the 6 terrain features closest to the 
center of the table.  
 
When a figure is within 12” and Line of Sight  
of a feature with a marker, roll 1D6 for the 
feature. Multiple features can be revealed at 
the same time.
 
◆Each die that rolls a 1-3 is removed with 
no effect.
 
◆Each 4-5 adds a Sleeper with a Beam 
Weapon where the marker was (see  
page 154).
 
◆Each 6 adds a Sleeper with a Beam 
Weapon and another marker is placed in 
the closest terrain feature to the one just 
revealed. This can result in the newly placed 
marker being revealed immediately. 
Each time you kill a Sleeper outside of a Delve 
mission, if you can move to the location where 
it was destroyed before the battle ends, roll 
1D6+Savvy (remember, scientists roll twice 
and pick the best score). 
A test result of 5+ finds a Sleeper chip. 
A failed test destroys the chip. 
Once you have located 5 Sleeper chips, you 
can send out the shut-down signal when you 
return to your colony, ending the Calamity. 
Add 4 Research Points as your scientists 
dissect the chips afterwards.

=== PAGE 169 ===
167
Campaign Development
Slyn Assault
The Slyn are stepping up their attacks.
Until you have fended off this latest Slyn 
assault, every time you undertake a mission 
with a chance of Slyn interference, you must 
roll twice to see if they interfere. If both rolls 
indicate interference, you will automatically 
encounter 8 Slyn.
Keep track of the number of Slyn you kill. Once 
you have killed 30, they decide to step down 
their offensive. The Calamity ends once this 
occurs. You may immediately add 2 grunts 
to your Colony Tracking Sheet as the lessons 
learned allow you to train up new soldiers.
Swarm Infestation
The hostile alien lifeforms known as The Swarm 
have begun infesting the world.
Randomly select a map sector that is not 
enemy held. It is immediately infested with 
large numbers of the murderous aliens known 
as the Swarm. 
The Swarm refuse all attempts at interaction, 
resorting to immediate, frenzied attack when 
sapient life is within several kilometers. The 
only saving grace is that they can occasionally 
be lured away by the use of drones.
After you have completed your mission for the 
turn, check to see if the Swarm is spreading: 
Roll a D6 for each adjacent map sector to a 
Swarm sector. You can choose the order you 
want to test them in. Once you roll a 6, the 
Swarm infests that sector as well, and no 
more rolls are made this turn. If you test every 
adjacent sector without rolling a 6 the Swarm 
are currently laying low.
The Swarm prevents all access to sectors they 
occupy. If they infest a sector held by an enemy, 
the enemy loses the sector unless it was their 
final sector they held, in which case they 
evacuate to another random map sector. 
If the Swarm infest one of the 8 sectors 
adjacent to your colony, they will begin 
attacking your perimeter. Each campaign turn, 
roll 1D6 for each infested sector adjacent: On 
1-2, nothing occurs. On 3-5, your colony takes 
damage equal to the die roll. On a 6, you take 6 
Damage and one of your grunts is killed. 
If the Swarm would expand into your colony 
sector, you must fight a Pitched Battle mission 
next campaign turn. 
To defeat the Swarm, you must kill them off. 
This uses the Skirmish mission. Victory clears 
out the sector (for now).

Swarm:
Speed:	
6”
Combat Skill:	
+1
Toughness:	
4
Brawling:	
Claws, +1 Damage
Ranged Attack:	 none
Roll 2D6+4 for the number of Swarm

The Calamity ends when every sector has

Overcoming the Swarm allows you to recruit

Virus
A dangerous virus is tearing through your 
colony.
Randomly select two characters on your 
roster and roll 1D6 for each. If the roll is 1-5, 
this is how many Virus Points they have 
accumulated. If the roll is a 6, they immediately 
fall ill.
At the end of each campaign turn, select three 
characters on your roster. If they have no Virus 
Points, roll as above. If they do have Virus 
Points, increase the amount by +1. A total of 6 
means they fall ill.
Once a character falls ill, they must stay in Sick 
Bay (they are being quarantined) and can take 
no actions of any kind.
To overcome the virus, you need to synthesize 
a cure. This is done by playing the Hunt 
mission. Each time you complete the mission 
you receive two points of Cure Data. Roll 
2D6 at the end of each campaign turn before 
rolling for the spread of the virus. On a roll 
equal to or below the total amount of Cure 
Data, you have enough to start producing the 
cure. The virus no longer spreads, and your 
characters recover immediately.
The Calamity ends when the cure is discovered. 
Due to your medical advances, you may reduce 
any injury recovery times for injured characters 
by a total of 4 turns spread among characters as 
you see fit (2 characters reduced by 2 turns, 4 
characters reduced by 1 turn each, etc.). You can 
save any of this effect for later.
Wildlife Aggression
The lifeforms are far more aggressive than 
usual. Something appears to be agitating them. 
Your researchers speak of a Controller that must 
be located and destroyed. 
Any Contact reveal that places Lifeforms will 
place 1 additional Lifeform. It is placed in the 
nearest terrain feature to where the marker was. 
To find the Controller, you must play out a 
Hunt mission. Each time you reveal a Lifeform, 
roll 1D6 per Lifeform figure. On a 6, it is 
the Controller. The Controller will be of the 
standard type but has 3 additional KP added 
to the profile. The Controller will always attempt 
to move towards the nearest battlefield edge by 
the shortest route possible and, upon reaching 
it, it will flee. You will have to carry out another 
Hunt. If the path is blocked by a character, the 
Controller will move in a random direction, 
adding +1” to its movement rate. It will only 
enter brawling combat if this random move 
requires it to.
Killing the Controller and moving into base 
contact with where it fell to confirm it is dead 
ends the Calamity. 
Completing this Calamity gives you an 
increased understanding of the world. You may 
add 1 Augmentation Point.

Campaign Development
Mission Data
Gathering Mission Data is one of the keys to 
unravelling what the history of this world is and 
where it may have originated from. 
Mission Data is obtained from missions you 
undertake in the field as well as completing 
certain Research projects and Buildings. You 
also receive Mission Data when you complete a 
Campaign Milestone.
Each time you received Mission Data, process 
it: Roll 1D6; if the roll is equal to or below the 
amount of Mission Data you have acquired, 
you have made a significant Mission Data 
Breakthrough. Reduce the Mission Data by the 
die roll, then consult the list below.
First Mission Data Breakthrough
You have discovered the location of 2 Ancient 
Sites. Place them randomly on your map.  
Fully exploring each of these Sites awards you 
1 point of Mission Data in addition to any 
other rewards. 
Second Mission Data Breakthrough
You have discovered multiple important 
resource caches. Randomly select four map 
sectors. Each of them that is not currently 
Explored is Explored immediately. Then 
increase the current Resource value by +2. 
This can turn an Exploited sector into an 
Explored sector.
Third Mission Data Breakthrough
This world continues to intrigue you. 
Randomly select two sectors, marking them 
for Investigation. 
Fourth Mission Data Breakthrough
You have finally uncovered the information you 
need to put all the pieces together. This is the 
final Breakthrough, and after this point, Mission 
Data no longer has any value. Roll D100 on the 
table below to determine what you find:

Patterns
“The signs are unmistakable,” Shival declared 
to the assembled group. Without waiting for 
confirmation, she continued: “Every predictive 
simulation shows the same thing: There is 
something out there near the galactic core and 
the Slyn knows what it is.”
One of the younger researchers looked up 
from their data pad. “I tried reaching out to 
our Precursor contacts, but they were not 
forthcoming. Typical obfuscation we always get 
from them.” 
A few of the team members laughed bitterly. 
Despite all the achievements of Humanity, 
the Precursors still treated them as immature 
children at times. 
Shival nodded and called up the maps she 
had been working on. The digital image filled 
the monitors in layers, first the topographical 
map, then the scout survey data, and finally 
the icons and symbols she had added. The 
team stirred to life as they focused on what 
was in front of them.
“You see it too, don’t you?” She looked at them 
expectantly and was not disappointed.  
A cacophony erupted as everyone began to talk 
at once, pointing to elements of the map and 
proclaiming their interpretations and theories. 
She crossed her arms and waited. This was 
part of the creative process. Any minute now 
someone would…
“It’s a beacon.”
Sylas’s voice cut through the din and 
brought the room to silence. He looked up 
from the prediction software he was usually 
submerged in. 
Shival nodded. The information sunk in as the 
team assessed and reassessed the statement.  
As their eyes focused on her again, she crossed 
her arms and sat back on the edge of the table.
“The question is who built it and what is it 
pointing to?”
One of the newer recruits to the team, a Feral 
with a prior history in the army, grunted and 
interjected:
“The real question is why are they so angry we 
might find it.”

Appendices
174
Gaming Basics
If you aren’t an experienced gamer or, more 
likely, you are a somewhat experienced 
gamer who’d like a quick reference on the ‘5X’ 
approach, this short chapter is for you. 
Dice Rolling
This game involves a lot of dice rolling, most 
often using common six-sided dice, referred to 
here as D6.
 
◆When only a single die is rolled it can be 
written as D6 or 1D6.
 
◆If you must roll multiple six-sided dice and 
add the results together, this will be written 
as 2D6 (two dice with six sides each), 3D6 
(three dice), and so forth.
 
◆Sometimes you may be asked to roll 
multiple D6, and not add them together; in 
such a case this will be written as ‘two D6’, 
for example.
A ten-sided dice is known as a D10. To run 
your game smoothly, have five or six D6 and a 
couple of D10 available. Multiple colors of dice 
are also handy, as is a full set of roleplaying 
dice including D4, D8, D12, and D20.
Dice rolls are often modified. This means a dice 
roll has a number added to or subtracted from 
the roll to produce the final result. For example, 
a D6 with a +1 modifier (written as D6+1) 
gives a final result between 2 and 7.
If the rules say something happens on a 
‘natural’ result, it means the number must be 
rolled on the actual die, before any modifiers 
are applied. If something is going to happen 
on a natural 6, rolling a 5 with a +1 modifier 
does not count.
The Campaign rules and Mission Setup also 
use percentile dice (usually written as D100). 
Unless you’re lucky enough to own a one-
hundred-sided die (yes, someone does make 
them), roll two D10 with the first counting as 
the tens and the second counting as the ones.  
So, rolling a 3 and a 7 would be a score of 37, 
a 0 and a 4 would be a 4, a 5 and a 0 would be 
a 50. 0 and 0 counts as 100 (although tables 
usually just show it as ‘00’)
If the rules allow something to be rerolled, the 
original result is ignored. 
Random Directions
You can purchase custom direction dice, 
or you can make your own by drawing 
arrows on blank dice. Otherwise, 
a ten-sided die can be used. 
Roll it near the origin point 
and then use the tip of 
the uppermost face to 
show the direction. 
Random Selection
You will often be called upon to select randomly 
from among multiple choices such as terrain 
features or characters. To do so, roll a D10 and 
count from top to bottom (on a roster) or left 
to right (on a table). If you roll higher than the 
number of possible choices, you may select 
the outcome. For example, if you are selecting 
randomly from a roster of 7 characters, a roll of 
1-7 would be the character with that number 
on the roster, while 8-10 means you choose. 
Miniatures on the Table
Characters in the game world are represented 
by miniatures, each of which stands in for 
a single character or creature. Figures are 
mounted on a flat plate called a base. These 
can be decorated nicely with bushes, rocks, 
sand or textured modelling material and paint. 
When the base of a figure is touching 
something, it is said to be in base contact.  
For example, two opposing characters can  
fight in brawling combat when they are in  
base contact.
7
3
8
4
0

=== PAGE 177 ===
175
Appendices
When moving figures, sometimes two figures 
who should obviously be in base contact cannot 
be placed in contact due to the way their arms, 
wings, and similar are posed. If so, place them 
as close as you can and just make a mental note. 
The bases of two figures cannot overlap, but if 
you have a figure on a particularly large base 
you may have to amend this rule a little bit.
The base is also used to determine the position 
of the figure for all game purposes. If the base 
is partially within a terrain feature, the figure 
is assumed to be “in” that feature for game 
purposes such as being in cover. 
Exact facing of the miniatures is not a factor 
in these game rules. Characters can always 
fire in any direction, not just the direction they 
appear to be facing (although the game is more 
visually appealing if they do face the action!).
Ranges and Measurements
Movement, weapons fire, and more are 
measured in tabletop inches. Miniatures games 
traditionally used inches, and we’ve stuck with 
that. Players who prefer metric measurements 
can multiply all values by 2.5. 
To measure from a figure, measure from the 
point of the base nearest the direction you are 
Base Contact Examples:
Figures (A) and (B) are in base contact. 
Figures (C) and (D) are seperated by 
a narrow barricade. As they can still 
reasonably be engaged in close assault 
across this obstacle, they are also 
considered to be in base contact. 
A
B
C
D
Measurement Example:
measuring to. To measure to a figure, measure 
to the nearest point of the base. Never measure 
to or from gun barrels, hands, or the figure itself.
Again, you may amend this slightly if you have 
a figure mounted on an unusually large base.
You can always measure before deciding 
whether to do something.
Line of Sight
To determine if two figures can shoot at each 
other, see if they have a Line of Sight (LoS). 
This is a straight line drawn between the 
figures: If it reaches the target, they are in sight. 
If a terrain feature or another figure is in the 
way, there is no Line of Sight.
Some players use a string or laser line tool 
to measure this, but estimating is fine. You 
can always lean down over the table to gain a 
‘model’s eye’ view. You might well be surprised 
at what is (and often is not) visible.
Sometimes terrain provides Cover. Depending 
on the type of terrain, this may break LoS.
completely or may make someone a more 
difficult target to hit (see examples, right). This 
is discussed in ‘Shooting’, on pages 35-36.
Absolute Timing
The game uses a principle called Absolute 
Timing: Everything on the table is in that exact 
state and position when you resolve a rule. For 
example, if one character downs their target, 
that may mean another character now lacks a 
target to shoot at (or now has an open path to 
move). It also means actions are not usually 
queued up or declared in advance. 

=== PAGE 178 ===
Appendices
176
Learning to Play 
Adventure Wargames
Live by the Dice, Die by the Dice
Adventure wargaming relies heavily on dice. 
This means that things can become very 
unpredictable very quickly. You may run into 
fights that are extremely difficult, or you may 
find the battle a breeze. Some turns you are 
swimming in rewards and others you lose two 
of your most valuable squad members.
The rules provide Story Points (see page 56) 
to take the edge off the worst luck, but the ebb 
and flow of fortune is a key part of the game 
experience. In general, you will find that being 
“good” at the game means optimizing your 
opportunities and minimizing the bad luck.
The variability also helps make the game feel 
more exciting and less planned. When new 
players look back at a few campaign turns 
completed, they often find themselves being 
surprised at how well the experience fits 
together when taken as a whole. 
Nobody is Special
There is no special player character role in this 
game. All of your characters are at risk of injury 
and death, and when the dice has it out for you, 
it is time to pay the piper.
This is not a bleak and depressing thing, 
however. It also means that everybody is a hero 
waiting for their moment. There is something 
immensely satisfying about pulling off a tricky 
combat, because you played your tactics well 
and had a few hot dice to boot. 
Embrace the moments when they happen, and 
they will help carry the day.
Playing with Friends
The simplest way to play the game together is 
to split your squad during missions. Usually this 
means each of you taking four characters and 
otherwise playing as you normally would. More 
ambitious players can invent more involved 
systems such as taking turns commanding the 
monsters or playing two separate campaigns 
that take place on the same world. 
For more advice on playing with a Game 
Master, see page 185.
Avoid Do-overs
When learning a new game, it is easy to mess 
something up. You forgot something that was 
supposed to be in a scenario, forgot to check 
for enemy Panic, or you misunderstood how a 
weapon works. Unless the mistake is the last 
action you did, just move on and avoid a similar 
mistake going forward. Trying to undo and 
rework multiple actions or even an entire game 
turn is almost never going to be worth the 
frustration and can be really demoralizing.
In the long run, things will usually even out. 
If you feel like you seriously messed up the 
difficulty of the game, hand out a few extra 
Story Points if you made it too difficult or add 
a few extra enemies the next time you are in 
battle if you made it too easy.
Help!
If you are unsure how a rule is supposed 
to work, read it again carefully. Most of the 
rules are intended to be interpreted in a 
literal fashion. If applying rules text exactly 
as written does not produce a logical result, 
you are welcome to ask the author for help at 
nordicweaselgames@icloud.com or search 
for Five Parsecs on Discord.
With a big, randomly generated game, you 
may well run into a weird case where things 
do not make sense, even though they would 
normally. If so, adopt the most obvious answer 
for what ought to happen and move on.

=== PAGE 179 ===
177
Appendices
Finding Suitable Miniatures
There are two lines of thought when it comes to 
selecting figures: The first: Choose figures you 
really like the look of and create characters and 
settings to match. The other: Let the rules create 
a character and then source a suitable figure. 
Either way is fine, depending on what you have 
in mind. Many players pick one or two figures 
they really want to use, then let the game 
decide for the rest.
It is difficult to match equipment exactly, 
especially as characters often change their 
equipment between missions. A general 
approach of “pistol, rifle, big gun” works.  
A figure holding a rifle can represent any rifle 
you need it to be. This is especially true for 
characters brought in from other 
Five Parsecs games where the array 
of weaponry can become staggering. 
These game rules are intended to be scale-
neutral;15mm figures are readily available and 
usually have more of a “hard science fiction” 
look to them. They are also cheap and easy to 
paint up. Their larger 28/32mm brethren are 
often more detailed and rewarding for detailed 
painting, but cost and space taken up naturally 
increases. Both options will serve you well.
There are too many excellent small-scale 
manufacturers out there to highlight some 
and omit others, although we should probably 
just mention...
...Five Parsecs Minis
Five Parsecs from Home is proud to be a minis-
agnostic rule set, but if you prefer to use figures 
specifically designed to replicate the species 
and enemies of the 5PFH setting, a wide 
range of official resin and 3D print miniatures, 
vehicles and terrain is now available from 
www.fiveparsecs.com.

=== PAGE 180 ===
Appendices
178
If you absolutely cannot find what you are 
looking for, a time-honored tradition is to 
convert a miniature to fit. This is the process of 
modifying an existing miniature – for example 
replacing a weapon, swapping the head for a 
different one, removing a piece of equipment 
or adding one, and so forth. Plastic figures 
are easiest to cut and glue back together. 
Converting is often seen as an expert-level 
skill, but it is easy to do and can be a lot of fun. 
Just make sure you try it out first on a cheap 
miniature you don’t need!
If you visit the Five Parsecs Facebook fan group 
or the Nordic Weasel Games discord, you 
can find countless photos of people’s figures 
and gaming tables. No doubt something will 
inspire you.
Alternatives to Traditional Miniatures
3D printing is an expensive proposition to get 
started on, but if you are (or have become!) a 
dedicated miniatures gamer, it can represent 
great savings, long term. Many 3D figures can 
also be scaled up or down to work with your 
preferred scale. It is common for players to use 
a mixture of regular and printed miniatures.
A cheap but fun option is paper miniatures. 
These are available from various companies 
with many being intended for use in role 
playing games. They are cheap, and you can 
print out as many as you need, which is always 
helpful for monsters and enemy troops. 
The digital frontier has reached miniatures 
gaming as well, and virtual 
tabletops can be downloaded, 
too. While most of these are aimed 
at board games and role-playing games, 
they can support miniatures just fine. You don’t 
escape having to build things, as you need to 
collect suitable assets, but there are packs out 
there for use, including fan packs specifically 
for Five Parsecs games.
The Face of Battle
Many gamers enjoy collecting, and the 
temptation to build a squad of each enemy 
type is high. You do not have to do this to 
play the game, of course. At the most basic, 
if you have a general “enemy” squad with 
a few grunts, a leader, and a few special 
weapons figures you can tell apart, they 
can do double duty for most of the Tactical 
Enemies in the game.
As you experience the game, you will discover 
what enemies you are fine reusing figures for 
and which ones you want to use new figures 
to represent. If you have already played Five 
Parsecs from Home, you are likely to already 
have figures you can use for many of the 
enemies you encounter. You might enjoy 
adding a few new figures to those collections 
to refresh them, but you can enjoy all the hard 
work you did painting miniatures. 
If you have an ongoing story from an old 
campaign, you might even link them. Maybe 
the renegades you encounter in Planetfall are 
the remnants of a pirate gang you encountered 
in your last campaign? 
For animals, your imagination can run wild. As 
the animals in the rules are described by their 
abilities and not their appearance, anything 
goes. Proper alien lifeforms are not common 
in figure ranges available for sale but, luckily, 
many fantasy figure ranges offer all manner 
of monsters and critters. A visit to a 
toy store will also furnish 
you with any number of 
plastic or rubber insects, 
lizards, and more. 
These can often be 
used as is, but a quick 
paint job can really 

=== PAGE 181 ===
179
Appendices
change the look for a figure and some of them 
will end up looking impressive. Conversion 
work can also be done, such as adding horns, 
cutting down tails, or even attaching (or 
removing) a couple of extra legs. 
Finding Suitable Terrain Pieces
While Planetfall can be played with any gaming 
terrain you have available, the campaign will 
feel more exciting if your terrain reflects your 
brave new world. Fortunately, wargaming 
terrain is readily available commercially. 
Unfortunately, truly “alien” terrain is much less 
common, and you may have to improvise a 
little bit.
A good first place to look is the local pet store. 
The fish tank section will often have an array 
of strange and alien-looking plastic plants and 
rock formations. The rock formations can usually 
be used as they are, whereas the plants will 
usually need mounting on some sort of base 
or attaching to metal wire to create interesting 
alien flora. They can usually be had very cheaply. 
A simple trick to add an “alien” quality is 
to play with colors. Earth’s natural flora is 
predominantly green, so modifying or even 
replacing that can be extremely effective. 
An alien forest of purple shades will look 
incredibly striking on the table, though you 
may find that the terrain ends up being a bit 
too bold to use in other games!
Of course, there is no reason alien planets 
cannot have green grass and rolling hills. 
In this case, you can use smaller features to 
change the feeling, such as placing rocks and 
rock formations or scattered odd-looking plants 
around the table. A plowed field surrounded by 
purple plants will look intriguing and does a 
great job bringing home the “not on Earth after 
all” feel you are looking for.
A trip to a home improvement store can 
often provide interesting items intended for 
decoration, such as colored pebbles or  
plastic crystals.
The table surface itself can also be used to 
good effect, such as using a colored tablecloth. 
This can be spray-painted to create colored 
patches in various shades. Even a subtle effect 
can be noticeable. 
Alien ruins are a part of many science fiction 
books and shows and there are plenty of kits 
ready to purchase from various game lines. 
Ruined walls can also be built easily using 
foam board cut to shape: For some quick ruins, 
cut out a big pile of foam board squares, then 
cut from corner to corner in a zig-zag motion to 
create a pair of walls. Reassemble the various 
walls on a base of cardboard or foam board and 
paint them appropriately. 
Styrofoam from packaging material can also 
be cut into suitable shapes though it needs 
to be treated with a sealing material before 
spray-painting it (PVA glue mixed with water 
will do fine).
A lot of gaming terrain is also commercially 
available in plastic or MDF (laser-cut wood), and 
if you would rather purchase things outright, 
do not feel ashamed in doing so. MDF kits are 
very cheap and lightweight, though they tend 
to lack detail. Plastic is easy to modify if you 
want to cut something away, but the price is 
often higher.
As your terrain collection grows, you can start 
to create unique looks across your alien world, 
which can help tell the story. For example, if 
you use a lot of rock formations in one game, 
the next battle may have a small number of 
rocks placed alongside one edge of the table 
to show that your mission takes place near the 
prior location. 
You can also create groups of terrain that you 
tend to use together to create a more cohesive 
look. In nature, we usually see similar plants 
near each other, and your table will look far 
more realistic when it is built in this fashion.

=== PAGE 182 ===
Appendices
180
Unified Space
Five Parsecs is an open-ended game that can 
easily be adapted to any science fiction setting 
you enjoy or want to create. However, it also has 
its own setting to explore. This chapter provides 
the basic information you need to know to get 
by in Unified Space.
Unity
Unity stretches across the stars wherever 
Humanity has ventured. It serves as promoter 
and defender of Human interests and is 
usually considered the strongest of the galactic 
powers, possessing vast armies and fleets to 
enforce its will.
Formed in response to external threats, most 
Human worlds exist within the protective 
web of Unity, accepting the rule of law (and 
its enforcement) in return for access to trade 
networks and a high standard of living. 
Nominally, all Human majority worlds are 
considered subject to Unity rule, but rebellions, 
splinter factions, and dissidents are common, 
and the armies of Unity are never idle for long. 
Life among the core worlds presents some of 
the most advanced and comfortable living 
conditions Humans have ever experienced, 
but can also be stifling in its mundanity and 
the weight of a highly regimented culture. 
The more adventurous have traditionally 
found employment among the star ships, the 
fleets, or the military, while many seek out the 
ultimate adventure as a colonist. 
The Human Condition
The Humans of the far future take many forms. 
Unity scientific achievement has given rise to 
near-miraculous levels of genetic engineering 
and modification, allowing for a Human 
species that lives longer and is in greater health 
than their ancestors. It has also allowed both 
practical and cosmetic modifications including 
changes of coloration, hair growth patterns, or 
installation of peculiar features such as animal 
claws or tails. 
Such adaptation is expensive and less common 
among frontiers folk, but Humanity can be an 
unpredictable lot to look at.
Some Humans are, in fact not Human at 
all. Along with uplifted animals such as the 
canine-hybrid Ferals, genetically engineered 
sub-species such as Hulkers and Stalkers are 
Human as far as originating from Human 
genetic stock. 
Clothes, fashion, habits, and social mores are 
unpredictable among the Human species. 
While Unity enforces certain cultural factors, 
there are too many Humans on too many 
worlds, influenced by too many factors to make 
any definitive prediction about how a given 
Human will look, talk, and be outfitted. 
Language
In Unity, the dominant language is SVIM 
(Standardized Vocal Interface Method): A 
constructed language developed for clarity 
and ease of learning. SVIM is mandatory on 
all Unity-controlled worlds and due to its 
ubiquitous nature, it is also spoken widely 
beyond the reach of Unity itself. 
Due to this widespread usage, it is also 
common with many non-Humans, and most 
traders and diplomats across the galaxy are 
likely to be at least somewhat proficient. For 
the same reason, most translation systems can 
process SVIM as well.
Unity does not restrict the use of local 
secondary languages, and it is not uncommon 
for entire core-world enclaves to speak their 
own language. In order to be permitted for 
usage, the language must be interpretable by 
standard translation devices. 

=== PAGE 183 ===
181
Appendices
Among the Fringes, SVIM is still common 
though linguistic drift can cause some dialects 
to be difficult to understand. Many colonies 
adopt loan-words from other languages, 
especially if the colony has significant dealings 
with alien species. 
The Administration takes considerable effort 
to update SVIM to account for technological 
developments, popular alien loan words, 
and to contain linguistic drift. On remote 
colony worlds, it is not unusual to find 
linguists hunting for new words in the same 
manner a treasure hunter might search for 
forgotten treasures. 
Precursors and the K’Erin can both speak 
SVIM with ease and can adapt to most Human 
languages relatively easily. 
Swift learn SVIM quickly; however, the high 
pitch and rapid-fire delivery makes them 
difficult to understand. Their peculiar logic 
means that even when transcribed, they rarely 
make much sense. 
As Ferals are Human uplifts, they speak SVIM, 
though tend to have a distinctive accent due to 
their jaws and vocal cords, making them hard 
to understand unless the listener is familiar. 
Some separatist groups try to develop Feral 
languages more suitable to their physique. 
Despite popular misconceptions, Ferals do not 
use body language any more than Humans do 
and find the suggestion offensive.
The Major Galactic Powers
The galaxy is brimming with sapient life all 
pursuing their own agendas and ambitions 
among the stars. The three major political 
powers that share the stage with Unity are the 
Precursors, an ancient and refined species 
with a knack for psionic talents, the K’Erin, 
a proud and warlike species that thrives on 
conflict, and the Soulless, a purely digital 
species that operate in the physical world 
through robotic shells connected to their 
hivemind network.

=== PAGE 184 ===
Appendices
182
A number of small species contest their 
particular regions of space, ranging from major 
habitation centers across dozens of star systems 
to a single system and perhaps a few colonies. 
Interactions between powers can range from 
warfare to trade to diplomacy to coexistence 
to ignoring each other, often at the same time. 
With the scale of galactic civilizations, societies 
are simply too vast to have a consistent 
relationship. The K’Erin and Unity may well be 
engaged in a brutal war of conquest in one 
system, while diplomats praise a newly signed 
military alliance to help suppress a Converted 
incursion in another system.
Keeping a running account of the myriad 
diplomatic and political conditions is far 
beyond the capabilities of any one diplomat 
and each government employs massive 
systems to both catalogue and formulate 
policies and to anticipate future events. 
While few species are deathly rivals, with 
pragmatism and trade tending to win out long-
term, there are exceptions. The Converted, a 
species of cybernetic organisms, harvesting 
captives and corpses alike to turn into new 
shock troops, are a menace that threatens all of 
galactic civilization if it should be permitted to 
spread unchecked. 
Otherwise, species such as the Swarm appear 
to lack large-scale organization but react to 
the presence of other lifeforms with such 
unrelenting aggression that nearly all species 
employ a “destroy on sight” policy when 
encountering them.
The Slyn are a relatively minor species, their 
domains only encompassing a handful of 
worlds far from other civilizations. What has 
drawn them into the Kensington Reach is 
unknown, as is the reason for their high levels 
of aggression towards other species. 
The Rogue and the Renegade
The edges of civilized space have ample room 
for those who would benefit from being 
outside the watchful eye of the law. Pirates raid 
shipping to resell the cargo (and occasionally 
the ship itself), petty warlords demand 
extortionate tolls, and raiders strike at colonies 
for precious resources. 
Each of the major powers spends some effort 
in suppressing such lawless groups, though 
the amount of effort expended is often more 
expensive than the occasional damage 
caused as massive fleets prowl presumed-
empty systems for signs of starships or 
clandestine hideouts. 
Of course, one government’s bandit is 
another’s privateer, and it is not unusual for 
such lawless groups to be in the employ of 
one benefactor or another, whether to cause 
instability or simply to have them on hand 
as a useful resource if a war breaks out. Such 
agreements are illegal under intergalactic 
law, but getting justice in court can take years. 
Most fleet captains prefer vaporizing the 
attacking ship and letting the diplomats deal 
with the niceties.
Far less benign are the threats to life itself such 
as infestations of hostile alien Lifeforms like 
the dreaded Vent Crawler or invasion forces of 
species inimical to life such as the Horde or the 
Converted. Unity fleets have standing orders 
to relay the sighting to the authorities by any 
means available and then engage to destroy 
the intruder if possible. It is not unknown for 
ships boarded by Converted assault troops to 
detonate their power sources to prevent the 
crew being taken alive. 
Unity Armed Forces
The “grunts” – as they are affectionately 
known in recruiting commercials – are the foot 
troops representative of the massive Unity 
military forces. Spanning land troops, fleet 
units, and far more esoteric roles, the Unity 

=== PAGE 185 ===
183
Appendices
trooper strikes a dashing sight in their grey 
uniforms with black and red armor. The grunt 
is somewhat well paid, fairly well equipped 
and reasonably well trained, and often proves 
more than a match for the horrors the galaxy 
can throw at their platoon.
Exploration missions tend to include small 
detachments of troopers, many of them 
veterans from prior conflicts, to act as a cadre 
for the training of a colonial militia. Many have 
previously been stationed in the bug hunting 
squads, while others are promising individuals 
straight out of the academy. 
Being a good soldier is not always an indication 
that an individual will fit the colonial role. 
Those detached here must be dependable, 
resourceful, and able to operate independently. 
Due to the smaller scales of missions, the 
highest-ranking officer is often no more than a 
lieutenant. 
In the event a colony comes under sustained 
attack, the nearest fleet will move to interdict 
the system, preventing the enemy from moving 
in and out of the system, and isolating any 
troops already landed.
Once this has been done, landing forces carry 
out a combination of strikes against enemy 
positions and landing more conventional 
forces to try to link up with any surviving 
defenders. The fleet can bring a shocking 
amount of firepower to bear, though priority 
is usually to secure the colony intact and save 
any survivors. 
Unity Field Agents
Unity is a massive behemoth that is incapable 
of quick decision-making. To keep the wheels 
of civilization turning, Humanity relies on 
a vast number of special agents endowed 
with far-reaching decision-making authority. 
While they have a bewildering array of ranks 
and responsibilities, these operatives are 
collectively known as Field Agents and they 
can be found throughout Human space, 
tracking down criminals, watching for signs of 
insurrection, infiltration, or natural hazards. 
Many specialize in particular concerns such as 
corruption or invasion, while others are tasked 
with gathering information for the Bureaucracy 
to consider for future decision making.

=== PAGE 186 ===
Appendices
184
The Promise has seen scores of agents 
dispatched on an array of missions with both 
scientific and security concerns in mind. Most 
explorer teams will have at least one covert 
agent among the specialists and official 
visits are common. The arrival of an agent 
can be tense, especially as many colonial 
administrators are not above a little creative 
bookkeeping as a reward for a relatively boring 
job. The general population tends to feel better 
with an agent around, if for no other reason 
than the fact every agent has the authority to 
request fleet and army detachments if they 
deem a colony is sufficiently at risk.
The effect of their presence is to keep an ever-
watchful eye out for destabilizing influences, 
alien infiltration, and threats to Unity standing 
and influence in the colony. Even if a rebel 
faction manages to take over control of a 
colony, an Agent is usually able to get a distress 
signal out and request a Unity relief operation.
Unity Fleet Forces
A galactic civilization can only remain a galactic 
civilization on the strength of its naval forces. 
The starfaring vessels of Unity comprise the 
Fleet, tirelessly prowling the space near planets 
to patrol for hazards or providing assistance 
when needed. 
Fleet captains are among the most admired 
people within Unity: They are often seen as 
the only individuals who can stand between 
the destruction of an entire world and its 
salvation, and most captains do little to dispel 
that reputation.
Any galactic civilization could deploy a military 
fleet capable of destroying entire solar systems, 
if it was concentrated, but the demands of 
patrols, military actions and escort duties keep 
most ships busy and, as space is vast, only 
areas near settled planets can meaningfully be 
patrolled or interdicted.
What the Fleet does not provide, the private 
sector does in abundance. No matter where you 
want to go, someone is going there whether 
to trade legally or illegally, deliver something, 
pick up something, and occasionally just 
because they want to visit. While most of 
the grand trade is controlled by mega- and 
ultra-corporations, space does remain the last 
region of society to remain somewhat wild and 
anarchic. An enterprising crew with a ship can 
go almost anywhere, particularly if they are 
willing to dodge a few Fleet interdiction points, 
and this sort of freewheeling lifestyle can be 
appealing. Many of these freelancers find their 
ways into Unity service at some point, whether 
in the Fleet, as agents, or being assigned to 
colonization projects as specialists and advisors.
Galactic Warfare
Galactic societies are large but many of the 
population centers are not. Colonies often 
have only a few thousand inhabitants and the 
cost of moving armed forces through space is 
significant. 
Many armed conflicts take place on an 
essentially local scale. It is not at all unusual 
for a “planetary war” to have involved a few 
battalions of infantry slugging it out near the 
one colony of the planet.
In part, this is also due to concern about 
escalation. If a conflict drags on, it may draw 
in increasing amounts of resources, which in 
turn causes the conflict to escalate further. 
Massive fleets and armies cannot be easily 
stood down once they are committed, and a 
war that expands too quickly risks expanding 
uncontrollably. 
In the interests of preserving civilization, most 
military vessels also include diplomats to help 
reach a satisfactory solution or at least one that 
is not catastrophic.

=== PAGE 187 ===
185
Appendices
Game Mastering
While Game Mastering is usually associated 
with roleplaying games, it is an old tradition 
in miniatures gaming and can be used to 
enhance your Planetfall campaign considerably. 
A Game Master (GM) acts as an additional 
input into the campaign, with a typical Game 
Mastered campaign having one player act as 
the GM while one or more players control the 
player forces. You can also GM for yourself. 
GM’ing narrative miniatures games relies 
heavily on creativity and improvisation, but 
this section offers a few points to get you up 
and running. 
Controlling the Enemy
The simplest place to start is to GM a single 
battle by taking control of the enemies. Enemy 
forces run by the GM are not bound by their AI 
and may instead move and attack freely.
Unless you are playing a custom scenario, the 
enemies should still be reasonably bound 
by the limits normal rules apply to them. An 
animal cannot suddenly pick up a laser rifle, 
and a pirate grunt still moves and shoots once 
per action, as normal. 
This is particularly suitable to intelligent 
enemies, allowing them to use a greater 
degree of tactics.
When controlling enemy forces, the GM 
should note that their role is to provide a more 
exciting encounter, not to win a competitive 
game. Injecting an element of roleplaying can 
greatly enhance the fun of the game by having 
enemies act appropriately based on what 
they can see or their personality. For example, 
the GM might decide that the enemy in this 
particular battle is a reconnaissance patrol 
which will try to avoid engaging in combat 
unless attacked by the players’ forces. These 
types of personality-driven tactics can be a lot of 
fun if the player or players try to figure out what 
is happening. 
Adjudicating Player Actions
Playing with a GM also opens up the 
possibility of player actions. A player can 
request to take actions that are not normally 
covered by the rules, such as trying to start a 
fire, building a sniper’s nest, or hacking into a 
computer terminal. 
The GM determines if the action is reasonable 
and assigns a chance of success usually based 
on a die roll. If you are uncertain, a simple 4+ 
or 5+ die roll is usually fine. You will need to 
determine what happens on a failure. “It does 
not work” is fine and keeps the game moving 
on, while more creative GMs may allow things 
to get more problematic. After all, trying to 
build your own demolition charges can be 
rather dangerous.
However, resist the temptation to be overly 
punitive: If the risks are always much worse 
than the rewards, players will stop using  
the option. 
Player actions are intended to do cool and 
interesting things, not be a dull way to get a 
few +1 hit modifiers. They may be suitable 
for some scenarios, but not others. If you are 
playing on your own, you can still use this but 
based on your own sense of what works for the 
game. Some players prefer to stick closely to 
the normal combat rules while others enjoy 
the idea that, for example, a character could 
use their weapon to knock down a tree, thus 
blocking a road. 
Players who desire a more extensive approach 
to this can consult Five Parsecs: Tactics for a 
discussion of the subject with more rules, 
options, and material. 

=== PAGE 188 ===
Appendices
186
Custom Scenarios
A GM can also create special scenarios to 
play through. These may change the normal 
encounter rules and might involve everything 
from sneaking into an enemy base to trying to 
tame a wild animal. 
They may change how figures are deployed, 
what the objectives are, and anything else they 
can think of doing.
A custom scenario can be part of an overarching 
storyline, or it can be a stand-alone custom 
encounter. When playing with a GM, it is 
common to mix the two together alongside 
regular missions from the rulebook, giving you 
a lot of variety in what is happening. Often, the 
GM can use things that occur randomly as a 
springboard for a side story or the beginning 
of an epic quest. This can be small: A K’Erin 
landing party is testing a strange atmospheric 
manipulation device and you must track down 
the three keys to break through their force 
shield and deactivate it. It can also be a huge, 
sweeping tale with countless encounters, 
recurring villains, and revelations. 
Start small. It is almost always better to begin 
with something manageable that you can 
finish than to try something huge that will fizzle 
out. If you are playing with a group, you should 
make sure everyone is on the same page. If 
your players are expecting a huge custom 
storyline and you are expecting to just do a few 
custom enemies here and there, there will be 
dissatisfaction. Take a few minutes before the 
campaign begins to discuss what you are really 
interested in, what sounds fun, and what levels 
of reward and challenge should be included.
A scenario can be based around almost any idea 
you can think of. A good starting point is some 
sort of mystery: Someone has been sabotaging 
the power storage cells, a strange signal is 
detected from a drill site, a researcher has gone 
missing. Science fiction novels and television 
can furnish numerous examples suitable for 
stealing and putting into your games. 
When creating longer story lines, evaluate how 
it will interface with the conventional campaign 
mechanics. Stories that mostly take place in the 
tactical environment of tabletop battles are the 
easiest with which to work. If a story requires 

=== PAGE 189 ===
187
Appendices
characters to be away from the colony for an 
extended period, for example, consider how 
that might affect other elements of the game. 
If the entire squad is stuck in the wilderness 
because their shuttle crashed, what happens if 
an enemy attacks the colony? You can handle 
these questions on the fly (and some events 
can be treated as “nothing happens”, if needed) 
but it is helpful to think of these things in 
advance to avoid unpleasant surprises. 
Things to be Wary of
Creating fun, engaging scenarios is a skill that 
improves with practice, but below are some 
guidelines to help avoid common problems:
 
◆If the scenario involves a time limit, check 
that the objectives can be achieved in the 
time frame available, especially if they 
involve significant movement.
 
◆Lean on the cautious side when adding 
special rules to a scenario. It is easy to go 
overboard and end up with a scenario that 
is confusing or difficult to play. To avoid 
complexity, try to focus special rules on 
specific things in the scenario such as 
terrain features or characters rather than 
modifications to the core combat rules. 
 
◆Avoid adding random dice rolls for 
everything. You are already rolling a lot 
of dice playing the scenario. If there are 
too many random factors, the player can 
easily feel like they have no control over the 
experience, and it is at the whim of the dice.
 
◆Multi-stage actions or objectives that require 
a series of successful dice rolls become 
statistically likely to fail. For example, an 
action that requires three consecutive rolls of 
4+ has only a 12.5% chance of success.
 
◆New items, skills, or weapons are very strong 
motivations, but unless they are limited to 
a set number of uses, remember that they 
will probably stay in the campaign for a long 
time. A hyper-laser with 5 attack dice, +1 to 
hit, and which ignores armor is fun but will 
make alien critters seem less dangerous. 
 
◆Think carefully about scenarios that can 
inflict permanent damage to the characters, 
beyond the normal injury rules.

=== PAGE 190 ===
Appendices
188
Designer Notes
You have arrived at the end. Did you read 
everything in this book?
It’s okay if you didn’t – you’ll get there when 
needed. Obviously a “Parsecs” game needs 
certain features and, in particular, it must 
be mechanically compatible, but the game 
should be a unique experience as well. While 
the familiar beat of “carry out actions, fight 
missions, track things” remains the same, the 
details are all different and, in some cases, 
quite so. For one, you have less direct control of 
your characters this time. You are administering 
a colony, and your squad members all have 
jobs to do outside the battlefield.
In a lot of ways, you might think of the colony 
as the real player character: It is, after all, almost 
a living thing that can be injured or even die. 
You may find that at first glance, you may feel 
like you are not in command of your destiny. 
As you play more, you will begin to notice the 
subtle ways in which you can alleviate random 
occurrences. The thrust of the campaign is that, 
on a long enough timeline, the colony will fail 
due to morale concerns unless you actively 
work to get to where you need to go.
Some players will want to focus very narrowly 
on achieving a particular victory condition, 
while others will prefer playing and exploring 
the game before eventually stumbling into 
the End Game. Both are completely fine and 
depend more on your interests as a player. 
The various enemy types are designed to 
provide different experiences, each of which 
may feel familiar to players of the existing 
games. Tactical enemies are engaged in small 
arms combat, much as may be familiar to you 
from Five Parsecs from Home, while animals 
roam around the countryside much like foes in 
Bug Hunt (although, mercifully, far fewer tend 
to show up here). 
The new aliens, the Slyn, are an occasional 
threat that will show up to pick a fight and 
often disappear just as mysteriously. They are 
intended to be enigmatic and difficult to pin 
down, both in motivation and literally within 
the game. They are the only foe you cannot 
choose to take on intentionally.
On a tactical level, the rules remain fairly 
simple. It is my belief that a solo game, and 
especially one with a lot happening, needs to 
be a little bit simpler than what a player versus 
player system might demand. You may find 
that a typical firefight is over rapidly and will 
quickly punish the careless (or unlucky). 
Don’t let the simplicity fool you, however. 
Outflanking the enemy to fire on them without 
the benefit of cover is often critical to winning, 
especially since the enemy may match you in 
sheer volume of fire. Make sure your characters 
are working together in pairs or in threes so 
they can support each other, especially when an 
enemy specialist gets the drop on you.
A constant question when developing a solo 
game is how much emphasis should be given 
to sealing off loopholes that a crafty player can 
exploit to get ahead. In my opinion, effort spent 
here is often better spent somewhere else, and 
I suspect that, in the end, most players would 
prefer having more creatures and events in 
the rulebook than a more iron-clad “security” 
system in the campaign.
You will no doubt find some options more 
effective than others, actions that have better 
payoffs, or even an optimal path through the 
game’s Milestones. Should you take them? 
That’s up to you. Some players enjoy figuring 
out how to “beat the system”. Others are into 
it for a story, or they just want to roll dice and 
see what happens. If you manage to solve the 
game in the most efficient way, congratulate 
yourself and have a cup of tea. I bet that took a 
lot of thinking and experimenting, and it can 
be fun to find the best routes. 

=== PAGE 191 ===
189
Appendices
It is up to you whether to then restrict yourself 
to only those options or if you want to widen 
your scope further. Maybe go back and try 
again with some of the sub-optimal options or 
try for a different play style. 
Over the years, many players come to these 
games come from backgrounds other than 
miniatures gaming, such as board games or 
roleplaying games and, to be sure, the game 
has elements of all of them. I find that many 
questions about the reason things are done in 
certain ways can be answered by remembering 
that it is, at its heart, a miniatures game. 
The purpose of the campaign is to build a story, 
have fun, and to do exciting things, but it is also 
to move miniatures around a tactical setting, 
shooting at an array of different enemies. 
The campaign narrative is painted with a broad 
brush, perhaps even a broader one than usual. 
If you pay attention to the background, the 
stories and the events in the game, a picture 
should begin to emerge but, as is always the 
case with Five Parsecs titles, the details remain 
up to you. From experience, about a third of the 
players seem to be really into the Unified Space 
setting, a third prefer inventing their own or 
adapting a setting from other media, and the 
last third use some parts and mix in their own. 
It is a bit of an art form to try to balance these 
approaches, and you must be the judge of 
whether it has succeeded.
Planetfall is a bold and ambitious game. I 
had to draw on ideas and experiences that I 
had never seen before in a miniatures game, 
though I would not be so bold as to assume 
nobody has had the same ones at all. After all, 
it is a sprawling hobby. I hope you enjoy the 
adventure. Who knows what might lurk in the 
next map sector? 
Love and peace, Ivan Sørensen.
Inspirations
The Five Parsecs setting (known as Unified 
Space) was inspired by books, television, 
tabletop, and video game experiences both old 
and new that stood out to me. Those mentioned 
below includes a number of things that were 
not foundational experiences for me, but which 
I think you will either enjoy or find inspiring. 
 
◆Films: Soldier; Blade Runner; 
the Alien franchise; Edge of Tomorrow; and 
Valerian and the City of a Thousand Planets.
 
◆Television shows: The Trigun and Ghost 
in the Shell animated series; Farscape; 
Battlestar Galactica; The Expanse; Blakes 7; 
Space 1999; Star Trek; Doctor Who.
 
◆Video Games: The Mass Effect series; 
Star Control 1/2; Doom 1/2; Quake 2/4; 
Unreal / Unreal Tournament; Final 
Liberation; UFO / XCOM; Battle Isle series, 
especially Incubation; Hired Guns;  
Alien Breed; Alpha Centauri.
 
◆Miniatures Games: Warzone 1st and 2nd 
edition; Beamstrike; Slammer; Stargrunt 2;  
Laserburn / Imperial Commander; 
Inquisitor; 5150; Warhammer 40,000 
(Rogue Trader and 2nd edition).
 
◆Roleplaying Games: Rymd; Mutant 
Chronicles; Traveller.
 
◆Comic Books: Valerian; Nemesis;  
Strontium Dogs.
 
◆Novels: Armor; Forever War / Forever Peace; 
Jon Bing’s “Alexandria” series ...and various 
science fiction books I got from the library 
as a child and barely understood, but which 
left much of an impression. 
 
◆Music: Dvne – particularly Asheran; Slough 
Feg – Traveller; Miseration – The Mirroring 
Shadow; Gary Numan – Savage; Tactical Sekt 
– Geneticide; VNV nation – Empires; plus 
soundtracks to Command&Conquer, Final 
Liberation, Unreal Tournament, and more.

Quick Reference
Weapon Traits
AP Ammo: If stationary, the 
shooter may add +1 to damage. 
Area: Select a target point in range 
and sight. Each enemy within 
2” is hit on D6 roll of 4+. Cover 
provides no protection. (Friendly 
characters unaffected by bio-gun).
Armor-piercing: Ignore Armor 
Saving Throws.
Burning: Negates Saving Throws.
Civilian: Open to all classes  
except grunts.
Clumsy: If the opponent has higher 
base Speed, -1 brawling penalty.
Critical: A natural 6 to hit inflicts  
2 hits on the target figure.

Cumbersome: Range is reduced 
to 12” when moving and firing.
Flexible: Can carry a handgun at 
the same time.
Focused: Both shots must be fired 
at the same target.
Grunt: Restricted to Grunt-class 
characters.
Hail of Fire: May fire 4 shots if all 
of them target the same figure.
Heavy: -1 to hit penalty if moving 
and firing.
Hyperfire: If a hit does not 
destroy the target, the trooper 
may immediately fire again at the 
same target. Continue until a shot 
misses or the target is destroyed.
Knockback: A target that survives 
a hit is Knocked Sprawling.
Melee: +2 bonus when used as a 
brawling weapon.
Mind-link: Roll to hit using Savvy 
instead of Combat Skill. Scientists 
may roll twice and pick the best.
Phased fire: The target receives 
no benefits from cover or scatter 
terrain. The target must be visible.
Pistol: +1 weapon bonus  
when brawling.
Scout: Restricted to Scout-class 
characters.
Shockwave: A surviving target is 
knocked 1D6” backwards.
Stabilized: If the shooter does 
not move, +1 to hit.
Stream: Draw a straight line 
6” long and 1” wide from the 
firer. Any figure (including 
allies) touched by the line is hit 
automatically, no roll required.
Tier 1: To access this, your colony 
must have built the Advanced 
Manufacturing Plant.
Tier 2: To access this, your colony 
must have built the High-Tech 
Manufacturing Plant.
Trooper: Restricted to Trooper-
class characters.
